كتاب الرهن / تعريفه وشروطه وما يصح به والتصرف فيه
وشاة قيمتها عشرة رُهِنَتْ بها، فماتت فدبغ جلدها، فعدل درهماً، فهو رهن به والخمر ليس محلا للبيع ابتداءً، لكن محل له بقاء، فكذا الرهن.
وشاة قيمتها عشرة رُهِنَتْ بها، فماتت فدبغ جلدها، فعدل درهماً، فهو رهن به [قوله: بقاء؛ حتى أنّ من اشترى عصيراً فتخمّر قبل القبض يبقى العقد إلا أن يتخير في البيع لتغير وصف المبيع بمنزلة ما إذا تعيب، وإنّما قلنا ببقاء العقد؛ لأن الخمر مال إلا أنه ليس بمتقوم. فبالنظر إلى جهة المالية يقتضي المحليّة، وبالنظر إلى أنه ليس بمتقوم يقتضي انعدام المحلية، فعملنا بالشبهين، فقلنا بأنه ليس بمحل ابتداء، وأنه محل بقاء، ولم نقل بالعكس؛ لأنّ ما يكون محلاً للابتداء فهو محل للبقاء، فإنّ البقاء أسهل من الابتداء، فلا يمكن اعتبار الشبهين. هذا ملخص ما في «الكفاية». (٢) قوله: فهو رهن به؛ أي بدرهم؛ لأن الرهن يتقرر بالهلاك، فإذا حيى بعض المحل يعود حكمه بقدره، هذا إذا كانت قيمة الجلد يوم الرهن درهماً. وأما إذا كانت قيمة الجلد يوم الرهن درهمين كان الجلد رهناً بدرهمين، وإنما يعرف هذا فيما إذا نظر إلى قيمة الجلد وإلى قيمة اللحم يوم الارتهان، فإن كانت قيمةُ اللحم تسعة، وقيمة الجلد درهماً كان الجلد رهناً بدرهم، وإنما يعرفُ إذا نظر إلى قيمة الشاة حيَّة، وإلى قيمتها مسلوخة، فإن كانت قيمتها حيّة عشرة وقيمتها مسلوخة تسعة علم أن قيمة الجلد درهم يوم الرهن؛ لأنه بإزاء كلّ درهم من الشاة درهم من الدين، فيسقط من الدين تسعة، ويبقي الجلد رهناً بدرهم. وإن كانت قيمتها حية عشرة، وقيمتها مسلوخة ثمانية عُلِمَ أن قيمة الجلدِ درهمان، فيكون الجلد رهناً بدرهمين، وإنّما وجب النظر إلى قيمة الجلد واللحم يوم الارتهان لا يوم الدباغة؛ لأنّ الأصل أنَّ قيمة الرهن إنما تعتبر يوم الارتهان، هذا الذي ذكرنا فيما إذا كانت قيمة الشاة مثل الدين، وأما إذا كانت قيمة الشاةِ أكثر من الدين، فتفصيله مذكور في الكفاية شرح الهداية فانظر هناك.

ونماء الرهن كولدِهِ ولبنه وصوفه وثمره لراهنه، وهو رهن مع أصله، ويهلك بلا شيء، فإن هلك أصله فك بقسطه يقسمُ الدِّينُ على قيمته يومَ فكه، وقيمة أصله يوم قبضه، وتسقط حصة أصله، وفُك بقسطه، والزيادة في الرهنِ تصح، وفي الدين لا ونماء الرهن كولدِهِ ولبنه وصوفه وثمره الراهنه، وهو رهن مع أصله، ويهلك بلا شيء، فإنَّه لم يدخل تحت العقد مقصوداً، فإن هلك أصله وبقي هو فك بقسطه يقسمُ الدِّينُ على قيمته يومَ فكه، وقيمة أصله يوم قبضه، وتسقط حصة أصله، وفك بقسطه كما إذا كان الدين عشرة، وقيمة الأصل يوم القبض عشرة، وقيمةُ النَّماء يومَ الفك خمسة، فثلثا العشرة حصة الأصل فيسقط، وثلث العشرة حصة النماء، فيفك به.
والزيادة في الرهن تصح، وفي الدِّينِ لا هذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد وعند أبي يوسف له: يجوز الزيادة في الدين أيضاً، فإنَّ الدِّينَ بمَنْزِلَةِ الثَّمن، والزِّيادة [قوله: لراهنه؛ لأنه متولّد من ملكه، وهو رهن مع أصله؛ لأنه تبع له، والرهن حقٌّ لازم؛ أي متأكد بحيث لا اختيار فيه، فيسري إليه، ألا ترى أن الراهن لا يملك البطالة بخلاف ولد الجارية الجانية، حيث لا يسري حكم الجناية إلى الولد، ولا يتبع أمه فيه؛ لأنّ الحق فيها غير متأكد حتى ينفرد المالك بإبطاله بالفداء. قوله: فك بقسطه؛ لأنّ الرهن يصير مضموناً بالقبض، والزيادة تصير مقصودة بالفكاك إذا بقي إلى وقته، والتبعُ يقابله شيء إذا صار مقصوداً كولد المبيع فيما أصاب الأصل يسقط من الدين؛ لأنه يقابله الأصلُ مقصوداً، وما أصاب النماء افتكه الراهن لما ذكرنا، هكذا في «الهداية». قوله: وفي الدين لا؛ أي لا يجوز الزيادة في الدين، كما إذا رهن عبداً بألف، ثم حدث للمرتهن دين آخر بالشراء والاستقراض، فيجعل الرهن بالدين القديم رهناً بدون الدين الحادث. كذا في الجلبي]
Book of Pledge / Its definition, conditions, what is valid for it, and disposal of it. A sheep worth ten was pledged for ten, it died, its leather was tanned, then it was worth a dirham, so it is a pledge for it. And wine is not a place for sale initially, but it is a place for it in remaining, so likewise the pledge. A sheep worth ten was pledged for ten, it died, its leather was tanned, then it was worth a dirham, so it is a pledge for it. [His statement: remaining; such that if someone bought grape juice and it became wine before possession, the contract remains except that he has an option in the sale due to change in description of the sold item similar to when it becomes defective. And we only said the contract remains because wine is property but not valuable property (muttaqawwim). So from the perspective of property-ness it necessitates being a place, and from the perspective that it is not valuable it necessitates absence of being a place, so we acted on both aspects, and we said that it is not a place initially, and it is a place in remaining, and we did not say the opposite because what is a place initially is a place in remaining, for remaining is easier than initiating, so it is not possible to consider both aspects. This is a summary from al-Kifayah. (2) His statement: so it is a pledge for it; i.e. for a dirham, because the pledge is established by destruction, so if some of the place remains, its ruling returns according to its amount. This is if the value of the leather on the day of pledge was a dirham. And if the value of the leather on the day of pledge was two dirhams, then the leather is a pledge for two dirhams. And this is known when one looks at the value of the leather and the value of the meat on the day of taking pledge. If the value of the meat was nine and the value of the leather one dirham, then the leather is a pledge for one dirham. And it is known when one looks at the value of the sheep alive and its value skinned. If its value alive was ten and its value skinned nine, it is known that the value of the leather on the day of pledge is one dirham, because for every dirham of the sheep there is a dirham of the debt, so nine of the debt are dropped, and the leather remains a pledge for one dirham. And if its value alive was ten and its value skinned eight, it is known that the value of the leather is two dirhams, then the leather is a pledge for two dirhams. And it is necessary to look at the value of the leather and meat on the day of taking pledge, not the day of tanning, because the principle is that the value of the pledge is only considered on the day of taking pledge. This is what we mentioned when the value of the sheep equals the debt. And if the value of the sheep is more than the debt, its detail is mentioned in al-Kifayah Sharh al-Hidayah, so look there. And the increase of the pledge, like its offspring, milk, wool, and fruit, belongs to the pledgor, and it is a pledge along with its origin, and it perishes without anything. If its origin perishes, it is redeemed proportionally: the debt is divided over its value on the day of its redemption and the value of its origin on the day of its possession, then the share of the origin is dropped, and it is redeemed proportionally. And increase in the pledge is valid, and in the debt it is not. And increase of the pledge like its offspring, milk, wool, and fruit belongs to the pledgor, and it is a pledge with its origin, and it perishes without anything, because it did not enter under the contract intentionally. If its origin perishes and it remains, it is redeemed proportionally: the debt is divided over its value on the day of its redemption and the value of its origin on the day of its possession, then the share of the origin is dropped, and it is redeemed proportionally, as when the debt is ten, the value of the origin on the day of possession is ten, and the value of the increase on the day of redemption is five, then two thirds of ten is the share of the origin so it is dropped, and one third of ten is the share of the increase, so it is redeemed by it. And increase in the pledge is valid, and in the debt it is not. This according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. According to Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, increase in the debt is also permissible, for the debt is like the price, and the increase [His statement: belongs to the pledgor; because it is generated from his ownership, and it is a pledge with its origin because it follows it, and the pledge is a binding right, i.e., confirmed such that there is no choice in it, so it extends to it. Don't you see that the pledgor does not have the power to annul, unlike the offspring of a slave girl who committed an offense, where the ruling of the offense does not extend to the offspring, and it does not follow its mother in it, because the right over her is not confirmed such that the owner can annul it by ransom. His statement: it is redeemed proportionally; because the pledge becomes guaranteed by possession, and the increase becomes intended by redemption when it remains to its time, and what follows is met by something when it becomes intended, like the offspring of the sold item when something hits the origin it is dropped from the debt because the origin meets it intentionally, and what hits the increase the pledgor redeems it for what we mentioned. Thus in al-Hidayah. His statement: and in the debt it is not; i.e., increase in the debt is not permissible, like when he pledged a slave for a thousand, then another debt occurs to the mortgagee by purchase or loan, then the pledge for the old debt is made a pledge without the new debt. Thus from al-Jalabi.]
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah addresses two main issues regarding pledges. First, it discusses a scenario where a sheep worth ten dirhams is pledged for a debt of ten dirhams. The sheep dies and its leather is tanned, now worth one dirham. The ruling is that the tanned leather becomes a pledge for that one dirham, proportionally reducing the debt by the value of the meat. The commentary explains that this is analogous to wine: wine is not a valid subject of sale initially because it is not valuable property (muttaqawwim), but it can be retained as property in certain contexts. Similarly, the leather becomes a pledge because the pledge is established upon the destruction of the original item. The value of the leather and meat are assessed on the day of the pledge, not the day of tanning. If the value of the sheep equals the debt, the calculation is straightforward: for every dirham of the sheep's value, one dirham of debt is cancelled. If the sheep's value exceeds the debt, a more detailed rule applies, referenced to al-Kifayah. Second, the passage covers the increase (nama') of the pledged item—such as offspring, milk, wool, and fruit. This increase belongs to the pledgor, but it is also part of the pledge, attached to the original item. If the original item perishes, the increase is redeemed proportionally based on its value at the time of redemption. The debt is divided between the original and the increase. The central dispute here is between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (who say that increase in the debt is not valid) and Abu Yusuf (who allows it by analogy to a sale price). The relied-upon position according to the commentary is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, as stated by the commentator.

Key Terms
رهن (rahn): A pledge: an item given as security for a debt.
فك (fakk): Redemption: the release of the pledge upon repayment of the debt or part thereof.
قسط (qist): Proportionate share; the debt is divided proportionally between the original pledge and its increase.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a sheep worth 10 dirhams is pledged for a debt of 10 dirhams, and it dies, the leather after tanning is worth 1 dirham. What happens to the debt and the leather?
A) The entire debt is cancelled and the leather is free.
B) 9 dirhams of the debt are cancelled, and the leather remains pledged for 1 dirham.
C) The debt remains 10 dirhams and the leather becomes the property of the mortgagee.
D) The leather is returned to the pledgor and the debt is reduced by 1 dirham.

Key Takeaway
When a pledged item is partially destroyed, the debt is reduced proportionally, and the remaining part becomes the pledge for the remaining debt.

Ruling on Increase of Pledge and Dispute on Increasing Debt
Explains rulings on increase of pledge and the dispute regarding increasing debt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on the increase of a pledge and evaluate the dispute between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad vs Abu Yusuf regarding increasing the debt.

Core Idea
Increase of a pledge belongs to the pledgor but is part of the pledge; adding a new debt to an existing pledge is invalid according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.

This lesson covers two related issues: first, the status of increase (nama') of a pledged item—such as offspring, milk, wool—which belongs to the pledgor but is attached to the pledge. Second, the intra-Hanafi dispute over whether a mortgagee can add a new debt to an existing pledge. Understanding these nuances is essential for correct application of pledge law.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle of increase
Increase belongs to the pledgor but is part of the pledge; it perishes without compensation if the origin perishes.
The increase follows the origin because it is generated from the pledgor's property.
2
Calculate proportionate redemption if origin perishes
Debt is divided proportionally between origin and increase based on values at different times.
Example given: debt 10, origin value at possession 10, increase value at redemption 5 → 2/3 debt dropped, 1/3 redeemed.
3
Differentiate increase in pledge vs increase in debt
Increasing the pledged item (e.g., adding more items) is valid; increasing the debt (adding a new debt to the same pledge) is not.
This is the majority Hanafi position.
4
Analyze Abu Yusuf's objection
He analogizes debt to price; increase in price is valid in a sale, so increase in debt should be valid.
The majority response: the analogy is imperfect because a pledge is a security right, not a price.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (no increase in debt) is the relied-upon position for fatwa.
The commentary states this clearly.

(٢) قوله: فهو رهن به؛ أي بدرهم؛ لأن الرهن يتقرر بالهلاك، فإذا حيى بعض المحل يعود حكمه بقدره، هذا إذا كانت قيمة الجلد يوم الرهن درهماً. وأما إذا كانت قيمة الجلد يوم الرهن درهمين كان الجلد رهناً بدرهمين، وإنما يعرف هذا فيما إذا نظر إلى قيمة الجلد وإلى قيمة اللحم يوم الارتهان، فإن كانت قيمةُ اللحم تسعة، وقيمة الجلد درهماً كان الجلد رهناً بدرهم، وإنما يعرفُ إذا نظر إلى قيمة الشاة حيَّة، وإلى قيمتها مسلوخة، فإن كانت قيمتها حيّة عشرة وقيمتها مسلوخة تسعة علم أن قيمة الجلد درهم يوم الرهن؛ لأنه بإزاء كلّ درهم من الشاة درهم من الدين، فيسقط من الدين تسعة، ويبقي الجلد رهناً بدرهم. وإن كانت قيمتها حية عشرة، وقيمتها مسلوخة ثمانية عُلِمَ أن قيمة الجلدِ درهمان، فيكون الجلد رهناً بدرهمين، وإنّما وجب النظر إلى قيمة الجلد واللحم يوم الارتهان لا يوم الدباغة؛ لأنّ الأصل أنَّ قيمة الرهن إنما تعتبر يوم الارتهان، هذا الذي ذكرنا فيما إذا كانت قيمة الشاة مثل الدين، وأما إذا كانت قيمة الشاةِ أكثر من الدين، فتفصيله مذكور في الكفاية شرح الهداية فانظر هناك.

ونماء الرهن كولدِهِ ولبنه وصوفه وثمره لراهنه، وهو رهن مع أصله، ويهلك بلا شيء، فإن هلك أصله فك بقسطه يقسمُ الدِّينُ على قيمته يومَ فكه، وقيمة أصله يوم قبضه، وتسقط حصة أصله، وفُك بقسطه، والزيادة في الرهنِ تصح، وفي الدين لا ونماء الرهن كولدِهِ ولبنه وصوفه وثمره الراهنه، وهو رهن مع أصله، ويهلك بلا شيء، فإنَّه لم يدخل تحت العقد مقصوداً، فإن هلك أصله وبقي هو فك بقسطه يقسمُ الدِّينُ على قيمته يومَ فكه، وقيمة أصله يوم قبضه، وتسقط حصة أصله، وفك بقسطه كما إذا كان الدين عشرة، وقيمة الأصل يوم القبض عشرة، وقيمةُ النَّماء يومَ الفك خمسة، فثلثا العشرة حصة الأصل فيسقط، وثلث العشرة حصة النماء، فيفك به.
والزيادة في الرهن تصح، وفي الدِّينِ لا هذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد وعند أبي يوسف له: يجوز الزيادة في الدين أيضاً، فإنَّ الدِّينَ بمَنْزِلَةِ الثَّمن، والزِّيادة [قوله: لراهنه؛ لأنه متولّد من ملكه، وهو رهن مع أصله؛ لأنه تبع له، والرهن حقٌّ لازم؛ أي متأكد بحيث لا اختيار فيه، فيسري إليه، ألا ترى أن الراهن لا يملك البطالة بخلاف ولد الجارية الجانية، حيث لا يسري حكم الجناية إلى الولد، ولا يتبع أمه فيه؛ لأنّ الحق فيها غير متأكد حتى ينفرد المالك بإبطاله بالفداء. قوله: فك بقسطه؛ لأنّ الرهن يصير مضموناً بالقبض، والزيادة تصير مقصودة بالفكاك إذا بقي إلى وقته، والتبعُ يقابله شيء إذا صار مقصوداً كولد المبيع فيما أصاب الأصل يسقط من الدين؛ لأنه يقابله الأصلُ مقصوداً، وما أصاب النماء افتكه الراهن لما ذكرنا، هكذا في «الهداية». قوله: وفي الدين لا؛ أي لا يجوز الزيادة في الدين، كما إذا رهن عبداً بألف، ثم حدث للمرتهن دين آخر بالشراء والاستقراض، فيجعل الرهن بالدين القديم رهناً بدون الدين الحادث. كذا في الجلبي]
Book of Pledge / Its definition, conditions, what is valid for it, and disposal of it. A sheep worth ten was pledged for ten, it died, its leather was tanned, then it was worth a dirham, so it is a pledge for it. And wine is not a place for sale initially, but it is a place for it in remaining, so likewise the pledge. A sheep worth ten was pledged for ten, it died, its leather was tanned, then it was worth a dirham, so it is a pledge for it. [His statement: remaining; such that if someone bought grape juice and it became wine before possession, the contract remains except that he has an option in the sale due to change in description of the sold item similar to when it becomes defective. And we only said the contract remains because wine is property but not valuable property (muttaqawwim). So from the perspective of property-ness it necessitates being a place, and from the perspective that it is not valuable it necessitates absence of being a place, so we acted on both aspects, and we said that it is not a place initially, and it is a place in remaining, and we did not say the opposite because what is a place initially is a place in remaining, for remaining is easier than initiating, so it is not possible to consider both aspects. This is a summary from al-Kifayah. (2) His statement: so it is a pledge for it; i.e. for a dirham, because the pledge is established by destruction, so if some of the place remains, its ruling returns according to its amount. This is if the value of the leather on the day of pledge was a dirham. And if the value of the leather on the day of pledge was two dirhams, then the leather is a pledge for two dirhams. And this is known when one looks at the value of the leather and the value of the meat on the day of taking pledge. If the value of the meat was nine and the value of the leather one dirham, then the leather is a pledge for one dirham. And it is known when one looks at the value of the sheep alive and its value skinned. If its value alive was ten and its value skinned nine, it is known that the value of the leather on the day of pledge is one dirham, because for every dirham of the sheep there is a dirham of the debt, so nine of the debt are dropped, and the leather remains a pledge for one dirham. And if its value alive was ten and its value skinned eight, it is known that the value of the leather is two dirhams, then the leather is a pledge for two dirhams. And it is necessary to look at the value of the leather and meat on the day of taking pledge, not the day of tanning, because the principle is that the value of the pledge is only considered on the day of taking pledge. This is what we mentioned when the value of the sheep equals the debt. And if the value of the sheep is more than the debt, its detail is mentioned in al-Kifayah Sharh al-Hidayah, so look there. And the increase of the pledge, like its offspring, milk, wool, and fruit, belongs to the pledgor, and it is a pledge along with its origin, and it perishes without anything. If its origin perishes, it is redeemed proportionally: the debt is divided over its value on the day of its redemption and the value of its origin on the day of its possession, then the share of the origin is dropped, and it is redeemed proportionally. And increase in the pledge is valid, and in the debt it is not. And increase of the pledge like its offspring, milk, wool, and fruit belongs to the pledgor, and it is a pledge with its origin, and it perishes without anything, because it did not enter under the contract intentionally. If its origin perishes and it remains, it is redeemed proportionally: the debt is divided over its value on the day of its redemption and the value of its origin on the day of its possession, then the share of the origin is dropped, and it is redeemed proportionally, as when the debt is ten, the value of the origin on the day of possession is ten, and the value of the increase on the day of redemption is five, then two thirds of ten is the share of the origin so it is dropped, and one third of ten is the share of the increase, so it is redeemed by it. And increase in the pledge is valid, and in the debt it is not. This according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. According to Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, increase in the debt is also permissible, for the debt is like the price, and the increase [His statement: belongs to the pledgor; because it is generated from his ownership, and it is a pledge with its origin because it follows it, and the pledge is a binding right, i.e., confirmed such that there is no choice in it, so it extends to it. Don't you see that the pledgor does not have the power to annul, unlike the offspring of a slave girl who committed an offense, where the ruling of the offense does not extend to the offspring, and it does not follow its mother in it, because the right over her is not confirmed such that the owner can annul it by ransom. His statement: it is redeemed proportionally; because the pledge becomes guaranteed by possession, and the increase becomes intended by redemption when it remains to its time, and what follows is met by something when it becomes intended, like the offspring of the sold item when something hits the origin it is dropped from the debt because the origin meets it intentionally, and what hits the increase the pledgor redeems it for what we mentioned. Thus in al-Hidayah. His statement: and in the debt it is not; i.e., increase in the debt is not permissible, like when he pledged a slave for a thousand, then another debt occurs to the mortgagee by purchase or loan, then the pledge for the old debt is made a pledge without the new debt. Thus from al-Jalabi.]
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah addresses two main issues regarding pledges. First, it discusses a scenario where a sheep worth ten dirhams is pledged for a debt of ten dirhams. The sheep dies and its leather is tanned, now worth one dirham. The ruling is that the tanned leather becomes a pledge for that one dirham, proportionally reducing the debt by the value of the meat. The commentary explains that this is analogous to wine: wine is not a valid subject of sale initially because it is not valuable property (muttaqawwim), but it can be retained as property in certain contexts. Similarly, the leather becomes a pledge because the pledge is established upon the destruction of the original item. The value of the leather and meat are assessed on the day of the pledge, not the day of tanning. If the value of the sheep equals the debt, the calculation is straightforward: for every dirham of the sheep's value, one dirham of debt is cancelled. If the sheep's value exceeds the debt, a more detailed rule applies, referenced to al-Kifayah. Second, the passage covers the increase (nama') of the pledged item—such as offspring, milk, wool, and fruit. This increase belongs to the pledgor, but it is also part of the pledge, attached to the original item. If the original item perishes, the increase is redeemed proportionally based on its value at the time of redemption. The debt is divided between the original and the increase. The central dispute here is between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (who say that increase in the debt is not valid) and Abu Yusuf (who allows it by analogy to a sale price). The relied-upon position according to the commentary is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, as stated by the commentator.

Key Terms
نماء (nama'): Increase or growth of the pledged item, such as offspring, milk, wool, fruit.
فك (fakk): Redemption: the release of the pledge upon repayment of the debt or part thereof.
زيادة في الدين (ziyada fi al-dayn): Increase in the debt: adding a new debt to an existing pledge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad), if a mortgagee lends additional money to the pledgor, does the existing pledge automatically cover the new debt?
A) Yes, because the debt is like a price.
B) No, the pledge only covers the original debt; a new pledge is needed.
C) Yes, but only if the pledgor agrees.
D) No, the pledge is voided and a new contract must be made.

Key Takeaway
Increase of a pledged item belongs to the pledgor and is part of the pledge; increasing the debt attached to a pledge is invalid according to the majority of Hanafi imams.

Introduction to the Book of Jinayat: Definition and Purpose
Opening of Kitab al-Jinayat with definition and structural justification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define jinaya as a legal term and explain the structural placement of Kitab al-Jinayat after Kitab al-Rahn based on the principle of means over ends.

Core Idea
Jinaya is a prohibited act harming life or limbs; its rulings protect life, just as rahn protects property – means before ends.

This lesson opens the Book of Jinayat, a critical section of Hanafi fiqh dealing with homicide and bodily injuries. The commentator first explains why this book follows the Book of Pledge (Rahn), linking both to the theme of protection. He then clarifies the linguistic and legal meaning of jinaya, and sets out a structured framework for the discussion. Understanding this foundation is essential before delving into the specific rulings.

Guided Path
1
Examine the structural placement
The commentator places jinayat after rahn. Identify the underlying principle: both are for protection. Rahn protects wealth, jinaya protects life. Since wealth is a means to life, the means comes first.
2
Analyze the objection and response
The passage critiques an opinion from Ghayat al-Bayan that rahn is prioritized because it is legislated while jinaya is a prohibited act. Follow the commentator's rebuttal: the rulings of jinayat are legislated, not the prohibited acts themselves.
3
Define jinaya linguistically and legally
Linguistically, jinaya means 'what one earns' (general evil). Legally, it is specified as a prohibited act harming life or limbs. Distinguish between killing (qatl) and wounding (jarh).
4
List the seven aspects of jinayat
The commentary outlines seven aspects: legislation, cause of obligation, linguistic definition, legal definition, pillar, condition, and ruling. These will structure the subsequent discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الجنايات كتاب الجنايات)
١ ] قوله : كتاب الجنايات ؛ أورد الجنايات بعد الرّهن ؛ لأن كل واحد منهما للوقاية والصيانة، فإنّ الرّهن وثيقة لصيانة المال، وحكمُ الجناية لصيانة النفس، ألا ترى إلى قوله : وَلَكُمْ فِي الْقِصَاصِ حَيَوةٌ يَتأُولِي الْأَلْبَابِ ، ولَمَّا كان المالُ وسيلة لبقاء النفس ، قَدَّمَ الرَّهن على الجنايات، بناءً على تقدم الوسائل على المقاصد، كذا في أكثر الشروح.
(1)
وما قال في «غاية «البيان»: ولكن قَدَّمَ الرّهنَ ؛ لأنه مشروع بالكتاب والسنة بخلاف الجناية؛ لأنها محظور، فإنّها عبارة عمّا ليس للإنسان فعله ؛ ليس بشيء؛ لأنّ المقصود بالبيان في كتاب الجنايات إنّما هو أحكام الجنايات دون أنفسها، ولا شك أنّ أحكامها أيضاً مشروعة وثابتة بالكتاب والسنة.
وأيضاً لا معنى لتأخيرها من هذه الحيثية، ثمّ إنّ الجناية في اللغة اسم لما تجنيه من شيء؛ أي تكسبه، وهي في الأصل مصدر جنى عليه شر جناية، وهو عام في كل ما يقبح بسوء إلا أنه في الشرع خُص بفعل محرم حل بالنفس والأطراف، والأوّل يُسمى قتلاً، وهو فعل من العباد تزولُ به الحياة، والثاني يسمى قطعاً وجرحاً، وفي الشروح : الكلام في الجنايات من أوجه : الأوَّلُ : في معرفة مشروعيتها. والثاني : في سبب وجوبها. والثالث : في تفسيرها لغة. والرابع : في تفسيرها عند الفقهاء. والخامس: في ركنها. والسادس: في شرطها. والسابع : في حكمها.
(1) البقرة : ۱۷۹.
The Book of Jinayat (Homicide and Injuries). [1] His saying: 'The Book of Jinayat'; he placed Jinayat after Rahn because each of them is for protection and preservation: Rahn is a documentary security for the preservation of wealth, while the ruling of Jinaya is for the preservation of life. Do you not see His saying: 'And there is for you in legal retribution life, O people of understanding.' (2:179). And since wealth is a means for the preservation of life, he placed Rahn before Jinayat, based on the precedence of means over ends. This is found in most commentaries. (1) What is said in Ghayat al-Bayan: 'But he placed Rahn first because it is legislated by the Book and Sunnah, unlike Jinaya because it is a prohibited act; for it is an expression of what one should not do' – this is not correct; because the purpose of explanation in the Book of Jinayat is only the rulings of Jinayat, not the acts themselves, and no doubt the rulings are also legislated and established by the Book and Sunnah. Also there is no reason to delay it from this perspective. Then, Jinaya in language is a noun for what one earns from something, i.e., acquires, and it is originally a verbal noun meaning 'he committed an evil act', and it is general for everything ugly with evil, but in law it is specified as a prohibited act affecting life and limbs. The first is called killing, which is an act of the servant by which life ceases, and the second is called amputation and wounding. In the commentaries: Discussion of Jinayat has several aspects: First: Knowing its legislation. Second: The cause of its obligation. Third: Its linguistic interpretation. Fourth: Its interpretation among jurists. Fifth: Its pillar. Sixth: Its condition. Seventh: Its ruling. (1) Al-Baqarah: 179.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage is the opening of the Book of Jinayat (Homicide and Injuries) from a commentary on al-Wiqaya. It begins by justifying the placement of this book after the Book of Rahn (Pledge). The author explains that both topics deal with protection: Rahn protects property, while Jinayat protects life. Since property is a means for preserving life, the means (Rahn) is placed before the end (Jinayat). This is supported by the Quranic verse (2:179) which states that in retaliation there is life. The passage then critiques an opinion from Ghayat al-Bayan that claimed Rahn was placed first because it is legislated by Quran and Sunnah while Jinaya is a prohibited act (and thus not legislated). The commentator rejects this, arguing that the rulings of Jinaya are indeed legislated. The passage then provides a linguistic and legal definition of 'jinaya'. Linguistically, it means earning or acquiring, but legally it is specified as a prohibited act that causes harm to life or limbs. Killing (qatl) is one type, and wounding/amputation is the second. Finally, the commentary outlines seven aspects under which Jinayat will be discussed: legislation, cause of obligation, linguistic definition, legal definition, pillar, condition, and ruling. This sets the framework for the detailed fiqh rules to come.

Key Terms
جناية (jinaya): A prohibited act that causes harm to life (killing) or limbs (wounding/amputation).
قصاص (qisas): Retaliation, the prescribed punishment for intentional homicide or injury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary reason given in the passage for placing Kitab al-Jinayat after Kitab al-Rahn?
A) Rahn is more important.
B) Rahn is a means to preserve wealth, which is a means to preserve life, so means precede ends.
C) Jinaya is a prohibited act, so it is placed later.
D) The order is arbitrary.

Key Takeaway
Jinaya is a prohibited act harming life or limbs; its rulings are legislated to protect life, and the book's placement after Rahn follows the principle of means before ends.

Intentional Killing: Definition, Qisas, and Kaffarah
Commentary on definition, qisas, and kaffarah in intentional killing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of intentional killing, explain why qisas is determined (wajib 'aynan), and evaluate the Shafi'i argument for kaffarah.

Core Idea
Intentional killing is pure sin; qisas is determined, and kaffarah does not apply.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah addresses three critical questions in Hanafi criminal law: what counts as intentional killing, whether the guardian may choose diya instead of qisas, and whether kaffarah is required. These rulings are contrasted with the Shafi'i school, giving us a clear view of Hanafi usul in action.

Guided Path
1
Define intentional killing
Identify the Hanafi definition of 'amd, noting the intra-school difference between Abu Hanifa (requires sharp instrument) and the Sahibayn (any instrument the body cannot bear). Determine the mufta bihi.
Sahibayn's view is relied upon.
2
Establish the sin and qisas obligation
Note the Quranic proof for sin (4:93) and qisas (2:178). Explain how the verses are applied, including extension to dhimmis by qiyas.
3
Analyze the dispute over qisas being determined
Compare the Hanafi position (qisas is determined, no diya without consent) with the Shafi'i position (guardian chooses). Understand the Hanafi qiyas: accidental killing requires diya because no equivalence; intentional killing has equivalence, so qisas is fixed.
The key principle: mumathalah (equivalence).
4
Examine the dispute over kaffarah
State the Shafi'i argument (qiyas a priori: if kaffarah in accidental, then more so in intentional) and the Hanafi rebuttal (intentional killing is pure sin, kaffarah is worship; worship does not attach to pure sin).
5
Note the consequence of inheritance deprivation
Recognize that the killer is deprived of inheritance based on the hadith 'The killer does not inherit.'
This is a separate ruling but related.

The book of Criminal Acts / Types of Killing and What Necessitates Qisas and Testimony Thereon. And with it he incurs sin, and qisas is wajib 'aynan. This according to Abu Hanifah, and according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and according to al-Shafiʿi: if he strikes him intentionally with something that the body cannot normally bear, such that if he struck him with a large stone or a large piece of wood, it is intentional (amd). (And with it he incurs sin), and qisas is wajib 'aynan. This is our view, contrary to al-Shafiʿi. [1] He said: This is according to Abu Hanifah, i.e., the requirement of sharpness (hiddah) meaning that killing with something that has no sharpness, like something that kills, or the flat side of iron or copper, does not necessitate qisas according to him. And Qadi Khan mentioned for him: that a wound (jarh) is not required in iron and what resembles it, like copper and others, in the zahir al-riwayah. Thus in al-Tabyin. [2] He said: With it he incurs sin due to His statement (Quran 4:93): 'And whoever kills a believer intentionally, his reward is Hell, abiding therein eternally.' And many hadiths have spoken about it, and consensus has been formed upon it. And the mentioned verse, although it indicates sin in killing a Muslim intentionally only in its explicit wording, yet it indicates sin in killing a dhimmi intentionally also by implication of the text, based on the establishment of equality in sanctity (ismah) between the Muslim and the dhimmi with regard to obligation (taklif) or abode (dar). [3] He said: And qisas is wajib due to His statement (Quran 2:178): 'Prescribed for you is legal retribution for those killed.' And the verse in its apparent sense does not distinguish between intentional and accidental killing, but it is restricted by the description of intentionality due to his statement: 'Intentional killing necessitates qisas.' And because the injury is thereby completed, the deterrent wisdom is achieved, and the ultimate punishment has no legal basis without that qisas (with two fathahs: qisas). [4] He said: 'Aynan, i.e., the guardian has no right except to it, and he has no right to take diya from the perpetrator except with his consent. This is the summary of what is in the commentaries of al-Hidayah. Then he said: (No kaffarah). Husn al-Dirayah li-Awakhir Sharh al-Wiqayah. For qisas is not determined according to him (al-Shafiʿi), rather the guardian has a choice between qisas and taking diya. Our proof: that wealth (diya) is only obligatory in accidental killing as a necessity to preserve blood from being wasted, since there is no equivalence (mumathalah) between wealth and the soul. So in intentional killing it is not wajib when there is the possibility of equivalence (mithl) in form and meaning. (No kaffarah), contrary to al-Shafiʿi, as he says: It (kaffarah) is wajib in accidental killing, so it is even more appropriate that it be wajib in intentional killing. And we say: It does not follow from kaffarah covering accidental killing that it covers intentional killing, because intentional killing is pure sin (kabirah mahdah). [1] He said: For qisas is not determined; meaning that what is obligatory is one of two – either qisas or diya, not specified, and it becomes specified by the choice of the guardian, because the right of the servant is legislated as a compensation, and in each there is a type of compensation, so he chooses between qisas and taking diya. [2] He said: Our proof that wealth... etc. Explanation: Indeed wealth is not suitable as a cause due to lack of equivalence, and qisas is suitable for equivalence, and in it is the benefit of preserving life, as deterrent and compensation, so it becomes determined. And in accidental killing, the obligation of wealth is necessary to preserve the blood from being wasted, and there is no certainty that the guardian will not have intent after taking the wealth, so it is not determined to prevent loss. [3] He said: So it is even more appropriate that it be wajib... etc. Because the need for kaffarah in intentional killing is more pressing than in accidental, so intentional killing is more deserving of making it wajib. And our proof: Intentional killing is pure sin, with no hint of permissibility, and in kaffarah there is the meaning of worship, so it cannot be attached to such a thing. And because kaffarah is from the fixed measures of the Shariʿah, and its designation in the Shariʿah is to repel the lesser, not to repel the greater. And from its ruling is the deprivation of inheritance (hirman al-mirath), although it is not explicitly stated, but he alluded to it later by saying: And no inheritance except here, as will come. The proof of deprivation is his statement: 'A killer does not inherit.' (1) See: Rawd al-Talib and its commentary Asna al-Matalib (4:3) and others. (2) Its explanation: that kaffarah applies to what is between prohibition and permissibility, and intentional killing is pure sin, not befitting that kaffarah cover it due to the presence of the meaning of worship in it. See: Majmaʿ al-Anhur (2:616), al-Muhit (pp. 217-218). (3) On 'The killer does not inherit' in Sunan al-Tirmidhi (4:425), Sunan al-Darimi (2:479), Sunan Ibn Majah (2:883), al-Athar (1:161), Sunan al-Darqutni (4:96), Sunan al-Bayhaqi al-Kabir (6:220), al-Bayhaqi said: Its supporting evidences strengthen it.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on a matn concerning the rulings of intentional killing (qatl al-ʿamd). It addresses three main points: (1) the definition of intentional killing according to the Hanafi school, (2) the obligation of qisas (qawad) and whether it is determined (muʿayyan) or optional, and (3) the non-obligation of kaffarah for intentional killing. First, the commentator clarifies that according to Abu Hanifah, for killing to be considered intentional (ʿamd) and thus liable for qisas, the instrument used must be sharp (haddah) – i.e., it must cut or pierce. If one uses a blunt object like the flat side of a sword or a large stone, it does not constitute ʿamd in the sense that requires qisas; rather it may be considered quasi-intentional (shibh ʿamd). However, his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, along with Imam al-Shafiʿi, hold that if the blow is delivered intentionally with an object that the body cannot normally withstand – even if it is not sharp – it is ʿamd and qisas is wajib. The later relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is that of the Sahibayn, and this is given in the zahir al-riwayah as mentioned in al-Tabyin. Second, the commentator explains the sin (ithm) of such killing, citing Quran 4:93 and extending it to dhimmis by qiyas of equal sanctity. Third, concerning qisas, he argues that it is determined (ʿaynan); meaning that the guardian of the victim has no right to demand diya unless the killer agrees. This is contrary to al-Shafiʿi, who gives the guardian a choice. The Hanafi proof is based on a qiyas: in accidental killing (khata), wealth (diya) is obligatory out of necessity to prevent bloodshed from going unavenged because there is no exact equivalence between wealth and a human life. In intentional killing, there is the possibility of equivalence (mumathalah) through qisas, so the Shariʿah determines qisas, not diya. Fourth, the passage discusses the non-obligation of kaffarah for intentional killing. al-Shafiʿi argues that since kaffarah is wajib in accidental killing, it should be even more so in intentional killing. The Hanafi response is that intentional killing is pure sin (kabirah mahdah) with no shade of permissibility, whereas kaffarah is an act of worship that is prescribed for lesser offenses that have some ambiguity. Since kaffarah involves worship, it cannot be attached to a pure major sin. The text also mentions that one of the consequences of intentional killing is the killer's deprivation of inheritance, as established by the hadith 'The killer does not inherit.' All rulings are based on Zahir al-Riwayah unless otherwise noted.

Key Terms
العمد (al-ʿamd): Intentionally striking with an object that the body cannot normally bear, done with the intention to kill.
القود (al-qawad): Qiṣāṣ, legal retribution; taking the life of the killer in return for the life of the victim.
الکفارة (al-kaffarah): Expiation; a penalty consisting of fasting, feeding the poor, or freeing a slave to atone for certain sins.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling regarding kaffarah for intentional killing?
A) Kaffarah is wajib, just as in accidental killing.
B) Kaffarah is not required because intentional killing is pure sin.
C) Kaffarah is recommended but not obligatory.
D) Kaffarah is wajib only if the victim is a Muslim.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, intentional killing mandates qisas without the option of diya (unless the killer consents) and does not require kaffarah, contrary to the Shafi'i school.

Definition and Ruling of Shibh al-'Amd (Semi-Intentional Killing)
Defines shibh al-'amd and presents intra-Hanafi dispute on tool criteria

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of shibh al-'amd and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the tool size criterion.

Core Idea
Shibh al-'amd: intentional striking with a non-lethal tool; size determines 'amd or shibh.

Shibh al-'amd (semi-intentional killing) occupies a middle ground between intentional murder and accidental killing. Its precise definition hinges on the nature of the tool used and the intent behind the strike. This passage from the Hanafi tradition not only defines the category but also presents a key internal dispute: does striking with a large stone or heavy wood still qualify as shibh al-'amd, or does it cross into intentional murder ('amd)? Understanding this dispute is essential for correctly applying the rules of retaliation (qisas) and blood-money (diyah).

Guided Path
1
Define shibh al-'amd
Read the definition: intentional striking with a tool that is not ordinarily fatal (like a stick, whip, or small stone). The killer intends to strike but not to kill.
The tool is key: it must be for discipline, not killing.
2
Identify the legal consequences
Note the four consequences: sin (ithm), expiation (kaffarah), aggravated blood-money (diyah maghullazah) on the 'aqilah, and no retaliation (qisas).
Contrast with 'amd (qisas) and khata' (lighter diyah).
3
Trace the intra-Hanafi dispute
Compare Abu Hanifa's position (large stone/wood = shibh al-'amd) with that of the Sahibayn and al-Shafi'i (large stone/wood = 'amd). Focus on their respective proofs.
Abu Hanifa cites a hadith about the whip and stick; opponents argue the tool is lethal.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
Understand the practical difference: according to Abu Hanifa, no qisas but diyah; according to the Sahibayn, qisas applies.
This difference can mean life or death for the perpetrator.
5
Evaluate the mufta bihi
Consider that the passage does not give a clear tarjih; later Hanafi authorities often follow Abu Hanifa, but some prefer the Sahibayn.
The dispute remains alive in Hanafi fatwa practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشبه العمد : ضربه قصداً بغير ما ذكر، وفيه الإثم والكفارة، ودِيَةٌ مغلظة على العاقلة ، بلا قود، وهو فيما دون النفس عمد. وشبه العمد: ضربه قصداً بغير ما ذكر : كالعصا والسوط ، أو الحجر الصغير، وأما الضرب بالحجرِ العظيم، والخشب العظيم فمن شبه العمد أيضا عند أبي حنيفة له، خلافاً لغيره، (وفيه الإثم والكفارة، ودِيَةٌ مغلظة على العاقلة) ، سيأتي تفسيرُ الدِّيةِ المغلظة، وتفسير العاقلة إن شاء الله ، (بلا قود. وهو فيما دون النفس عمد) : أي ضربُهُ قصداً بغير ما ذكر فيما دونَ النَّفْسِ عمد موجب للقصاص ، فليس فيما دون النفس شبه عمد. قوله : وشبه العمد ؛ إنما سمي هذا شبه العمد ؛ لأنّ في الفعل معنيين، معنى العمد باعتبار قصد الفاعل إلى الضرب، ومعنى الخطأ باعتبار انعدام قصدِ القتل بالنظرِ إلى الآلة التي استعملها، إذ هي آلة الضرب للتأديب دون القتل، وإنما يقصد إلى كلِّ فعل بآلته ، فكان ذلك خطأ يشبه العمد صورةً ، من حيث إنه كان قاصداً إلى الضرب، وإلى ارتكاب ما هو محرّم عليه. كذا في «الكفاية). قوله : خلافاً لغيره ؛ قال في «الهداية) : وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد ، وهو قول الشافعي : إذا ضربه بحجر عظيم أو بخشبة عظيمة فهو عمد، وشبه العمد أن يتعمد ضربه بما لا يقتلُ به غالباً كالعصا الصغير إذا لم يوال في الضربات. أما إذا والى فيها فهو عمد، لكن فيه يتقاصرُ معنى العمدية باستعمال آلة صغيرة لا يقتل غالباً لما أنه يقصد بها غير القتل كالتأديب ونحوه ، فكان شبه العمد. ولأبي حنيفة قوله : «ألا إنّ قتيل خطأ العمد – أي شبه العمد – قتيل السوط والعصا فيه مئة من الإبل» رواه ابن أبي شيبة في «مصنفه» عن الحسن ؛ ولأن الآلة غير موضوعة للقتل ولا مستعملة فيه.
And semi-intentional (shibh al-'amd): his striking intentionally with other than what was mentioned [i.e., not with a weapon that usually kills], and in it there is sin and expiation, and an aggravated blood-money on the 'aqilah, without retaliation; and it [the rule regarding what is below the soul] is intentional [i.e., retaliation applies] below the soul. And semi-intentional: his striking intentionally with other than what was mentioned: like a stick and a whip, or a small stone. As for striking with a large stone and large wood, it is also from semi-intentional according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, contrary to others. (And in it there is sin and expiation, and an aggravated blood-money on the 'aqilah) – the interpretation of the aggravated blood-money and the interpretation of the 'aqilah will come, Allah willing. (Without retaliation. And it [the rule] regarding what is below the soul is intentional): i.e., his striking intentionally with other than what was mentioned regarding below the soul is intentional, rendering retaliation obligatory; so there is no semi-intentional below the soul. He said: 'And semi-intentional'; it is only called semi-intentional because in the act there are two meanings: the meaning of intentionality in consideration of the actor's intent to strike, and the meaning of mistake in consideration of the absence of intent to kill with regard to the tool he used, since it is a tool for striking for discipline, not for killing, and one intends every act by its tool; so it was a mistake that resembles intentional in form, in that he intended to strike and to commit what is prohibited upon him. This is in 'al-Kifayah'. He said: 'contrary to others'; he said in 'al-Hidayah': Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, and it is the saying of al-Shafi'i: if he strikes with a large stone or large wood, it is intentional; and semi-intentional is that he intentionally strikes with what does not usually kill, like a small stick if he does not repeat the strikes. But if he repeats them, it is intentional. However, in that the meaning of intentionality is diminished by using a small tool that does not usually kill, because he intends by it other than killing, like discipline and the like, so it was semi-intentional. And for Abu Hanifa is his statement: 'Indeed, the killed of the mistake of intention – i.e., semi-intentional – the killed of the whip and stick, in it one hundred camels' – narrated by Ibn Abi Shaybah in his 'Musannaf' from al-Hasan; and because the tool is not placed for killing nor used for it.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage defines shibh al-'amd (semi-intentional killing) and presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the criteria for distinguishing it from intentional murder ('amd). The core definition: shibh al-'amd occurs when the killer intends the act of striking but does not intend to kill, using a tool that is not ordinarily lethal (like a stick, whip, or small stone). The ruling: it entails sin (ithm), expiation (kaffarah), an aggravated blood-money (diyah maghullazah) payable by the 'aqilah (the killer's male paternal relatives), and no retaliation (qisas). Importantly, in cases below the soul (i.e., injuries not causing death), there is no shibh 'amd; such acts are treated as 'amd and require qisas. The commentary explains that the term 'shibh al-'amd' derives from the act having two aspects: intentionality in the striking, and mistake in the lack of intent to kill given the tool. The intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa holds that striking with a large stone or large wood is still shibh al-'amd, because the tool is not specifically designed for killing (it could be used for other purposes). In contrast, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, and al-Shafi'i consider such an act to be intentional murder, because the tool is lethal. Abu Hanifa supports his view with a hadith: 'The killed of the mistake of intention – i.e., semi-intentional – the killed of the whip and stick, in it one hundred camels.' The passage also clarifies that if the striker repeats blows with a small stick, it may become 'amd according to the majority, but even then the intentionality is diminished due to the small tool.

Key Terms
شبه العمد (shibh al-'amd): Semi-intentional killing: the killer intends the act (striking) but does not intend to kill, using a tool that is not ordinarily fatal.
الدية المغلظة (al-diyah al-mughallaẓah): Aggravated blood-money: a higher amount of diyah (e.g., 100 camels with specific age/types) imposed for shibh al-'amd.
العاقلة (al-'āqilah): The male paternal relatives ('asabah) of the killer who are responsible for paying the diyah in cases of shibh al-'amd and khata'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if someone strikes and kills with a large wooden club?
A) It is 'amd according to all Hanafi imams.
B) It is shibh al-'amd according to Abu Hanifa, but 'amd according to the Sahibayn.
C) It is khata' because the club is not a standard weapon.
D) It is shibh al-'amd according to the Sahibayn, but 'amd according to Abu Hanifa.

Key Takeaway
Shibh al-'amd is defined by the tool's non-lethal nature; its application to large tools is disputed, with Abu Hanifa adopting a broader definition than the Sahibayn.

Apportioning Diyah by Cause Grouping (Jins)
Ruling on diyah when death results from self-action, third party, and animals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi method of grouping causes by jins to determine the share of diyah when multiple agents contribute to death.

Core Idea
Causes are grouped by legal classification (jins) – animal actions count as one, self-action as another, and a third party as a third.

When death is caused by multiple factors, the Hanafi school does not count each actor individually but groups them by their legal category (jins). This lesson examines a case involving the deceased's own action, a third party, and animals. Understanding the grouping principle is key to correctly apportioning diyah.

Guided Path
1
Identify all contributing causes
List every action that contributed to the death: the deceased's own action (fi'l nafsihi), Zaid's act, the wild beast's attack, and the snake's bite.
The text mentions four actions, but the ruling groups some together.
2
Classify each action by legal consequence
Determine whether each action is absolutely hadar (no worldly liability), partially hadar, or fully liable. Animal actions are absolutely hadar; self-action is hadar only in this world (sin remains); Zaid's action is fully liable.
This classification is key to the grouping.
3
Group actions into ajnas (types)
Actions sharing the same classification form one jins. The beast and snake are both absolutely hadar → one jins. Self-action is a separate jins (hadar in this world only). Zaid's action is a third jins.
4
Count the number of effective groups
The ruling considers the number of categories, not individual actors. Here there are three groups: animal actions (1), self-action (2), Zaid's action (3).
5
Assign Zaid's share proportionally
Since Zaid's action is one of three equal categories, he must pay one-third of the full diyah. This is the mufta bihi position.
An objection from the hashiya argues for four groups (one-fourth), but the relied-upon ruling is one-third.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي موت بفعل نفسه وزيد وسبع وحيةٍ ثلث الدِّيةِ على زيد.
قوله : وفي موت بفعل نفسه وزيد وسبع وحيّةٍ ثلث الدِّيةِ على زيد)؛ لأَنَّهُ ماتَ بثلاثة أفعال، ففعلُ السبع والحيّة جنس واحد لكونه هدرا مطلقاً، وفعل نفسه جنس آخر، وهو أنه هدرّ في الدُّنيا لا في الآخرة ، وفعل زيد جنس آخر ، فيجب ثلث الدية.
[١] قوله : لا في الآخرة ; حتى يؤثم عليه، وفي «النوادر»: إن عند أبي حنيفة له ومحمد له يغسل ويصلّى عليه، وعند أبي يوسف له : يغسل ولا يصلى عليه. قال في «الكفاية) : هذا أثر كون فعله غير معتبر ; لأنه لَمَّا كان يغسل ويصلى عليه صار كأنه مات حتف أنفه بمرضه من غير فعله على نفسه عندهما. أما عند أبي يوسف الله فجنايته على نفسه معتبرة حتى لا يصلى عليه، وصار بمنزلة الباغي، ولو كان فعله هدراً أصلاً كنهش الحية ، ولم يكن جناية مع كونه مقتولاً حقيقة، لكان شهيداً، ويسقط غسله، فلم يكن فعله هدراً مطلقاً، فكان جنساً آخر، وفعل السبع والحيّة هدرّ في الدنيا والآخرة، وفعل الأجنبي معتبر في الدنيا والآخرة، فيكون التالف بفعل كل واحد ثلاثة ، فيجب عليه ثلث الدية.
[٢] قوله : أقول يجب... الخ ; اعترض به على قوله : ثلث الدية على زيد، حاصله : إن المؤثر هاهنا أمور أربعة ; لكلّ واحدٍ منها تأثير في القتل، فلا اعتبار للاتحاد في كونها هدراً في الدنيا والآخرة، فلَمَّا كان فعلُ المؤثرات الثلاث هاهنا هدراً ينبغي أن يجب على الرابع ربع الدية لا ثلثها، فضمير اتحاده وتعدده راجع إلى ما ، واللام في لكونهما صلة للاعتبار لا لتعليق عدم الاعتبار، كما لا يخفى ، هـذا مـا أفـاده أخي جلبي.
And in death by the action of himself, Zaid, a wild beast, and a snake, one-third of the blood-money is upon Zaid. He said: 'And in death by the action of himself, Zaid, a wild beast, and a snake, one-third of the blood-money is upon Zaid) because he died by three actions: the action of the wild beast and the snake are one type because they are absolutely hadar (irrecoverable), and his own action is another type, which is hadar in this world but not in the afterlife, and the action of Zaid is another type, so one-third of the blood-money is obligatory. [1] He said: 'not in the afterlife' so that he is sinful for it, and in al-Nawadir: According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, he is washed and prayed upon, and according to Abu Yusuf, he is washed but not prayed upon. He said in al-Kifayah: This is the effect of his action being unconsidered; because when he is washed and prayed upon, it is as if he died naturally by his illness without his own action according to them. According to Abu Yusuf, his injury to himself is considered, so he is not prayed upon, and he becomes like a rebel (baghi). And if his action were absolutely hadar like a snake bite, and it were not an injury while he is actually killed, he would be a martyr, and his washing is dropped. So his action is not absolutely hadar, so it is another type. And the action of the wild beast and snake is hadar in this world and the afterlife, and the action of an outsider is considered in this world and the afterlife, so the one who suffers from the action of each is three, so one-third of the blood-money is obligatory upon him. [2] He said: 'I say that it is necessary...' to the end; he objects to his saying 'one-third of the blood-money upon Zaid'. Its gist: The effective causes here are four things; each of them has an effect in the killing, so there is no consideration of unity in their being hadar in this world and the afterlife. Since the action of the three effective causes here is hadar, it should be obligatory upon the fourth one-fourth of the blood-money, not one-third. The pronoun of its unity and multiplicity refers to what, and the lam in 'li-kawnihima' is a connection for consideration, not to suspend lack of consideration, as is not hidden. This is what my brother Jalabi mentioned.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on diyah when multiple causes contribute to death. The matn states that when a person dies due to his own action, the action of Zaid, a wild beast, and a snake, one-third of the diyah is upon Zaid. The sharh explains the reasoning: the causes are grouped into three categories (ajnās) based on their legal classification. The beast and snake are one type because their actions are absolutely hadar (no worldly liability). The self-action is hadar only in this world but not in the afterlife (so it carries sin). Zaid's action is a third type that is considered. Thus, there are three effective causes, and Zaid bears one-third. The hashiya [1] provides an intra-Hanafi dispute: on the issue of whether the person who dies by his own action should be washed and prayed over. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad say yes, while Abu Yusuf says no. This dispute affects the classification: according to the former, the self-action is not considered a valid cause in this world, so it groups with the animals? Actually the sharh says it is a separate type, but the hashiya explains that Abu Yusuf considers it a valid injury (jinaayah), which would make it a fourth cause, but the text still says one-third. The hashiya [2] objects to the one-third, arguing that there are actually four effective causes (self, beast, snake, Zaid), so Zaid should only pay one-fourth. This objection is noted but the relied-upon ruling is one-third as in the matn.

Key Terms
هدر (hadar): An action that carries no worldly legal liability; the killer does not have to pay diyah, but may be sinful in the afterlife.
جنس (jins): A category of actions sharing the same legal classification for the purpose of apportioning diyah.
فعل نفسه (fi'l nafsihi): The deceased's own action contributing to his death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Full diyah
B) One-half diyah
C) One-third diyah
D) One-fourth diyah

Key Takeaway
When multiple causes contribute to death, diyah is apportioned by grouping causes with the same legal classification (jins), not by counting individual actors.

Obligation to Kill One Who Draws a Sword on Muslims
Ruling with clarifying commentary on obligation to kill

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rules that killing a sword-brandisher is wajib only when it is the sole means to repel harm.

Core Idea
Killing a Muslim aggressor is wajib only when it is the uniquely effective way to stop him.

The passage presents a seemingly absolute ruling: it is obligatory to kill anyone who draws a sword against Muslims. But the commentary immediately qualifies it, explaining that the primary obligation is repelling harm, not killing per se. This lesson unpacks that nuance and shows how the Hanafi school balances public safety with necessity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the literal ruling
State the explicit statement: 'It is obligatory to kill one who draws a sword on Muslims.' This is the ruling given in the matn.
2
Recognize the commentary's qualification
Note that the commentator calls the literal ruling a 'ta'assuf' (strained interpretation) and clarifies that the true obligation is to repel evil and harm by any means.
This indicates the literal wording is not to be taken mechanically.
3
Understand the principle of daf' al-sharr
The underlying obligation is daf' al-sharr wa al-darar (repelling evil and harm). Killing is merely one possible method; it becomes obligatory only when it is the only effective method.
This aligns with the greater purpose of protecting life.
4
Apply the condition of necessity
Determine when killing becomes wajib: only when other means (e.g., restraint, warning, disabling) are impossible or ineffective. If the sword-bearer can be stopped without killing, killing is not obligatory.
This prevents misuse of the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجب قتلُ مَن شَهَر سيفاً على المسلمين.
قوله : ويجب قتل من شهر سيفاً ... الخ ; فيه نوع تعسف ; لأنّ الواجب دفع الشر والضرر على أي طريق كان لا عين القتل، وإنما يجب لكونه طريقاً متعيناً له.
And it is obligatory to kill one who draws a sword on Muslims. He said: 'And it is obligatory to kill one who draws a sword... to the end; in it there is some difficulty (ta'assuf); because what is obligatory is repelling evil and harm by any means, not specifically killing, but it becomes obligatory because it is the only way to do so.'
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling that it is wajib (obligatory) to kill a person who draws a sword against Muslims. The commentary (sharh) notes a nuance: the primary obligation is to repel evil and harm (daf' al-sharr) by any effective means, not specifically killing. However, if killing is the only way to stop the aggressor, then it becomes specifically obligatory. The commentary characterizes this as a degree of ta'assuf (forced interpretation) because the literal ruling seems to prescribe killing categorically, but the deeper principle is about necessity.

Key Terms
شهر سيفاً (shahara sayfan): To draw or brandish a sword with the intent to attack Muslims.
تعسف (ta'assuf): A forced or strained interpretation, indicating that the literal ruling requires explanation to align with the overall principle of repelling harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) To kill the person immediately.
B) To repel harm by any effective means.
C) To report the person to the authorities.
D) To forgive the person if they repent.

Key Takeaway
The obligation to kill a sword-brandisher is conditional: it only becomes wajib when killing is the sole effective means to repel harm.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Combined Causes of Death
Intra-Hanafi dispute on division of diya and funeral rites

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when death results from combined acts of a lion, snake, and a person, and explain the dispute between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf regarding funeral prayers.

Core Idea
Liability for diya is divided only among legally relevant causes; acts of animals are hadar and do not share in the obligation.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains a case where a lion, a snake, and a person (Zaid) all contribute to a death. The Hanafi school agrees that Zaid owes one-third diya, but they differ on whether Zaid's act is considered a self-offense affecting funeral prayers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
What is the factual situation? A lion, a snake, and a person (Zaid) all contribute to a death.
2
State the agreed ruling
The Hanafi school agrees that Zaid owes one-third of the diya (blood money).
The lion and snake acts are hadar (legally wasted) and do not require diya.
3
Identify the point of dispute
The dispute is about funeral treatment: should Zaid be washed and prayed over?
4
State Abu Hanifa and Muhammad's view
Zaid is washed and prayed over because his act is not considered a self-offense; it is akin to natural death.
Their reasoning: if his act were absolutely hadar like snakebite, he would be a martyr and not washed; but since he is washed, his act has some legal effect.
5
State Abu Yusuf's view
Zaid is washed but not prayed over, because his act is a self-offense, similar to a rebel (baghi).
6
Determine the practical difference
The practical consequence (thamarah) is whether Zaid receives funeral prayers (salat al-janazah) or not.
The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, as supported by the commentator.

The book of crimes / Types of killing and what necessitates qisas and testimony upon it. Another category: it is deemed blood-wasted in this world but not in the Hereafter, and Zaid's act is another category, so one-third of the diya is obligatory. [2] I say: It is necessary to look at what is effective in death, and to look at its unity and multiplicity, for the lion and snake are two, and there is no consideration in that for both being blood-wasted. (It is obligatory to kill one who draws a sword against Muslims) [1] His statement: not in the Hereafter, so that he is sinful for it. And in al-Nawadir: According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, he is washed and prayed over, and according to Abu Yusuf, he is washed but not prayed over. He said in al-Kifaya: This is an effect of his act not being considered, because when he is washed and prayed over, it becomes as if he died of a disease from his own act. According to Abu Yusuf, his offense against himself is considered such that he is not prayed over, and he is like a rebel. And if his act were absolutely wasted like the bite of a snake, and it were not an offense while being truly slain, he would be a martyr and his washing would be omitted. So his act is not absolutely wasted, but is another category. The act of the lion and snake is wasted in both worlds, and the act of the stranger is considered in both worlds, so the destruction by each of the three acts is one-third, so one-third of the diya is obligatory. [2] His statement: I say it is obligatory... etc.; an objection to his statement: one-third of the diya on Zaid. The summary: the effective factors here are four, each having an effect in killing. So there is no consideration for unity in their being wasted. Since the acts of the three effective factors here are wasted, it is appropriate that on the fourth one-fourth of the diya should be obligatory, not one-third. The pronoun of 'unity and multiplicity' refers back to 'what', and the lam in 'for both' is a connective for consideration. This is what my brother Jalabi expressed.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the division of diya when multiple causes—a lion, a snake, and a human act—contribute to a death. The text begins by stating that the combined effect results in one-third diya on Zaid (the human actor). The commentator then explains that the effective causes are a lion, a snake, and Zaid's act; however, only Zaid's act carries legal liability in both worlds, whereas the lion and snake are considered legally irrelevant (hadar). The basis is that the lion and snake's actions are not punishable; thus only Zaid's share matters. The commentary then details the differing views of the Hanafi imams: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that Zaid is washed and prayed over (his act is not self-offense), while Abu Yusuf holds that he is not prayed over because his self-offense is considered. This difference stems from whether Zaid's act is analogous to suicide or to uncontrollable injury. The passage concludes with an objection that the effective factors are actually four (lion, snake, Zaid, and an unknown?), prompting a defense that the unity of the lion and snake as two is not considered, so diya is one-third.

Key Terms
هدر (hadar): Legally deemed wasted blood, meaning no liability and no claims for diya or qisas.
جنس (jins): A category or genus of action; here referring to the classification of acts causing death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because he is an unbeliever
C) Because the snakebite made him impure
D) Because his family refused to allow it

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, diya is divided only among causes that carry legal liability; animal actions are hadar and do not contribute, but human actions are considered, and whether it constitutes self-offense affects funeral rites.

Ruling on Killing a Sword-Brandisher Against Muslims
Stating the ruling, conditions, and proof for killing a sword-brandisher with impunity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on killing a sword-brandisher, identify the conditions under which no liability arises, and apply the distinction between weapons and sticks based on immediacy and availability of help (ghawth).

Core Idea
Whoever draws a sword against Muslims may be killed with impunity; his blood is wasted.

In the Hanafi school, when a person brandishes a weapon against Muslims, the community is obligated to repel the threat, and the killer bears no liability. But what if the aggressor uses only a stick? Or attacks at night in a city? This lesson unpacks the detailed conditions that determine when killing is lawful and when the killer's action might incur financial or penal consequences. We will examine the primary proof from hadith and the logical reasoning behind the distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central ruling
Read the first line of the Arabic: 'It is obligatory to kill one who draws a sword against Muslims, and there is nothing for killing him.' This is the core hukm. Note the hukm level (wajib) and the consequence (no liability).
The ruling is fard kifayah if possible, but the individual killer acts in self-defense.
2
Understand the need for the second clause
The commentator raises a possible objection: if killing is obligatory, why add 'no liability'? The answer: to exclude the possibility that the killer might be liable despite the obligation, as in some other cases (e.g., killing a rampaging camel).
This shows the precision of fiqh language.
3
Analyze the conditions for impunity
The text distinguishes between weapons and sticks, and between day/night and city/desert. For weapons: always impunity because the attack is immediate (ghayr mulabbath). For sticks: impunity only if the victim cannot get help (ghawth) – i.e., at night in a city or by day outside a city.
The key concept is ghawth (availability of help).
4
Examine the dalil and rationale
The hadith 'Whoever draws his sword against Muslims, his blood is wasted' is cited. Additionally, the aggressor is considered a baghi (rebel), so his inviolability is lost. The killer is also acting in legitimate self-defense.
The hadith is ahad, thus zanni in thubut but accepted.
5
Consider the juristic expansion
The jurists add that a small stick that does not delay (like a club) is treated like a weapon. Also, a thick blunt weapon may be similar. This shows usul al-fiqh at work: extending the ruling via qiyas.
The principle: if the attack is immediate or help is absent, killing is justified without liability.

Source Text

( ويجب قتلُ مَن شَهَر سيفاً على المسلمين
ولا شيء بقتله، ولا في مَن شَهَرَ سلاحا على رجل ليلا أو نهارا في مصر وغيره، أو شَهَرَ عليه عصاً ليلاً في مصر أو نهاراً في غيره فقتله المشهور عليه ولا شيء بقتله) ، فإن قلت : لَمَّا قال يجب قتلُ مَن شَهَر ، فما الاحتياج إلى قوله : لا شيء بقتله ، قلت : يحتمل أن يجب قتله دفعاً للشر، ومع . ذلك يجب بقتله شيء. (ولا في مَن شَهَرَ سلاحاً على رجل ليلاً أو نهاراً في مصر وغيره، أو شَهَرَ عليه عصاً ليلاً في مصر أو نهاراً في غيره فقتله المشهور عليه)، السَّلاحُ إِذَا شَهَرَهُ فلا شيء بقتله مطلقاً ؛ لأنه غير ملبث ، والعصا إذا شَهَرَهُ ليلاً في مصر أو نهاراً في غيره فلا شيء بقتله أيضاً ؛ لأنَّهُ وإن كان ملبثاً ففي الليل في المصر لا يلحقه الغوث، وكذا في النهار في غير المصر. من حيث هو شهر سيفه : أي سلّه وجرَّدَه من غمده

[٢]قوله : ولا في من شهر سلاحاً... الخ ؛ أي لا شيء على القاتل في هذه الصور لما ذكرنا في المسألة التي قبل هذا، دفع دخل مقدَّر ، وهو أن من الجائز أن يجوز قتله، وعليه الضمان كما في قتل الجمل الصائل، وأكل مال الغير حال المخمصة، فقال في دفعه : ولا في من شهر سلاحا ... الخ.
[٣] قوله : لأنه غير ملبث ؛ فيحتاج إلى دفعه بالقتل والعصاء الصغيرة وإن كان يلبث ولكن في الليل لا يلحقه الغوث، فيضطر إلى دفعه بالقتل، وكذا في النهار في غير المصر، لا يلحقه الغوث، فإذا قتله كان دمه هدراً ، قال الفقهاء : فإن كان عصاب عظمه لا تلبث، يحتمل أن يكون مثل السلاح عندهما، فيستوي فيه أن يُشَهرَ ليلاً أو نهاراً، وفي مصر أو في غير مصر.
[٤]قوله : لا يلحقه الغوث ... الخ ؛ الغوث : بالغين المعجمة كالنصر والعون وزناً
It is obligatory to kill one who draws a sword against Muslims, and there is nothing (of liability) for killing him. Nor is there anything for one who draws a weapon on a man by night or day in a city or elsewhere, or draws a stick on him by night in a city or by day elsewhere and the person against whom it is drawn kills him; there is nothing for killing him. If you say: Since he said 'killing such a person is obligatory', what is the need for his statement 'there is nothing for killing him'? I say: It is possible that killing him is obligatory to repel evil, yet something might be required for killing him. (Nor is there anything for one who draws a weapon on a man by night or day in a city or elsewhere, or draws a stick on him by night in a city or by day elsewhere and the person against whom it is drawn kills him). As for the weapon, when he draws it, there is nothing for killing him absolutely, because it does not cause delay in attacking. As for the stick, when he draws it by night in a city or by day elsewhere, there is nothing for killing him either, because although it causes delay, in the night in the city he may not get help, and likewise by day outside the city. [1] His statement: And there is nothing for killing him; meaning his blood is wasted, due to what al-Nasa'i narrated from Ibn Abi al-Zubayr and al-Hakim in al-Mustadrak from Ma'mar, marfu': 'Whoever draws his sword—against Muslims—his blood is wasted.' And because he is a rebel, his inviolability is nullified by his rebellion, and because it becomes a determined way to repel killing from himself, so he may kill him. [2] His statement: Nor in one who draws a weapon... etc.; meaning there is nothing on the killer in these cases due to what we mentioned in the previous question, as a rebuttal to a possible objection, which is that it is permissible to kill him, but he may be liable as in killing a rampaging camel or eating another's property in desperation. So he said in reply: nor in one who draws a weapon... etc. [3] His statement: Because it does not cause delay; so he needs to repel him by killing. And a small stick, although it causes delay, but at night he does not get help, so he is forced to kill him. Similarly by day outside the city, he does not get help, so if he kills him, his blood is wasted. The jurists said: If it is a bone-throwing weapon that does not cause delay, it is likely to be like a weapon according to them, so it is equal whether drawn by night or day, in a city or elsewhere. [4] His statement: He cannot get help... etc.; al-ghawth with the letter ghayn, like al-nasr and al-'awn in form.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the chapter of jinayat, establishing that a person who brandishes a sword against Muslims is liable to be killed, and the killer bears no liability for his blood. The commentator expands on the matn by explaining that this applies not only to swords but also to any weapon when drawn, and to sticks if drawn at night in a city or by day outside a city, because the victim is unlikely to receive help (ghawth). The ruling is supported by a hadith from al-Nasa'i and al-Hakim. The commentator also addresses a potential objection: why mention 'nothing for killing him' if killing is obligatory? The answer is to preclude the possibility that the killer might be liable for diya or qisas despite the obligation. The passage clarifies that the sword-brandisher is considered a rebel (baghi), so his inviolability ('ismah) is lost. The distinction between weapons and sticks is based on whether the attack is immediate (non-delaying) or delayed; if the stick is such that it does not delay (like a club that can strike quickly), it is treated like a weapon. The jurists also consider that a thick, blunt weapon might be similar. Ultimately, the principle is that when the killer has no alternative but to kill to defend himself, the blood of the aggressor is lawful.

Key Terms
شهر سيفاً (shahara sayfan): To draw a sword from its scabbard with the intent to attack or threaten.
هدر (hadar): Blood that is legally worthless, meaning the killer is not liable for qisas or diya.
غوث (ghawth): Help or rescue; the availability of assistance to the threatened person.
ملبث (mulabbath): Delaying; a weapon or object that does not cause immediate harm, allowing time for intervention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) When the stick is drawn in a city during the day
B) When the stick is drawn at night in a city
C) When the stick is drawn in a desert during the night
D) When the stick is drawn in a city at any time

Key Takeaway
Whoever draws a weapon against Muslims may be killed immediately without liability; for sticks, the same applies only when the victim cannot obtain help (ghawth).
