Option for Victim When Head Wound Covers Entire Head
Option for victim when head wound covers his whole head but not attacker's

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule when a head wound on the victim covers his entire head but the attacker's does not, and explain the reasoning based on disfigurement.

Core Idea
When equality in qisas is impossible due to disparity in wound extent, the victim may choose a proportionate wound or compensation.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in qisas for head wounds. While the general rule is that qisas requires exact similarity, the Hanafi school recognizes an exception when the victim's wound covers his whole head but the attacker's does not. The commentary explains that this disparity creates greater disfigurement for the victim, making literal equality unattainable. Consequently, the victim is given the option to either inflict a wound of equal size to his own (which would be smaller than his original) or accept monetary compensation (arsh).

Guided Path
1
State the general rule for shijaj
Recall that every shajjah must observe mumathala (exact similarity) in qisas, as stated in the matn.
The rule applies to all head wounds that expose the bone (mudihah) and similar.
2
Identify the exception condition
Note the condition: the victim's shajjah covers his entire head (area between temples), but the attacker's does not.
This condition creates a disparity in the extent of the wound.
3
Analyze the reasoning (talil)
Understand that the reason for the option is the greater disfigurement (shayn) suffered by the victim due to the larger wound. The commentary contrasts with limb cutting where shayn is uniform.
The reasoning uses a qiyas: limb cutting has no option because shayn does not vary.
4
Determine the victim's options
The victim may either execute qisas by inflicting a wound equal to his own (which is a smaller wound on the attacker) or take arsh (compensation).
The option is a choice; neither is forced.
5
Contrast with general rule of limb cutting
In limb amputation, the disfigurement and benefit are identical regardless of size, so only qisas is possible. This highlights the unique nature of head wounds.
This contrast strengthens the reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل شجة تراعى فيها المماثلة) : كالموضّحة : وهي أن يظهر العظم. ذلك بحديدة أو بغير حديدة، (ثم قال:) قوله : كلّ شجة؛ عطف على الرجل، اعلم أن الصغر والكبر لا يعتبر في المسألة السابقة، حتى أجرى القصاص في الكلّ، ويعتبرُ بالشجة في الرأس إذا كانت استوعبت رأس المشجوج، وهي لم تستوعب رأس الشاج، فأثبت للمشجوج الخيار إن اقتص وأخذ بقدر شجّته، وإن شاء أخذ أرش ذلك؛ لأنّ ما لحقه من الشين أكثر؛ لأنّ الشجة المستوعبة لما بين قرينته أكثر شيئاً من الشجّةِ التي لم تستوعب ما بين قرينته. بخلاف قطع العضو، فإنّ الشين فيه لا يختلف، وكذا منفعته لا تختلف، فلم يمكن إلا القصاص ؛ لوجود المساواة فيه من كلّ وجه
And every head wound (shajjah) must observe similarity: like the mudihah which exposes the bone, whether by iron or not. (Then he said:) His saying: 'Every shajjah' is an addition to the previous ruling. Know that smallness and largeness are not considered in the previous issue, so qisas is applied in all. But consider a shajjah on the head: if it covers the entire head of the victim but does not cover the entire head of the attacker, then the victim is given the option: either to execute qisas and take (a wound) equal to his own wound, or to take the arsh for it. This is because the victim suffers more disfigurement (shayn) since the wound covering the area between his two temples causes more disfigurement than a wound that does not cover that area. In contrast, in the cutting of a limb, the disfigurement does not differ, and likewise its benefit does not differ, so only qisas is possible because equality exists in every respect.
Source pages p. 450

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling on head wounds (shijaj) where the wound on the victim's head is extensive, covering the entire area between his temples, while the attacker's wound is smaller. The passage type is a ruling with proof, as it provides a reasoning based on degrees of disfigurement. The matn states that every shajjah must observe similarity (mumathala), but the commentary clarifies an exception: when the victim's wound covers his whole head but the attacker's does not, perfect similarity is impossible, and the victim has the option. The reasoning is that a wound covering the entire head causes more disfigurement (shayn) than a smaller wound. Therefore, if qisas is performed literally (giving the attacker a wound equal to the victim's), the attacker would receive a smaller wound, which does not match the victim's larger wound in terms of disfigurement. To avoid this, the victim can choose to take a wound on the attacker equal to his own wound (i.e., a small wound) or take monetary compensation (arsh). This is contrasted with limb amputation, where disfigurement is essentially the same regardless of size, so only qisas is possible. The ruling reflects the Hanafi emphasis on exact similarity in qisas, and when that is unattainable, the victim is given the choice.

Key Terms
شجة (shajjah): A head wound that reaches the bone (mudihah) or other head wounds.
المماثلة (al-mumathala): Exact similarity in qisas, meaning the retaliation must match the injury in kind and extent.
الشين (al-shayn): Disfigurement or blemish resulting from the wound.
الأرش (al-arsh): Monetary compensation for injuries where qisas is not applicable or not chosen.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only qisas is permitted
B) Only arsh is permitted
C) The victim may choose qisas (proportionate) or arsh
D) The victim must forgive

Key Takeaway
When a head wound on the victim covers his whole head but the attacker's does not, the Hanafi school gives the victim the option of proportional qisas or arsh due to the disparity in disfigurement.

Ruling on Ja'ifah (Wound Reaching the Cavity)
Ruling on ja'ifah with conditions for qisas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three possible outcomes for a ja'ifah wound regarding qisas and identify when qisas becomes wajib.

Core Idea
Qisas for ja'ifah depends on healing: if spreading, wajib; if healed, none; if unknown, wait.

This lesson addresses a specific type of wound called ja'ifah, which penetrates to the internal cavity. The Hanafi school distinguishes its ruling based on whether the wound heals or spreads, reflecting the principle that qisas is tied to the probability of death.

Guided Path
1
Define Ja'ifah
Identify ja'ifah as a wound that reaches the internal cavity, typically from chest or back, as defined by the commentator.
Excludes wounds on neck, throat, or limbs.
2
Identify the Three Scenarios
List the three possible states: (1) wound heals – no qisas; (2) wound is spreading (sariyah) – qisas wajib; (3) wound has not yet spread – wait until outcome is known.
Spreading (sariyah) indicates that the injury will likely cause death.
3
Apply the Ruling
Given a description of a ja'ifah wound, determine whether qisas applies based on its healing status.
The key factor is whether the wound is spreading; only then is qisas obligatory.

قوله : فإن الجائفة ; قال أخي جلبي : وهي " الطعنة التي تبلغ الجوف." واللسان والذكر إلا أن يقطع الحشفة، وطَرْفُ المسلم والدمي سواء، وخير المجني عليه إن كانت يد القاطع شلاء ، أو ناقصة بأصبع ، أو الشجةُ لا تستوعب ما بينَ قرني الساج، واستوعب ما بين قرني المشجوج يجري فيها القصاص ؛ لأنَّ البرء فيها نادر ، فالظاهرُ أَنَّ الثَّاني يفضي إلى الهلاك ، أما إذا لم تبرأ ، فإن كانت سارية يجب القصاص، فإن لم تسر بَعْدُ لا يقتص إلى أن يظهر الحال من البرء والسراية، واللّسانِ والذكر إلا أن يقطع الحشفة)، هذا عندنا ؛ لأن الانقباض والانبساط يجري فيهما فلا تراعى المماثلة، وعن أبي يوسف له : إن كان القطع من الأصل يقتص. وطَرْفُ المسلم والذمي سواء) ، وخير " المجني عليه إن كانت يد القاطع شلاء، أو ناقصة بأصبع، أو الشجةُ لا تستوعب ما بين قرني الساج، واستوعب ما بين قرني المشجوج) : أي شجّ رجل رجلاً موضحة حتى وجب القصاص، والشَّجَةُ طولها مقدار شبر ويؤيد هذا التفسير ما في «الغاية» حيث قال : وأما الجائفةُ وهي التي تصل البطن من الصدر أو الظهر، ولا يكون في الرقبة ولا في الحلق ولا في اليدين ولا في الرجلين. انتهى. أقول : لا ضرورة بالتأييد ؛ لأنّ الشارح الله فسّر الجائفة بهذا التفسير في «كتاب الصوم» حيث قال : الجائفة الجراحة التي بلغت الجوف. فتأمل. ( [١]قوله : إلا أن يقطع الحشفة ؛ أي يجب القصاص إن قطع الحشفة ؛ لأنّ موضعَ القطع معلوم كالمفصل، ولو قطع بعض الحشفة أو بعض الذكر فلا قصاص فيه ؛ لأنّ البعض لا يعلم مقداره بخلاف الإذن إذا قطع كلّه أو بعضه؛ لأنه لا ينقبض ولا ينبسط ، ولـه حـد معلوم ، فيمكن اعتبار المساواة، والشفّةُ إذا قطعت كلّهـا يجـب القـصـاص ؛ لإمكان اعتبار المساواة بخلاف قطع البعض ؛ لأنه يتعذر اعتبارها. [٢] قوله : سواء ؛ فيجب القصاص في الأطراف بين المسلم والكافر؛ للتساوي بينهما في الإرث. [٣] قوله وخير ؛ على صيغة المجهول ؛ أي خُيّر المجني عليه إن شاء قطع اليد المعيبة، ولا شيء له غير اليد المعيبة، وإن شاء أخذ الأرش كاملاً ؛ لأنَّ استيفاء الحق كاملاً مُتَعَذِّرٌ ، فله أن يعفو بدون حقه، وله أن يعدل إلى العوض، كالمثلي إذا لم يبق منه
His saying: 'As for the ja'ifah' – said Akhi Jilabi: It is a stab that reaches the cavity. And the tongue and penis, unless he cuts the glans (hashafah), and the limb of a Muslim and a dhimmi are equal, and the victim is given a choice: if the cutter's hand is paralyzed, or missing a finger, or the wound does not cover the entire area between the two horns of the saj, but covers that of the wounded person, then qisas applies in it; because healing is rare in it, so it is apparent that the second leads to death. But if it has not healed, if it is spreading (sariyah), qisas is wajib. If it has not spread yet, no qisas is carried out until the situation of healing or spreading becomes clear. And the tongue and the penis, unless he cuts the glans – this is according to us, because contraction and expansion occur in them, so equivalence is not considered. And from Abu Yusuf: if the cut is from the root, he performs qisas. And the limb of a Muslim and a dhimmi are equal. And the victim is given a choice: if the cutter's hand is paralyzed, or missing a finger, or the wound does not cover the entire area between the two horns of the saj, but covers that of the wounded one. That is: a man inflicted a wound (mudihah) on another until qisas became wajib, and the wound's length is about a span. This interpretation is supported by what is in al-Ghayah. He said: As for the ja'ifah, it is that which reaches the belly from the chest or back, and it is not in the neck, nor in the throat, nor in the hands, nor in the feet. End. I say: There is no need for support, because the commentator (may Allah have mercy on him) interpreted ja'ifah with this explanation in the Book of Fasting, saying: Ja'ifah is a wound that reaches the cavity. So reflect. [1] His saying: 'Unless he cuts the glans' – meaning qisas is wajib if he cuts the glans, because the place of cutting is known like a joint. If he cuts part of the glans or part of the penis, there is no qisas, because the quantity of that part is not known, unlike the ear if all or part of it is cut, because it does not contract or expand, and has a known limit, so equality can be considered. And the lip if all of it is cut, qisas is wajib because equality can be considered, unlike cutting part of it, because considering it is difficult. [2] His saying: 'Equal' – so qisas is wajib in limbs between Muslim and disbeliever, because of equality between them in inheritance. [3] His saying: 'And given choice' – in the passive form; i.e., the victim is given a choice: if he wishes, he cuts the defective hand, and he has nothing else except the defective hand; and if he wishes, he takes the full compensation (arsh), because complete extraction of the right is impossible, so he may forgive without his right, or he may turn to compensation, like the fungible when none remains.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage presents multiple rulings on qisas for specific injuries. For ja'ifah (a wound reaching the cavity), if it heals, no qisas; if it does not heal and is spreading (sariyah), qisas is wajib; if it has not spread yet, wait until healing or spreading is known. For the tongue and penis, qisas only applies if the entire glans (hashafah) is cut, because contraction and expansion prevent exact equivalence. Abu Yusuf holds that if cut from the root, qisas applies. For limbs, the tip (taraf) of a Muslim and a dhimmi are equal, so qisas applies between them. For the victim's choice: if the offender's hand is defective (paralyzed or missing a finger), or if the wound on the offender does not cover the same area as the victim's wound, the victim may choose to cut the defective limb or take full compensation (arsh). This is because exact reciprocity is impossible.

Key Terms
الجائفة (al-ja'ifah): A wound that reaches the internal cavity (jwf) of the body.
سارية (saariyah): A wound that spreads and threatens life, triggering qisas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling for a ja'ifah wound that is spreading (sariyah)?
A) Qisas is wajib
B) No qisas, only compensation
C) Qisas is delayed until healing
D) Qisas is never applicable

Key Takeaway
Qisas for ja'ifah is wajib only when the wound is spreading; otherwise, it is delayed or nullified by healing.

Ruling on Cutting the Tongue and Penis (with Intra-Hanafi Dispute)
Ruling on tongue/penis qisas with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school limits qisas for the tongue and penis to cutting the glans, and evaluate Abu Yusuf's opposing view.

Core Idea
Qisas for tongue/penis applies only if the glans (hashafah) is cut; partial cuts do not allow exact equivalence.

This lesson examines the unique ruling for the tongue and penis, where the Hanafi school (except Abu Yusuf) requires that the entire glans be cut for qisas to apply, due to the organs' ability to contract and expand, making exact reciprocity difficult for partial cuts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Organs and the Exception
Note that the tongue and penis are treated similarly: qisas applies only if the entire glans (hashafah) is cut.
Hashafah is the glans tip; for the tongue, it is the tip.
2
Understand the Reasoning
Because these organs contract and expand, partial cuts cannot be matched exactly; only a full cut of the glans has a known limit (like a joint).
Contrast with the ear, which does not contract, so partial cuts are possible.
3
Examine the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Abu Yusuf holds that if the cut is from the root, qisas applies, but the majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) limit it to the glans.
The passage sides with the majority ('indana').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : فإن الجائفة ; قال أخي جلبي : وهي " الطعنة التي تبلغ الجوف." واللسان والذكر إلا أن يقطع الحشفة، وطَرْفُ المسلم والدمي سواء، وخير المجني عليه إن كانت يد القاطع شلاء ، أو ناقصة بأصبع ، أو الشجةُ لا تستوعب ما بينَ قرني الساج، واستوعب ما بين قرني المشجوج يجري فيها القصاص ؛ لأنَّ البرء فيها نادر ، فالظاهرُ أَنَّ الثَّاني يفضي إلى الهلاك ، أما إذا لم تبرأ ، فإن كانت سارية يجب القصاص، فإن لم تسر بَعْدُ لا يقتص إلى أن يظهر الحال من البرء والسراية، واللّسانِ والذكر إلا أن يقطع الحشفة)، هذا عندنا ؛ لأن الانقباض والانبساط يجري فيهما فلا تراعى المماثلة، وعن أبي يوسف له : إن كان القطع من الأصل يقتص. وطَرْفُ المسلم والذمي سواء) ، وخير " المجني عليه إن كانت يد القاطع شلاء، أو ناقصة بأصبع، أو الشجةُ لا تستوعب ما بين قرني الساج، واستوعب ما بين قرني المشجوج) : أي شجّ رجل رجلاً موضحة حتى وجب القصاص، والشَّجَةُ طولها مقدار شبر ويؤيد هذا التفسير ما في «الغاية» حيث قال : وأما الجائفةُ وهي التي تصل البطن من الصدر أو الظهر، ولا يكون في الرقبة ولا في الحلق ولا في اليدين ولا في الرجلين. انتهى. أقول : لا ضرورة بالتأييد ؛ لأنّ الشارح الله فسّر الجائفة بهذا التفسير في «كتاب الصوم» حيث قال : الجائفة الجراحة التي بلغت الجوف. فتأمل. ( [١]قوله : إلا أن يقطع الحشفة ؛ أي يجب القصاص إن قطع الحشفة ؛ لأنّ موضعَ القطع معلوم كالمفصل، ولو قطع بعض الحشفة أو بعض الذكر فلا قصاص فيه ؛ لأنّ البعض لا يعلم مقداره بخلاف الإذن إذا قطع كلّه أو بعضه؛ لأنه لا ينقبض ولا ينبسط ، ولـه حـد معلوم ، فيمكن اعتبار المساواة، والشفّةُ إذا قطعت كلّهـا يجـب القـصـاص ؛ لإمكان اعتبار المساواة بخلاف قطع البعض ؛ لأنه يتعذر اعتبارها. [٢] قوله : سواء ؛ فيجب القصاص في الأطراف بين المسلم والكافر؛ للتساوي بينهما في الإرث. [٣] قوله وخير ؛ على صيغة المجهول ؛ أي خُيّر المجني عليه إن شاء قطع اليد المعيبة، ولا شيء له غير اليد المعيبة، وإن شاء أخذ الأرش كاملاً ؛ لأنَّ استيفاء الحق كاملاً مُتَعَذِّرٌ ، فله أن يعفو بدون حقه، وله أن يعدل إلى العوض، كالمثلي إذا لم يبق منه
His saying: 'As for the ja'ifah' – said Akhi Jilabi: It is a stab that reaches the cavity. And the tongue and penis, unless he cuts the glans (hashafah), and the limb of a Muslim and a dhimmi are equal, and the victim is given a choice: if the cutter's hand is paralyzed, or missing a finger, or the wound does not cover the entire area between the two horns of the saj, but covers that of the wounded person, then qisas applies in it; because healing is rare in it, so it is apparent that the second leads to death. But if it has not healed, if it is spreading (sariyah), qisas is wajib. If it has not spread yet, no qisas is carried out until the situation of healing or spreading becomes clear. And the tongue and the penis, unless he cuts the glans – this is according to us, because contraction and expansion occur in them, so equivalence is not considered. And from Abu Yusuf: if the cut is from the root, he performs qisas. And the limb of a Muslim and a dhimmi are equal. And the victim is given a choice: if the cutter's hand is paralyzed, or missing a finger, or the wound does not cover the entire area between the two horns of the saj, but covers that of the wounded one. That is: a man inflicted a wound (mudihah) on another until qisas became wajib, and the wound's length is about a span. This interpretation is supported by what is in al-Ghayah. He said: As for the ja'ifah, it is that which reaches the belly from the chest or back, and it is not in the neck, nor in the throat, nor in the hands, nor in the feet. End. I say: There is no need for support, because the commentator (may Allah have mercy on him) interpreted ja'ifah with this explanation in the Book of Fasting, saying: Ja'ifah is a wound that reaches the cavity. So reflect. [1] His saying: 'Unless he cuts the glans' – meaning qisas is wajib if he cuts the glans, because the place of cutting is known like a joint. If he cuts part of the glans or part of the penis, there is no qisas, because the quantity of that part is not known, unlike the ear if all or part of it is cut, because it does not contract or expand, and has a known limit, so equality can be considered. And the lip if all of it is cut, qisas is wajib because equality can be considered, unlike cutting part of it, because considering it is difficult. [2] His saying: 'Equal' – so qisas is wajib in limbs between Muslim and disbeliever, because of equality between them in inheritance. [3] His saying: 'And given choice' – in the passive form; i.e., the victim is given a choice: if he wishes, he cuts the defective hand, and he has nothing else except the defective hand; and if he wishes, he takes the full compensation (arsh), because complete extraction of the right is impossible, so he may forgive without his right, or he may turn to compensation, like the fungible when none remains.
Source pages p. 453

Commentary
This passage presents multiple rulings on qisas for specific injuries. For ja'ifah (a wound reaching the cavity), if it heals, no qisas; if it does not heal and is spreading (sariyah), qisas is wajib; if it has not spread yet, wait until healing or spreading is known. For the tongue and penis, qisas only applies if the entire glans (hashafah) is cut, because contraction and expansion prevent exact equivalence. Abu Yusuf holds that if cut from the root, qisas applies. For limbs, the tip (taraf) of a Muslim and a dhimmi are equal, so qisas applies between them. For the victim's choice: if the offender's hand is defective (paralyzed or missing a finger), or if the wound on the offender does not cover the same area as the victim's wound, the victim may choose to cut the defective limb or take full compensation (arsh). This is because exact reciprocity is impossible.

Key Terms
الحشفة (al-hashafah): The glans of the penis or the tip of the tongue; the specific part that must be cut for qisas to apply.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view, when does qisas apply for cutting the tongue?
A) When any part of the tongue is cut
B) Only when the entire tongue is cut from the root
C) Only when the glans (tip) of the tongue is cut
D) When the victim is a free person

Key Takeaway
Qisas for tongue and penis is wajib only if the entire glans is cut, due to the difficulty of exact equivalence for other cuts.

Equality of Muslim and Dhimmi Limbs for Qisas
Ruling on equality of Muslim and dhimmi limbs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that the Hanafi school holds equality between Muslim and dhimmi limbs for qisas and identify the basis for this ruling.

Core Idea
In qisas for limbs, the Muslim and dhimmi are equal: a Muslim may be executed for cutting a dhimmi's limb.

This lesson covers the Hanafi position that the tip (taraf) of a Muslim and a dhimmi (non-Muslim citizen) are equal for purposes of qisas. This means if a Muslim cuts off the hand of a dhimmi, qisas is wajib, just as if the victim were a Muslim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
The passage states that the limb (taraf) of a Muslim and a dhimmi are equal, so qisas applies between them.
This is a clear statement of equality.
2
Understand the Basis
The commentary explains that this equality is based on equality in inheritance: both Muslim and dhimmi can inherit from each other (in certain cases), so their limbs are treated equally.
The underlying principle is equal protection under the law.
3
Contrast with Other Schools
Note that some other schools may hold that a Muslim is not executed for killing a dhimmi, but the Hanafi school extends equality to limbs as well.
This is a distinctive Hanafi view.

Victim's Choice When Offender's Limb is Defective
Ruling on victim's choice when offender's limb is defective

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the ruling that the victim of a limb injury may choose to cut the defective limb of the offender or take full compensation (arsh).

Core Idea
When exact qisas is impossible due to defect, the victim chooses between retaliation on the defective limb or full compensation.

This lesson addresses a scenario where the offender's hand is defective (paralyzed or missing a finger) or the wound area does not match. In such cases, the victim is given a choice: either to cut the offender's defective hand (if possible) or to take full monetary compensation. This ensures justice when exact reciprocity is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Condition
The scenario: the offender's hand is paralyzed (shalla) or missing a finger, or the offender's wound does not cover the same area as the victim's wound.
Exact reciprocity is impossible because the offender's limb is defective.
2
State the Two Options
The victim may choose: (1) cut the defective hand as is, receiving no extra compensation, or (2) spare the offender and take full compensation (arsh).
The victim cannot take both the defective limb and the full compensation.
3
Understand the Rationale
Complete extraction of the right (exact qisas) is impossible due to the defect, so the victim may settle for compensation, similar to cases where a fungible good is lost.
This is a form of fair relief when the ideal punishment is not feasible.

For ja'ifah (a wound reaching the cavity), if it heals, no qisas; if it does not heal and is spreading (sariyah), qisas is wajib; if it has not spread yet, wait until healing or spreading is known. For the tongue and penis, qisas only applies if the entire glans (hashafah) is cut, because contraction and expansion prevent exact equivalence. Abu Yusuf holds that if cut from the root, qisas applies. For limbs, the tip (taraf) of a Muslim and a dhimmi are equal, so qisas applies between them. For the victim's choice: if the offender's hand is defective (paralyzed or missing a finger), or if the wound on the offender does not cover the same area as the victim's wound, the victim may choose to cut the defective limb or take full compensation (arsh). This is because exact reciprocity is impossible.

Key Terms
تخيير (takhyeer): The victim's choice between cutting the defective limb or taking full compensation.
أرش (arsh): Monetary compensation for bodily injury, equivalent to the value of the lost limb.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What triggers the victim's choice to take compensation instead of cutting the offender's limb?
A) The victim's own limb is defective
B) The offender's limb is defective
C) The wound is on the head
D) The victim is a minor

Key Takeaway
When exact qisas is impossible due to a defect in the offender's limb, the victim may choose to cut the defective limb or take full compensation.

Pardon for Injury: Accidental vs Intentional – Effect on the Estate
Ruling with proof on pardon for injury and its effect on the estate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on pardon for injury and explain why accidental and intentional injuries are treated differently with respect to the estate.

Core Idea
Pardon for intentional injury (qisas) is valid fully; for accidental (diyah), it is a bequest limited to one-third.

When a victim pardons an injury before death, the effect on the estate depends on whether the injury was accidental or intentional. Accidental injury creates a debt of blood-money (diyah), which is property, so the pardon is treated as a will and limited to one-third. Intentional injury obligates retaliation (qisas), which is not property, so the pardon is entirely effective. This lesson unpacks the legal distinction and the proof from the hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
Recognize that the text distinguishes between accidental injury (diyah) and intentional injury (qisas). Accidental gives rise to a property claim; intentional gives rise to retaliation which is not property.
The classification determines the application of estate rules.
2
Apply the property test
Because diyah is property, the heirs have a right to it, so the victim's pardon is treated as a bequest (wasiyyah) from one-third. Since qisas is not property, no heirs' right attaches, so the pardon is valid from the whole estate.
This is the core legal reasoning.
3
Examine the hadith proof
The hadith 'To leave your heirs wealthy…' establishes that the testator cannot freely dispose of property beyond one-third because the heirs' right to wealth attaches to property. This supports the ruling for accidental injury.
The hadith is cited as the basis for the exception to the usual rule that heirs' rights only arise after death.
4
Address the objection on the killer's share
Some raise the issue that the killer might be among the 'aabilah, making a bequest to him invalid, but the text clarifies that pardon is a fresh donation, not a bequest, so it is valid. The relied-upon position is that the pardon is valid entirely without deducting the killer's share.
This intra-Hanafi point is settled by al-Kifayah and al-Inayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If he pardons the injury or the amputation and what results from it, it is a pardon for the life; for accidental, from one-third of his wealth; for intentional, from all of it. (If the injury was accidental and he pardoned it, it is a pardon for the blood-money, so it is considered from one-third; because the blood-money is property, and the right of heirs attaches to it, so the pardon is a will and is valid from one-third. As for intentional, its consequence is retaliation, which is not property, so the right of heirs does not attach to it, so the pardon is valid entirely.)
Source pages p. 465

Commentary
This passage states a ruling about pardoning an injury. The text distinguishes between accidental and intentional injury. For accidental injury, the obligation is blood-money (diyah), which is property. When the victim pardons the diyah, it is treated as a will (wasiyyah) because the heirs have a right to the property; thus the pardon is effective only from one-third of the victim's estate. For intentional injury, the primary obligation is qisas (retaliation), which is not property. The heirs have no property right in it, so the victim's pardon is fully effective and does not require deduction from one-third. The commentary explains that the diyah is property and the right of heirs attaches to it based on the hadith: 'To leave your heirs wealthy is better than to leave them poor and begging.' The rational proof is that the right of heirs is via inheritance, and they only acquire rights after the death of the testator; however, the hadith establishes that the heirs' right should attach to property to prevent the testator from dissipating it. Qisas not being property, this consideration does not apply. The passage also addresses an objection: if the perpetrator is one of the 'aabilah, how can bequest to him be valid? The answer is that the victim did not make a bequest but simply pardoned after the cause of obligation, which is a new gift, and such a gift is valid to a killer, as analogized to an outright gift.

Key Terms
العفو (al-afw): Pardon or remission of a claim (diyah or qisas) by the victim before death.
الدية (al-diyah): Blood-money, financial compensation for homicide or injury.
القود (al-qawad): Retaliation (qisas) for intentional killing or injury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is pardon for accidental injury considered from one-third of the estate, but pardon for intentional injury is valid from the whole?
A) Because accidental injury requires diyah which is property, and heirs' right attaches; intentional requires qisas which is not property.
B) Because the victim's intention matters.
C) Because accidental injury is less severe.
D) Because the hadith only applies to accidental cases.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between pardon for accidental injury (diyah is property, limited to one-third) and intentional injury (qisas is not property, fully valid).

Testimony of Two Heirs That the Third Heir Forgave the Killer
Analysis of testimony and its effect when two heirs claim the third's forgiveness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the effect of testimony and self-acknowledgment in qisas cases, and apply the Hanafi rule that self-interested testimony is invalid but may effect an implicit pardon.

Core Idea
Testimony that benefits the testifiers is invalid, but it operates as their own pardon, converting their share of qisas to diya.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah examines a nuanced scenario in qisas law. When two heirs testify that the third heir forgave the killer, their testimony appears beneficial to themselves—if qisas becomes diya, they gain financially. The Hanafi ruling is that such testimony is invalid regarding the third, but it is treated as their own forgiveness. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning and the four possible scenarios based on confirmation or denial by the parties, illustrating the Hanafi use of istihsan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the claim
The victim has three sons. Two sons testify that the third son pardoned the killer. The killer may confirm or deny this testimony, and the third son may also confirm or deny.
The claim is about the third son's pardon, not about the testifiers' own right.
2
Recognize the self-interest in the testimony
The testifiers would benefit financially if qisas is converted to diya, because they would receive a share of the blood money. Under Hanafi law, testimony that brings a direct benefit to the witness is rejected.
This is a general principle: a witness cannot testify in a case where they stand to gain.
3
Treat the testimony as an implicit pardon from the testifiers
Even though the testimony is invalid regarding the third son, it is considered as the testifiers' own pardon (afw) of their share of qisas. Their statement that qisas has fallen is accepted against themselves.
This is why the ruling says the testimony is also a 'pardon from them'.
4
Analyze the four scenarios based on confirmation/denial
The text explores four rational possibilities: (1) Both killer and third son confirm the testimony; (2) Killer confirms, third denies; (3) Killer denies, third confirms; (4) Both deny. For each, determine the diya outcome.
The scenario where only the third son confirms raises a qiyas vs istihsan dispute.
5
Determine the diya distribution in each scenario
Use the rulings given in the passage to assign shares: if both confirm, full diya divided equally; if killer confirms alone, testifiers get two-thirds, third gets one-third; if third confirms alone (istihsan), testifiers get nothing, third gets one-third; if both deny, testifiers get nothing, third gets one-third.
The killer's acknowledgment or denial is a key factor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

والحاصل أن الوجوة منقسمة على أربعة أقسام على القسمة العقلية، وذلك إمّا يصدقهما المشهود عليه والقاتل جميعاً ، أو يكذبهما المشهود عليه ويصدقهما القاتل، أو على العكس، وهاهنا بحث مذكور في الشرح فلا حاجة إلى البيان.

And likewise, if a slave owned jointly by two men, one of whom is absent, is killed... The case: a man killed a man, and the victim had three sons. Two of them testified that the third had pardoned the killer. Their testimony is invalid, and it is considered a pardon from them, because by their testimony they bring benefit to themselves—namely, the conversion of qisas into property—so the testimony is invalid. As for the explanation that it is a pardon: because they claim that qisas has fallen, and their claim is valid regarding themselves. If the killer confirms them, then the diya is divided into three parts, meaning if he alone confirms them; because if he confirms them, he acknowledges two-thirds of the diya to them, so his acknowledgment is valid. However, the killer claims the right of the testified-against party (the third son) has fallen, but the testified-against denies it, so the killer's claim is not accepted, and he must pay the third's share. If the testified-against denies them, they get nothing, and the third gets one-third, meaning if the killer also denies them. This is because they have acknowledged against themselves the fall of qisas, so their acknowledgment is accepted, and they claim conversion of their share into property, which is not accepted without proof. The share of the testified-against is converted, but their claim of pardon on his behalf, while he denies, is like an initial pardon from them, as if they said: we have pardoned regarding the testified-against, because the fall of qisas is attributed to them. If the testified-against alone confirms them without the killer, the killer must pay one-third of the diya to the testified-against, due to the killer's acknowledgment for him. We only said meaning if the killer also denies because if the killer confirms them despite the testified-against's denial, the killer must pay a full diya divided into thirds among them. This is because by the testimony they acknowledged the fall of qisas and conversion of their share into property, and the killer acknowledged that upon verification of confirmation... Pardon from them: if the killer confirms them alone, each of them gets one-third of the diya; if he denies them, they get nothing, and the third gets one-third; if only the brother (the third) confirms them, he gets one-third. Thus it is mentioned in al-Hidayah, but there is some consideration: if testimony is meant in its true sense, it cannot exist without a claim, and the claimant is the killer, so how can the killer's denial be part of this case? If testimony is meant as mere information, the ruling of invalidity absolutely cannot be correct, since it is specific to when they deny, and among the divisions is when the brother confirms them, in which case the information is not invalid. The killer must pay one-third diya to them and one-third to the testified-against, because he did not acknowledge the fall of qisas; rather, qisas fell by another's acknowledgment. In summary, the possibilities are divided into four rational divisions: either the testified-against and the killer both confirm them, or the testified-against denies and the killer confirms, or the opposite. There is a discussion in the Sharh so no need to elaborate. [1] His statement: pardon from them; because by the testimony they claim that qisas has fallen, and their claim is considered regarding themselves. [2] His statement: the killer alone; and the testified-against denied them in their testimony about the third's pardon; he restricted it to this because if the killer and the testified-against both confirm them, the killer guarantees two-thirds of the diya to the two witnesses only; because they claimed property from the killer and the killer confirmed them, and he does not guarantee anything to the testified-against because he acknowledged the pardon by confirming the witnesses. Thus in al-Kifayah. [3] His statement: if only the brother confirms them, he gets one-third. He said in al-Inayah: this is istihsan, and by qiyas he should not owe anything, because what the witnesses claimed against the killer was not established due to his denial, and what the killer acknowledged for the testified-against was invalidated by his denial.
Source pages p. 475

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, a commentary on Sharh al-Wiqayah, deals with the law of retaliation (qisas) and testimony. It first presents a case of a jointly owned slave killed intentionally, where the absent co-owner's alleged pardon affects the present co-owner's right to claim qisas. The main focus, however, is a scenario where a murderer has three sons of the victim as heirs. Two of these sons testify that the third son forgave the killer. The ruling: their testimony regarding the third is invalid because they stand to benefit—if qisas is converted to diya, they gain financially. However, their statement is considered as their own forgiveness (afw) of their share of qisas, since they are effectively claiming that the right of qisas has fallen. The text then explores four possible scenarios based on whether the killer and/or the third son confirm or deny the testimony, detailing the resulting distribution of diya. A commentator notes a nuance in the wording of al-Hidayah, questioning how the killer's denial can be part of the case if testimony requires a claimant. Finally, a point from al-Inayah mentions that when only the third son confirms the testimony, the killer paying him one-third diya is based on istihsan (juristic preference), whereas strict qiyas would require proof of the claim, which is lacking. This illustrates the Hanafi method of preferring istihsan over qiyas in certain cases.

Key Terms
قود (qawad): Retaliation in kind for intentional homicide (qisas).
عفو (afw): Pardon or forgiveness that waives the right to qisas, converting it to diya.
شهادة (shahādah): Testimony given in court; here, it refers to witnesses stating that the third heir pardoned the killer.
استحسان (istihsān): Juristic preference; deviation from qiyas for a more equitable outcome.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where two sons testify that the third son pardoned the killer, and both the killer and the third son deny the testimony, what do the two testifying sons receive?
A) One-third of the diya each
B) Two-thirds of the diya between them
C) Nothing
D) The full diya

Key Takeaway
Self-interested testimony is invalid, but it acts as the witnesses' own pardon, and the diya distribution depends on who confirms or denies the claim, with istihsan sometimes overriding qiyas for fairness.

Liability Based on Time of Act: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability when status changes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle that liability in homicide is determined at the time of the causative act, and apply it to distinguish Abu Hanifa's position from that of the Sahibayn in cases of intervening apostasy or emancipation.

Core Idea
Liability for homicide is fixed at the moment of the causative act; subsequent changes in victim status do not affect the obligation.

This passage addresses a critical intra-Hanafi dispute: if a victim's status changes between the act (throwing an arrow) and the result (death), does liability attach? Abu Hanifa holds that the time of the act governs; the Sahibayn dissent. The ruling hinges on whether subsequent events can negate a liability already established by the act. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply the school's principles consistently.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The passage presents two scenarios: (1) throwing at a Muslim who apostatizes before the arrow hits; (2) throwing at a slave who is freed before the arrow hits. For each, note the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.
2
State Abu Hanifa's principle
Abu Hanifa's core principle: 'al-ramy' (the act) is determinative. Liability is based on the status at the time of the act, not the result. The commentary explains this: 'liability is by his act, which is throwing, since there is no act from him after.'
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's objection for the apostate case
The Sahibayn analogize apostasy to a release (ibra) after a wound: since the victim's inviolability (taqawwum) is removed by apostasy, the thrower is acquitted. They compare it to a victim who forgives after injury but before death.
Note: The opposing analogy treats apostasy as a supervening cause that extinguishes liability.
4
Analyze the dispute on the freed slave case
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold that full value (price at time of throwing) is due; Muhammad argues only the diminution in value caused by the injury. This reflects a subtler disagreement: does emancipation affect the measure of damages?
5
Understand why qisas is not due despite intent
A possible objection: if the act was intentional and liability is based on the act, why is qisas not obligatory? The passage answers: shubha (doubt) arises from considering the time of impact (the moment the arrow strikes), which introduces uncertainty about the victim's status at that later moment, thus blocking qisas.
This shows the school's care in balancing principles and avoiding harsh outcomes where doubt exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعبرة لحالة الرمي لا للوصول فتجب الدية على من رمى مسلماً فارتد فوصل هذا عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما : لا يجب شيء إذ بالارتداد سقط تقومه فصار مبرئاً للرامي عن موجبه، كما إذا أبرأه بعد الجرح قبل الموت ، له : أن المرمي إليه حالة الرمي متقوم، والقيمة لسيد عبد رمي إليه فأعتقه فوصل هذا عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف الله ، وقال محمد له : فضل ما بين قيمته مرمياً إلى غير مرمي.
[١] قوله : إن المرمي إليه... الخ ؛ يعني إنّ الضمان يجب بفعله، وهو الرمي، إذ لا فعل منه بعده ، فيعتبر حالة الرمي، والمرمي إليه حالة الرمي متقوّم. فإن قيل : إن كان ما ذكرتم بجميع مقدّماته صحيحاً، والفعل كان عمداً فينبغي أن يكون القصاص واجباً. قلنا : الفعل وإن كان عمداً فالقود يسقط بالشبهة الناشئة من اعتبار حالة الإصابة.
[٢] قوله : فضل ما بين قيمته مرمياً ... الخ ؛ يعني لو كان قيمته قبل الرمي ألف درهم، وبعده ثمانمئة ، يلزمه مئتا درهم.
The consideration is for the state at the time of throwing, not the time of reaching. So diya is due upon one who threw at a Muslim who then apostatized and then it reached—this is according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), nothing is due, because by apostasy his inviolability (taqawwum) fell, so it becomes an acquittal for the thrower from its consequence, like if he released him after the wound before death. His proof: the one thrown at at the time of throwing is inviolable. And the value (for a slave) is due to the master of a slave who threw at him and then he was freed and then it reached—this is according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf. Muhammad said: only the difference between his value as thrown-to and not thrown-to is due.
[1] His statement: 'the one thrown at at the time of throwing is inviolable' means liability is by his act, which is throwing, since there is no act from him after, so the state at throwing is considered, and the thrown-at at that time is inviolable. If it is said: if what you mentioned with all its premises is correct, and the act was intentional, then qisas should be obligatory. We say: although the act was intentional, qisas falls due to the doubt arising from considering the state at the time of impact.
[2] His statement: 'the difference between his value as thrown-to and not thrown-to' means if his value before throwing was 1000 dirhams, and after it 800, he owes 200 dirhams.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage deals with the effect of a change in the victim's status between the act of throwing (ramy) and the resulting injury/death. The key principle is that liability depends on the time of the act, not the result. Two cases are discussed: (1) Throwing at a Muslim who then apostatizes before the arrow hits: Abu Hanifa holds diya is due because at the time of the act the victim was a protected Muslim. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say nothing is due because apostasy removes protection, equivalent to a release after injury. (2) Throwing at a slave who is then freed before the arrow hits: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold the full price (value at time of throwing) is due to the master. Muhammad holds only the difference in value (before and after) is due. The commentary explains that the reasoning is that liability is based on the act of throwing (which is the cause), and since no further act occurs, the state at that time is determinative. The question of why qisas is not due even if intentional is answered: doubt (shubha) arises from considering the time of impact, which prevents qisas.

Key Terms
الرمي (al-ramy): The act of throwing (e.g., an arrow); the time of the act is the determinant of liability.
تقوم (taqawwum): Inviolability/legal protection; the state of being a protected person (Muslim, dhimmi, or slave).
شبهة (shubha): Doubt or ambiguity that affects legal rulings; here, it prevents qisas despite intentional act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling if a man shoots a Muslim who then apostatizes before the arrow hits?
A) No liability, because apostasy removes protection.
B) Diya is due because the act was against a protected Muslim.
C) Qisas is due because the act was intentional.
D) Only kaffarah is due.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi homicide jurisprudence, liability is fixed at the moment of the causative act; subsequent changes in the victim's legal status do not affect the obligation of diya or compensation.

The Principle of al-'Ibra li-Halati ar-Ramy: Liability Attaches at the Time of the Act
Ruling on liability based on shooting time, not injury time

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the principle that liability attaches at the time of the act (ramy), apply it to cases of apostasy and emancipation, and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreements.

Core Idea
Liability is fixed when the act is performed; later changes do not undo it.

This lesson examines a foundational Hanafi principle in criminal law (jinayat): the moment that determines liability is the time the harmful act is done, not when the injury reaches the victim. The passage presents two test cases—apostasy after shooting and emancipation of a slave after shooting—to illustrate how this principle plays out. You will see how the Hanafi school applies istihsan over qiyas and how the three imams disagree on certain details.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Principle
Locate the statement 'والعبرة لحالة الرمي لا للوصول' and understand that the legal effect (diya, value) is determined by the state of the victim at the time of shooting, not at the time the arrow hits. This is an istihsani departure from strict qiyas.
The principle applies in jinayat, but also appears in other areas (e.g., penalties for muhrim).
2
Analyze the Apostasy Case
The passage states that if a Muslim is shot and then apostatizes before the arrow reaches, full diya is due according to Abu Hanifa. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say nothing is due because apostasy removes inviolability. Examine the evidence for each view.
Abu Hanifa's argument: the victim was ma'sum at the time of the act. Sahibayn's analogy to pre-death discharge.
3
Analyze the Slave Emancipation Case
If a slave is shot and then freed before the arrow hits, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf hold full value is due to the master; Muhammad holds only the deficiency (difference between value as slave and value as free) is due. Understand the reasoning about sarayah and the nature of shooting vs. amputation.
Muhammad's view: emancipation severs the progression, similar to cutting a limb and then freeing.
4
Apply the Principle to the Muhrim Case
The passage gives a parallel: a muhrim who shoots game and then exits ihram before the arrow reaches must pay the penalty (jaza’); a non-muhrim who shoots and then enters ihram pays nothing. This reinforces that the act's time governs.
The same principle operates in ibadat context.
5
Derive the Mufta Bihi Position
Based on al-Hidayah and al-'Inaya, the relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa for both cases: full diya/value due. Some later authorities prefer Abu Yusuf's distinction for apostasy. Understand the tarjih mechanism.
The principle itself is zahir al-riwayah.

الشهادة من المشهود له، وهو الولي أيضاً، غير أن تكذيب المقرّ له المقر في بعض ما أقرَّ به لا يبطل إقراره في الباقي، وتكذيب المشهود له الشاهد في بعض ما شهد به يبطل شهادته أصلاً ؛ لأنّ التكذيب تفسيق ، وفسقُ الشاهد يمنع القبول، وأمّا فسق المقرّ لا يمنع صحة الإقرار. كذا في «الهداية)). دليل محمد : إن العتق قاطع للسراية ؛ لاشتباه من له الحق ؛ لأن المستحق حالَ ابتداء الجناية المولى، وحال الإصابة العبد ؛ لحريته ، فصار العتق بمنزلة البرء، كما إذا قطع يد عبد أو جرحه ثمّ أعتقه المولى ثمّ سرى ، فإن العتق لقطع السراية حتى لا يجب بعد العتق شيء من الدية والقيمة، وإنما يضمن النقصان. ولهما : إنّ الرامي يصير قاتلاً من وقت الرمي ؛ لأن فعله الرمي، والعبد مملواً في حالةِ الرمي، فيجب قيمته ؛ لأنّ الفعل انعقد من الابتداء موجباً للقيمة، فلا يتغير باعتراض الحرية، بخلاف القطع والجرح ؛ لأنه إتلافُ بعض المحل، وأنه يوجب، وأنه موجب الضمان للمولى ؛ لأنه ورد على محل مملوك. بعد السراية لو وجب شيء يوجب للعبد ، فتصير النهاية مخالفة للبداية ، أما الرمي قبل الإصابة ليس بإتلاف شيء؛ لأنه لا أثر للرمي في المحل، وإنما قلت الرغبات في العبد، فلا يجب به ضمان، فلا تتخالف النهاية والبداية، فيجب قيمته للمولى. قال في «العناية»: إن قولنا : إنّ الرامي يصير قاتلاً من وقت الرمي ظاهر على مذهب الأعظم له، فإنه يعتبر وقت الرمي. أما أبو يوسف له فيحتاج إلى الفرق بين هذه وبين ما إذا رمى مسلماً فارتد والعياذ بالله قبل الإصابة، حيث اعتبر هناك حالة الإصابة، وهاهنا حالة الرمي، وهو أن المرمي إليه خرج بالارتداد عن أن يكون معصوماً، المنهية «البيانية»: قد ذكر الفقيه أبو الليث قول أبي يوسف محمد له في مسألة الإعتاق بعد الرمي، وفخر الإسلام مع أبي حنيفة . والله أعلم. (والجزاء على محرم رمى صيداً فَحَل فوصل لا على حلال رماه فأحرم فوصل)
And the consideration is for the state of shooting, not for the reaching; so the blood-money is obligatory upon the one who shot a Muslim and then he apostatized and [the arrow] reached, and the value is for the master of a slave who was shot and then he freed him and [the arrow] reached. Because rejection [of testimony] is fisq, and in the first case there is rejection by the confestee of the confessor in part of what he confessed, which is his sole participation in the killing, and this does not invalidate the confession. ...[This] is the view of Abu Hanifa, and with the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad] nothing is obligatory because by apostasy his inviolability is lost, so it becomes a discharge for the shooter from his liability, like if he discharged him after the wound before death. For him: that the one shot at at the time of shooting is inviolable, and the value is for the master of a slave who was shot and then he freed him and [the arrow] reached. This is the view of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad said: the difference between his value when shot and when not shot. ... Evidence of Muhammad: that emancipation cuts off the progression because of uncertainty as to who has the right; because at the beginning of the injury the right belongs to the master, and at the time of reaching the right belongs to the slave due to his freedom, so emancipation becomes like healing... For both of them [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad]: that the shooter becomes a killer from the time of shooting because his act is shooting, and the slave is a slave at the time of shooting so his value is obligatory... In al-'Inaya: our statement that the shooter becomes a killer from the time of shooting is apparent according to the great imam, for he considers the time of shooting. As for Abu Yusuf, he needs to differentiate between this and the case where he shot a Muslim and he apostatized before injury, where he considered the state of injury, and here the state of shooting... And the penalty is upon the muhrim who shot game and then halal and it reached, not upon the halal who shot it and then he became muhrim and it reached.
Source pages p. 480

Commentary
This passage discusses the Hanafi principle that liability for homicide or injury is determined at the time of the act (shooting) rather than at the time the injury reaches the victim. The author begins by stating the ruling: blood-money (diya) is due on one who shoots a Muslim, even if the victim apostatizes before the arrow hits; and the full value (qimah) of a slave is due to his master if the slave is shot and then freed before the injury, because the value is measured at the time of the shooting. The reasoning is that the shooter commits the act when the arrow is launched, and the victim at that moment is protected (ma'sum) or owned, so the liability attaches then. When the victim later apostatizes or is freed, it does not negate the obligation that already arose.

An intra-Hanafi dispute is then presented: Abu Hanifa holds that full blood-money is due to a Muslim's heirs even if he apostatizes before injury, and the value of a slave is due even if freed before injury. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) differ: they say nothing is due if the victim apostatizes because his inviolability is lost, making the shooter free of liability; but in the slave case, Abu Yusuf agrees with Abu Hanifa (full value due), while Muhammad says only the deficiency in value (difference between value before shooting and after shooting) is due, because emancipation severs the progression of injury. The text clarifies that the Sahibayn's view in the apostasy case is based on analogy to a pre-death discharge, while Abu Hanifa's view emphasizes that the victim was inviolable at the time of the act.

A subtle point is made about the difference between iqrar (confession) and shahada (testimony): if a confestee rejects part of a confession, the rest remains valid, but if a witness's testimony is rejected by the one for whom he testifies, the entire testimony is invalid because rejection implies fisq (sinfulness), which disqualifies a witness but does not invalidate a confession. This is an important procedural distinction.

The passage also mentions a related case of a muhrim (person in ihram) who shoots game and then exits ihram before the arrow hits: he must pay penalty (jaza') because the act occurred while he was muhrim. Conversely, if a non-muhrim shoots game and then enters ihram before the arrow hits, no penalty is due because the act was done while he was halal. This reinforces the principle that the ruling follows the time of the act.

The authority of the rulings: The principle itself is from the Zahir al-Riwayah as transmitted in al-Hidayah. The views of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad are given with their evidence. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) appears to be that of Abu Hanifa in the apostasy case, but the text notes that Abu Yusuf's position is consistent except in the apostasy case where he considers the time of injury due to loss of inviolability.

Key Terms
العبرة لحالة الرمي لا للوصول (al-‘ibra li-ḥālati ar-ramy lā li-l-wuṣūl): The principle that legal liability is determined by the state at the time the act (shooting) is performed, not at the time the injury reaches the victim.
تكذيب (takdhīb): Rejection or denial of a claim or testimony; in this context, it causes fisq (sinfulness) and for a witness nullifies testimony, but for a confession it does not nullify the rest of the confession.
سراية (sarāyah): The progression of an injury from a wound to death; in criminal law, it refers to the causal chain from the act of wounding to the eventual death.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No blood-money is due because the victim lost inviolability.
B) Full blood-money is due because the victim was a Muslim at the time of shooting.
C) Only half blood-money is due because of uncertainty.
D) The shooter is free of liability due to apostasy.

Key Takeaway
Liability is fixed at the moment of the act; later changes in the victim's status do not affect the obligation that has already arisen.

Liability for Shooting a Slave Freed Before Death
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability when a slave is freed after injury but before death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on liability when a slave is shot and then freed before death, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad/Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Liability attaches at the act of shooting; freeing later does not alter it.

When a slave is shot and then freed before the wound kills, a key question arises: is liability determined at the moment of shooting or at the moment of death? This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute where Muhammad argues that freedom cuts off liability, while Abu Hanifa maintains that the shooter owes the full value from the time of shooting. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying Hanafi principles of causation and liability.

Guided Path
1
State the Issue
Identify the legal scenario: a person shoots a slave, the master frees the slave, then the wound kills. What is the shooter liable for?
2
Identify the Opposing Views
Muhammad (and some transmissions of Abu Yusuf) hold that emancipation cuts off progression like healing, so only reduction in value is owed. Abu Hanifa holds that full value is owed because liability attaches at the shooting.
The two views represent a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.
3
Analyze the Proofs
Muhammad's proof: analogy to cutting a hand then freeing – there, liability is only for the defect. Abu Hanifa's proof: shooting before injury is not destruction of anything; the act is the cause, and at that time the slave was owned, so full value is owed.
The key distinction: cutting is partial destruction; shooting has no immediate effect.
4
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical consequence: if the slave dies after freeing, Muhammad says no full value; Abu Hanifa says full value to the master.
This difference affects the amount and recipient of compensation.
5
Note the Mufta Bihi
The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's view, as stated in al-'Inayah and affirmed by Fakhr al-Islam.
This is the operative fatwa in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل محمد : إن العتق قاطع للسراية ؛ لاشتباه من له الحق ؛ لأن المستحق حالَ ابتداء الجناية المولى، وحال الإصابة العبد ؛ لحريته ، فصار العتق بمنزلة البرء، كما إذا قطع يد عبد أو جرحه ثمّ أعتقه المولى ثمّ سرى ، فإن العتق لقطع السراية حتى لا يجب بعد العتق شيء من الدية والقيمة، وإنما يضمن النقصان.
ولهما : إنّ الرامي يصير قاتلاً من وقت الرمي ؛ لأن فعله الرمي، والعبد مملواً في حالةِ الرمي، فيجب قيمته ؛ لأنّ الفعل انعقد من الابتداء موجباً للقيمة، فلا يتغير باعتراض الحرية، بخلاف القطع والجرح ؛ لأنه إتلافُ بعض المحل، وأنه يوجب، وأنه موجب الضمان للمولى ؛ لأنه ورد على محل مملوك.
بعد السراية لو وجب شيء يوجب للعبد ، فتصير النهاية مخالفة للبداية ، أما الرمي قبل الإصابة ليس بإتلاف شيء؛ لأنه لا أثر للرمي في المحل، وإنما قلت الرغبات في العبد، فلا يجب به ضمان، فلا تتخالف النهاية والبداية، فيجب قيمته للمولى. قال في «العناية»: إن قولنا : إنّ الرامي يصير قاتلاً من وقت الرمي ظاهر على مذهب الأعظم له، فإنه يعتبر وقت الرمي. أما أبو يوسف له فيحتاج إلى الفرق بين هذه وبين ما إذا رمى مسلماً فارتد والعياذ بالله قبل الإصابة، حيث اعتبر هناك حالة الإصابة، وهاهنا حالة الرمي، وهو أن المرمي إليه خرج بالارتداد عن أن يكون معصوماً، والزمان يعتمد صفة العصمة، فلا يجب الضمان بالمنافي.
وأما الإعتاق فإنّه لا ينافي العصمة، فيجب عليه قيمته للمولى، ومن هذا يعلم أنّ أبا يوسف الله يعتبرُ وقت الرمي إلا في صورة الارتداد، وقال أخي جلبي له في المنهية «البيانية»: قد ذكر الفقيه أبو الليث قول أبي يوسف محمد له في مسألة الإعتاق بعد الرمي، وفخر الإسلام مع أبي حنيفة . والله أعلم.
Muhammad's proof: Emancipation cuts off the progression (of the wound) due to confusion over who has the right; because the one entitled at the beginning of the offense is the master, and at the time of injury is the slave due to his freedom, so emancipation becomes like healing. Similarly, if one cuts off a slave's hand or wounds him, then the master emancipates him, then it spreads, then emancipation cuts off the progression so that nothing of blood-money or value is owed after emancipation, but only the defect is compensated. And for them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): The shooter becomes a killer from the time of shooting, because his act is the shooting, and the slave is owned at the time of shooting, so his value is owed; because the act from the beginning necessitates the value, so it is not changed by the intervention of freedom, unlike cutting and wounding; because that is destruction of part of the subject, and it necessitates guarantee to the master because it occurred upon owned property. If after progression something were owed to the slave, then the end would contradict the beginning. But shooting before injury is not destruction of anything, because the shooting has no effect on the subject, and desires for the slave have decreased, so no guarantee is owed, so the end does not contradict the beginning, so his value is owed to the master. He said in al-'Inayah: Our statement that the shooter becomes a killer from the time of shooting is apparent according to the doctrine of the greatest imam (Abu Hanifa), for he considers the time of shooting. As for Abu Yusuf, he needs to differentiate between this and the case where one shoots a Muslim and he apostatizes (God forbid) before injury, where there he considered the time of injury, and here the time of shooting; this is because the target has left by apostasy from being inviolable, and the time depends on the attribute of inviolability, so no guarantee is owed due to the contradiction. And as for emancipation, it does not negate inviolability, so he owes his value to the master. From this it is known that Abu Yusuf considers the time of shooting except in the case of apostasy. And Akhi Jalabi said in al-Munyah al-Bayaniyyah: The faqih Abu al-Layth mentioned the statement of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad in the issue of emancipation after shooting, and Fakhr al-Islam is with Abu Hanifa. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 481

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding liability when a person shoots a slave, and the slave is then emancipated before the wound causes death. The issue concerns whether the liability is determined at the time of shooting or at the time of death. Muhammad argues that freedom cuts off the progression of liability, similar to healing, so the shooter owes only the reduction in value. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the two) argue that the shooter becomes liable from the moment of shooting because the act is the shooting, and since the slave was owned then, the full value is owed. The commentator from al-'Inayah clarifies that Abu Hanifa considers the time of shooting, while Abu Yusuf considers the time of injury except in apostasy cases. The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa, that the shooter owes the full value because the act is the shooting.

Key Terms
سراية (sirāyah): The progression of a wound that leads to death after the initial injury
عتق (ʿitq): Emancipation of a slave, which changes legal status from owned to free
نقصان (nuqsān): Defect or reduction in value of the slave due to injury

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the dispute about shooting a slave who is then freed before death, what is the mufta bihi position?
A) The shooter owes nothing because freedom cuts off liability
B) The shooter owes the full value of the slave because liability attaches at the time of shooting
C) The shooter owes the reduction in value only
D) The shooter owes only blood-money

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's view, that the shooter owes the full value because liability attaches at the time of shooting, is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.

Liability Rules for Traffic Accidents in Hanafi Fiqh
Modern statement of five liability rules for traffic accidents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the five Hanafi rules of direct actor (mubashir) and indirect cause (musabbib) to determine liability in traffic accident scenarios.

Core Idea
The direct actor is generally liable unless the indirect cause is the transgressor.

Traffic accidents are a modern issue, but their liability rules are rooted in classical Hanafi jurisprudence. This lesson presents five clear rules derived by Mufti Taqi Uthmani from the works of Hanafi jurists, distinguishing between the direct actor (the driver) and the indirect cause (e.g., a pedestrian). Understanding these rules will help you analyze who bears responsibility for damages.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties involved
Determine who is the direct actor (mubashir) and who is the indirect cause (musabbib). In a traffic accident, the driver is typically the mubashir, while a pedestrian or another driver who triggered the event is the musabbib.
2
Assess transgression (ta'addi)
Decide whether each party is transgressing, i.e., doing an act that is inherently prohibited (like running a red light) or not. Note that the driver may be non-transgressing if driving properly.
Transgression here means doing something prohibited, not just being at fault.
3
Apply the five rules
Match the scenario to one of the five rules based on who is transgressing. The rules dictate that if both are non-transgressing, or only the driver is transgressing, or both are transgressing, the driver (mubashir) is liable. Only when the cause is transgressing and the driver is not does liability shift to the cause.
The rules are hierarchical; always check the last rule (rule 5) as an exception.
4
Determine liability
State clearly who must pay damages (daman) based on the rule that applies. The liable party must compensate for the harm caused.
The driver is almost always liable unless a clear transgression by the cause overrides.
5
Distinguish from animal cases
Note that a vehicle is under human control, unlike an animal that moves independently. Therefore, the driver's liability is stricter because he can control the vehicle at all times.
This distinction prevents applying animal liability rules to modern vehicles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويدخل في الجنايات حوادث المرور، وقد أفاض في تفصيله قواعدها شيخنا العلامة محمد تقي الدين العثماني حفظه الله في بحوث في قضايا فقهية معاصرة» (ص 310 - 319)، فأوجز بعض ما قال فأقول : ضوابط الضمان المتعلقة بحوادث السير مستخلصة من كتب الفقهاء: 1. إذا كان المباشر هو السبب الوحيد في الإتلاف ، فهو ضامن سواء كان متعدياً، أو غير متعد، بمعنى أنه لم يفعل فعلاً محظوراً في نفسه. 2. إذا اجتمع المباشر والمسبب، وليس أحداً منهما متعدّيا بالمعنى المذكور فالضمان على المباشر. 3. إذا اجتمع المباشر والمسبب، والمباشر متعد والمسبب غير متعد، فالضمان على المباشر. 4. إذا اجتمع المباشر والمسبب، وكل واحد منهما متعدّ، فالضمان على المباشر. 5. إذا اجتمع المباشر والمسبب، والمسبب متعدّ، والمباشر غير متعدّ، فالضمان على المسبب. فالأصل أن سائق السيارة مسؤول عن كل ما يحدث بسيارته خلال تسييره إياها، وذلك لأن السيارة آلة في يده وهو يقدر على ضبطها، فكل ما ينشأ عن السيارة، فإنه مسؤول عنه، وهناك فرق بين الدابة والسيارة من حيث إن الدابة متحركة بنفسها بخلاف السيارة.
And included in the chapter of criminal offenses are traffic accidents, and our master, the scholar Muhammad Taqi al-Uthmani (may Allah preserve him) has elaborated on their rules in 'Discussions on Contemporary Fiqh Issues' (pp. 310-319). So summarizing some of what he said, I say: The principles of liability related to traffic accidents extracted from the books of jurists: 1. If the direct actor (mubashir) is the sole cause of destruction, he is liable whether he was transgressing or not, meaning he did not do an act that is inherently prohibited. 2. If a direct actor and a cause (musabbib) combine, and neither is transgressing in the aforementioned sense, liability is on the direct actor. 3. If they combine, and the direct actor is transgressing while the cause is not, liability is on the direct actor. 4. If they combine, and both are transgressing, liability is on the direct actor. 5. If they combine, and the cause is transgressing while the direct actor is not, liability is on the cause. The principle is that the driver of a vehicle is responsible for everything that occurs through his vehicle while he is driving it, because the vehicle is a tool in his hand and he can control it; everything that arises from the vehicle, he is responsible for. There is a difference between an animal and a vehicle in that the animal moves by itself, unlike the vehicle.
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This is a modern addition by the commentator, outlining principles of liability for traffic accidents derived from classical Hanafi jurisprudence. It sets out rules for when the direct actor (the person physically causing the harm) and the cause (the person indirectly responsible) are both involved. The key lesson is that in traffic accidents, the driver is considered the direct actor and is liable for all harm caused by his vehicle unless a more powerful cause (like a pedestrian's transgression) overrides. The distinction from an animal is important: a vehicle is under human control, whereas an animal may act independently.

Key Terms
مباشر (mubāshir): The direct actor; the one who physically causes the harm, e.g., the driver
مسبب (musabbib): The indirect cause; one who sets in motion a chain of events leading to harm
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability; compensation for harm caused

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the traffic accident rules, if a driver hits a pedestrian who suddenly jumped in front of the car (the driver is not transgressing, the pedestrian is), who is liable?
A) The driver, because he is the direct actor
B) The pedestrian, because he is the transgressing cause
C) Both are equally liable
D) No one is liable

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi traffic accident liability, the driver is presumptively liable unless the indirect cause (e.g., pedestrian) acts transgressively.

Ruling on Game Shot by a Muslim Who Apostatizes
Ruling with proof using analogy and principled reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on game shot by a Muslim who apostatizes before the arrow hits, and explain why the state at shooting is decisive.

Core Idea
The permissibility of game meat depends on the shooter's state at the moment of shooting, not at the moment of striking.

In this lesson, we examine a ruling from Al-Hidayah regarding game hunting when the hunter changes religious status between shooting and impact. The Hanafi school emphasizes that the act of shooting is equivalent to slaughter (dhakah), so the relevant moment for assessing eligibility is when the arrow is released, not when it strikes. This principle is reinforced by an analogy with the state of Ihram.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
The text presents two scenarios: a Muslim shoots game then becomes Magian, and a Magian shoots game then becomes Muslim.
2
State the ruling for each case
The meat is halal in the first case and not halal in the second.
Contrary to what one might assume based on the final state.
3
Extract the key principle
The commentator states: 'because the consideration is the state at the time of shooting' (al-mu'tabar hal al-ramy).
This is the core legal maxim.
4
Analyze the analogy with Ihram
The analogy: a Muhrim who shoots game is liable (dam), but a Halal person who shoots then enters Ihram is not. This shows the decisive moment is when the direct act (shooting) occurs.
The analogy reinforces the same principle.
5
Understand the rationale
Shooting is the direct cause of death (mubasharah) and constitutes dhakah. Therefore, the shooter's eligibility at that moment determines the meat's permissibility.
This is a matter of causation and legal capacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حسن الدراية لأواخر شرح الوقاية
ولا يضمن من رمى مقضياً عليه برجم فرجع شاهده فوصل وحل صيد رماه مسلم فتمجس نعوذ بالله فوصل ، لا ما رماه مجوسي فأسلم فوصل ولا يضمن من رمى مقضياً عليه برجم فرجعَ شاهده فوصل وحل صيد رماه مسلم فتمجس نعود بالله فوصل ، لا ما رماه مجوسي فأسلم فوصل)؛ لأن المعتبر حالة الرمي. ثم خرج من الإحرام، فوقع الرمي بالصيد، فعليه الجزاء.
لو رمي حلال صيداً ثم أحرم فلا شيء عليه ; لأنّ الضمان إنما يجب بالتعدي وهو رميه في حالة الإحرام، وفي الأول هو محرم وقت الرمي، وفي الثاني حلال; فلذا افترقا في الحكم. كذا في «الهداية»).
(1)
[1] قوله : وحلّ صيد رماه مسلم ...الخ ; صورته : إن رمى مسلم صيداً فتمجس; أي صار مجوسياً العياذ بالله، فوصل حل أكله ، وإن رماه مجوسي فأسلم لا يؤكل ; لأنّ المعتبر حال الرمي في حق الحل والحرمة ، إذ الرمي هو الذكاة، فتعتبر الأهلية وانسلابها عند الرمي. كذا في «الهداية»(۲) (۳)
And it is not liable for the one who threw (an arrow) at a person sentenced to stoning, and his witness returned and it reached, and (the meat of) game that a Muslim shot, then he became Magian (may Allah protect us), and it reached, is lawful; not what a Magian shot, then he became Muslim, and it reached. ... because the consideration is the state at the time of shooting. Then he left the state of Ihram, and the shooting fell upon the game, so upon him is the penalty. If a non-Muhrim shot game and then entered Ihram, then nothing is upon him; because liability is only due from transgression, and his shooting was in the state of Ihram; in the first, he is a Muhrim at the time of shooting, in the second, he is Halal; thus they differ in the ruling. Thus in Al-Hidayah. (1) He said: 'And the game that a Muslim shot becomes lawful...' its example: If a Muslim shot a game and then became Magian (i.e., became Magian, refuge with Allah), and it reached, its eating is lawful; and if a Magian shot it and then became Muslim, it is not eaten; because the consideration is the state at the time of shooting with respect to lawfulness and prohibition, since shooting is the slaughter (dhabh), so eligibility and its removal are considered at the time of shooting. Thus in Al-Hidayah (2) (3).
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage from the commentary of Al-Hidayah addresses two rulings combined. First, the case of a person sentenced to death by stoning: if a witness retracts after the stones are thrown, the thrower is not liable because the retraction came after the act. Second, the main topic: game hunting. The principle is that the permissibility of eating game depends on the religious status of the hunter at the moment of shooting (which is the act of dhabh), not at the moment the arrow hits. Thus, if a Muslim shoots and then becomes a Majusi (Magian) before hitting, the meat is halal. Conversely, if a Majusi shoots and then converts to Islam, the meat is not halal. The author reinforces this by analogy with Ihram: a Muhrim who shoots game is liable (dam), but a Halal person who shoots and then enters Ihram is not, because liability only occurs if the shooting was done while in a state that prohibits hunting.

Key Terms
الذكاة (al-dhakāh): The proper Islamic method of slaughter; here, the act of shooting is considered as dhakah for game.
تمجس (tamajjasa): Became a Magian (Zoroastrian); apostasy from Islam.
المباشر (al-mubāshir): The direct actor causing the death; here, the thrower of the arrow is the direct cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A Muslim shoots an arrow at a deer, then becomes a Magian (apostate) before the arrow hits the deer. Is the deer's meat halal?
A) Yes, because the Muslim state at shooting is considered.
B) No, because he apostatized before the kill.
C) Yes, because the arrow was released in a lawful manner.
D) No, because the act of killing is completed in apostasy.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of game meat depends on the shooter's religious status at the moment of shooting, not at the moment of death, because shooting is considered the act of slaughter (dhakah).
