Liability in Traffic Accidents: Rules of Direct and Indirect Causation
Principles of driver liability derived from Hanafi sources

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules of mubashir and musabbib to determine liability in traffic accident scenarios, including identifying the four exceptions where the driver is not liable.

Core Idea
The driver is liable as direct actor unless an overwhelming external factor breaks the chain of causation.

Modern traffic accidents present complex questions of liability. Hanafi fiqh, through the principles of direct causation (mubasharah) and indirect causation (tasabbub), provides a clear framework. In this lesson, we examine the rules extracted by Mufti Muhammad Taqi Uthmani from the classical sources, which prioritize the direct actor's liability unless the indirect causer is the sole transgressor. We then apply these rules to the driver-vehicle relationship and explore four scenarios where the driver's liability is lifted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the direct actor and indirect causer
In any accident, determine who is the immediate physical cause (mubashir) and who set the stage (musabbib). For a moving car, the driver is the mubashir.
2
Check for transgression (ta'addi)
Examine if either party violated a legal prohibition (e.g., traffic rules). Violation makes one a transgressor (muta'addi).
3
Apply the five rules
Using the combinations of mubashir/musabbib and transgression, assign liability per the five rules. The mubashir is liable except when the musabbib is the only transgressor.
4
Check the four exceptions
Even if the driver is normally liable, liability is removed if his control is overridden by an external force (third-party push, rear-end collision, sudden mechanical failure, or unforeseeable pedestrian jump).
5
Conclude on liability
Synthesize the findings: if the driver's direct causation is uninterrupted and no exception applies, he is liable. Otherwise, liability shifts to the indirect causer or is voided.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويدخل في الجنايات حوادث المرور، وقد أفاض في تفصيله قواعدها شيخنا العلامة محمد تقي الدين العثماني حفظه الله في بحوث في قضايا فقهية معاصرة» (ص ۳۱۰ - ۳۱۹)، فأوجز بعض ما قال فأقول : ضوابط الضمان المتعلقة بحوادث السير مستخلصة من كتب الفقهاء: 1. إذا كان المباشر هو السبب الوحيد في الإتلاف ، فهو ضامن سواء كان متعدياً، أو غير متعد، بمعنى أنه لم يفعل فعلاً محظوراً في نفسه. . إذا اجتمع المباشر والمسبب، وليس أحداً منهما متعدّيا بالمعنى المذكور فالضمان على المباشر. . إذا اجتمع المباشر والمسبب، والمباشر متعد والمسبب غير متعد، فالضمان على المباشر. . إذا اجتمع المباشر والمسبب، وكل واحد منهما متعدّ، فالضمان على المباشر. ه. إذا اجتمع المباشر والمسبب، والمسبب متعدّ، والمباشر غير متعدّ، فالضمان على المسبب. فالأصل أن سائق السيارة مسؤول عن كل ما يحدث بسيارته خلال تسييره إياها، وذلك لأن السيارة آلة في يده وهو يقدر على ضبطها، فكل ما ينشأ عن السيارة، فإنه مسؤول عنه ، وهناك فرق بين الدابة والسيارة من حيث إن الدابة متحركة بنفسها بخلاف السيارة.

وعليه فالأصل أن السائق ضامن لكل ضرر ينشأ من عجلاتها أو مقدمتها أو من خلفها أو من أحد جانبيها ; لأن السيارة آلة محضة في يد السائق ، فتنسب مباشرة الاضرار إليه. فإن كان متعدياً بمخالفة قواعد المرور كأن يسوق بسرعة غير معتادة، فلا خفاء في كونه ضامناً; لأن الضرر نشأ بتعديه، والمتعدي ضامن في كل حال. أما إذا لم يكن متعديا في السير، بأن ساق سيارته ملتزماً بجميع قواعد المرور، فإنه يضمن الضرر الذي باشره، ويجب لتضمينه أن تصح نسبة المباشرة إليه بدون مزاحم على وجه معقول، على هذا الأساس لا يضمن في الصور الآتية : ١. إذا كان السائق يسوق سيارته ملتزما بجميع قواعد المرور ، ولكن دفع شخص رجلاً آخر أمام سيارته فجأة بحيث لم يمكن له أن يوقف السيارة قبل أن تدهسه. . إذا أوقف السائق سيارته أمام إشارة المرور منتظراً إشارة فتح الطريق فصدمته سيارة من خلفه ودفعته إلى الأمام فصدمت سيارته أحداً ، فليس الضمان على سائق السيارة الأمامية، بل الضمان على سائق السيارة التي صدمتها من خلفها ; لأنه لا تصح نسبة المباشرة إلى السيارة الأمامية، فإنها مدفوعة بمنزلة الآلة للسيارة الخلفية. إذا كانت السيارة سليمة قبل السير بها، وكان السائق يتعهدها تعهداً معروفاً، ثم طرأ علهيا خلل مفاجئ في جهاز من أجهزتها، حتى خرجت السيارة من قدرة السائق ومكنته من ضبطها، فصدمت إنسانا. .{ إذا ساق إنسان سيارة في شارع عام ملتزما السرعة المقررة ، ومتبعاً خط السير حسب النظام، ومتبصراً في سوقه حسب قواعد السير، فقفز رجل أمامه فجأة ; إذ كان بقرب منها بحيث لا يمكن رها المعتاد في مثل ذلك المكان أن تتوقف بالفرملة، وكان قفزه لا يتوقع مسبقاً لدى للسيارة في سيره سائق متبصر محتاط ، فصدمته السيارة رغم قيام السائق بما وجب عليه من الفرملة ونحوها.
And traffic accidents enter into the book of jinayat (offenses). Our shaykh, the scholar Muhammad Taqi al-Uthmani (may Allah preserve him) has thoroughly detailed their rules in his research 'Contemporary Fiqh Issues' (pp. 310-319). I summarize some of what he said as follows: The rules of liability related to traffic accidents extracted from the books of the jurists: 1. If the direct actor (mubashir) is the sole cause of destruction, he is liable whether he is transgressing or not, meaning he did not do an act prohibited in itself. 2. If the direct actor and indirect causer (musabbib) are both present, and neither is transgressing in the mentioned sense, liability is on the direct actor. 3. If both are present, and the direct actor is transgressing but the indirect causer is not, liability is on the direct actor. 4. If both are present, and each one is transgressing, liability is on the direct actor. 5. If both are present, and the indirect causer is transgressing but the direct actor is not, liability is on the indirect causer. The basic principle is that the driver is responsible for everything that happens with his car during his driving, because the car is a tool in his hand and he is able to control it. So everything that arises from the car, he is responsible for. There is a difference between an animal and a car because an animal moves by itself, unlike a car. Accordingly, the basic principle is that the driver is liable for any harm arising from its wheels or its front or from its back or from either of its sides, because the car is a pure tool in the driver's hand, so direct causation of harm is attributed to him. If he is transgressing by violating traffic rules, such as driving at an unusual speed, there is no hiding the fact that he is liable, because the harm arose from his transgression, and the transgressor is liable in every case. But if he is not transgressing in driving, i.e., he drove his car complying with all traffic rules, then he is liable for the harm he directly caused, but for him to be liable, the attribution of direct causation to him must be valid without interference in a reasonable manner. On this basis, he is not liable in the following scenarios: 1. If the driver is driving his car complying with all traffic rules, but a person suddenly pushes another person in front of his car such that he cannot stop the car before running over him. 2. If the driver stops his car at a traffic signal waiting for the green light, and a car from behind hits him and pushes him forward, and his car then hits someone. In that case, liability is on the driver of the car that hit him from behind, not on the front car driver, because direct causation cannot be attributed to the front car, as it is pushed and is like a tool for the rear car. 3. If the car was sound before driving, and the driver maintained it with known maintenance, and then a sudden malfunction in one of its parts occurred, such that the car went out of the driver's control and he was unable to control it, and it hit a person. 4. If a person drives a car in a public street complying with the prescribed speed, following the lane according to regulations, and being cautious in his driving according to traffic rules, and a man suddenly jumps in front of him while he is near the car such that even with the usual braking in such a place it cannot stop, and his jump was not foreseeable beforehand by a cautious driver, and the car hits him despite the driver doing what was required of him such as braking etc.
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This section is an addition by the editor of the book, summarizing the rules of liability for traffic accidents from the writings of Mufti Muhammad Taqi Uthmani. It first presents five general rules derived from Hanafi fiqh regarding the interplay between direct causation (mubasharah) and indirect causation (tasabbub). The primary rule is that the direct actor (mubashir) is usually liable, except when the indirect causer (musabbib) is the transgressor and the direct actor is not. Applying this to driving, the rule is that the driver is the direct actor with his vehicle as a tool. However, liability is removed in four specific scenarios where the driver's control is overridden by external factors: a third party pushing a victim in front of the car, a rear-end collision pushing the driver's car into someone, a sudden mechanical failure, and a pedestrian unexpectedly jumping in front of the car such that stopping is impossible despite due care.

Key Terms
المباشر (al-mubāshir): The direct actor; here, the driver who directly causes harm through his vehicle.
المسبب (al-musabbib): The indirect causer; the one who creates a situation leading to harm without direct action.
الضمان (al-ḍamān): Financial liability to compensate for harm caused.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A driver is stopped at a red light. A car hits him from behind, pushing his car forward into a pedestrian. Who is liable for the pedestrian's injury?
A) The driver of the front car.
B) The driver of the rear car.
C) Both drivers equally.
D) The pedestrian.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the driver of a vehicle is liable as the direct actor (mubashir) for any harm caused, but liability is removed when an external force overrides his control and breaks the chain of causation.

Driver's Liability: General Principle and Exceptions
Ruling on driver liability with exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi principle of driver liability based on direct causation and identify the four exceptional cases where the driver is not liable.

Core Idea
A driver is liable for harm caused by his vehicle unless an external factor breaks direct causation.

This passage from a Hanafi fiqh text establishes the general rule that a driver is liable for harm caused by his vehicle because he directly causes it (mubāshara). However, the rule admits exceptions when an intervening factor severs the chain of direct causation, such as being pushed from behind or a sudden, unforeseeable act. Understanding these exceptions is crucial for correctly applying liability in modern traffic accidents.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Principle
Read the opening sentence: 'the principle is that the driver is liable for every harm that arises from its wheels or its front or its back or from one of its sides'. Note the broad scope: liability covers all parts of the vehicle.
The basis is that the car is a pure instrument in the driver's hand, so direct causation is attributed to him.
2
Understand the Basis of Liability
The text explains: 'because the car is a pure instrument in the hand of the driver, so direct causation of harm is attributed to him.' This is the Hanafi concept of mubāshara (direct causation). The driver’s control makes him the direct cause.
Contrast with tasabbub (indirect causation), which may have different liability rules.
3
Distinguish Transgressor from Non-Transgressor
The text first discusses a transgressor (violating traffic rules): clearly liable. Then it discusses a non-transgressor (adhering to rules): still liable in principle, but exceptions exist. Note that even without transgression, liability remains unless causation is broken.
The maxim 'al-mutaʿaddī ḍāmin' (the transgressor is liable) reinforces the basic rule.
4
List the Exceptions
The text provides four cases where the driver is not liable. Read each carefully: (1) someone pushed another in front of the car suddenly; (2) the driver’s car was pushed from behind at a traffic light; (3) sudden mechanical defect causing loss of control; (4) a pedestrian suddenly jumped in front with no time to stop despite due care.
In each case, an external factor prevents attributing direct causation to the driver.
5
Apply the Underlying Condition
The text states: 'for his liability it is necessary that attribution of direct causation to him be sound without an interfering factor in a reasonable manner.' This condition explains all exceptions. Confirm that each exception involves an 'interfering factor' that makes the driver not the true direct cause.
The standard is reasonable attribution: if it is unreasonable to attribute the harm to the driver, liability falls.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعليه فالأصل أن السائق ضامن لكل ضرر ينشأ من عجلاتها أو مقدمتها أو من خلفها أو من أحد جانبيها ؛ لأن السيارة آلة محضة في يد السائق ، فتنسب مباشرة الاضرار إليه. فإن كان متعدياً بمخالفة قواعد المرور كأن يسوق بسرعة غير معتادة، فلا خفاء في كونه ضامناً؛ لأن الضرر نشأ بتعديه، والمتعدي ضامن في كل حال. أما إذا لم يكن متعديا في السير، بأن ساق سيارته ملتزماً بجميع قواعد المرور، فإنه يضمن الضرر الذي باشره، ويجب لتضمينه أن تصح نسبة المباشرة إليه بدون مزاحم على وجه معقول، على هذا الأساس لا يضمن في الصور الآتية :
١. إذا كان السائق يسوق سيارته ملتزما بجميع قواعد المرور ، ولكن دفع شخص رجلاً آخر أمام سيارته فجأة بحيث لم يمكن له أن يوقف السيارة قبل أن تدهسه. . إذا أوقف السائق سيارته أمام إشارة المرور منتظراً إشارة فتح الطريق فصدمته سيارة من خلفه ودفعته إلى الأمام فصدمت سيارته أحداً ، فليس الضمان على سائق السيارة الأمامية، بل الضمان على سائق السيارة التي صدمتها من خلفها ؛ لأنه لا تصح نسبة المباشرة إلى السيارة الأمامية، فإنها مدفوعة بمنزلة الآلة للسيارة الخلفية. إذا كانت السيارة سليمة قبل السير بها، وكان السائق يتعهدها تعهداً معروفاً، ثم طرأ علهيا خلل مفاجئ في جهاز من أجهزتها، حتى خرجت السيارة من قدرة السائق ومكنته من ضبطها، فصدمت إنسانا.
.{
إذا ساق إنسان سيارة في شارع عام ملتزما السرعة المقررة ، ومتبعاً خط السير حسب النظام، ومتبصراً في سوقه حسب قواعد السير، فقفز رجل أمامه فجأة ؛ إذ كان بقرب منها بحيث لا يمكن رها المعتاد في مثل ذلك المكان أن تتوقف بالفرملة، وكان قفزه لا يتوقع مسبقاً لدى السائق في سيره متبصر محتاط ، فصدمته السيارة رغم قيام السائق بما وجب عليه من الفرملة ونحوها.
And upon this, the principle is that the driver is liable for every harm that arises from its wheels or its front or its back or from one of its sides; because the car is a pure instrument in the hand of the driver, so direct causation of harm is attributed to him. If he was transgressing by violating traffic rules, such as driving at an unusual speed, there is no doubt that he is liable; because the harm arose from his transgression, and the transgressor is liable in every case. As for when he is not transgressing in driving, by driving his car adhering to all traffic rules, he is liable for the harm he directly caused, and for his liability it is necessary that attribution of direct causation to him be sound without an interfering factor in a reasonable manner. On this basis, he is not liable in the following cases: 1. If the driver was driving his car adhering to all traffic rules, but someone pushed another person in front of his car suddenly such that he could not stop the car before it ran over him. 2. If the driver stopped his car at a traffic signal waiting for the green light, and a car hit it from behind and pushed it forward, and his car hit someone, then liability is not on the driver of the front car, but on the driver of the car that hit it from behind; because attribution of direct causation to the front car is not valid, as it is pushed and is in the position of a tool for the rear car. 3. If the car was sound before being driven, and the driver maintained it properly, then a sudden defect occurred in one of its mechanisms, such that the car went out of the driver's control and he could not control it, and it hit a person. 4. If a person drove a car on a public road adhering to the prescribed speed, following the lane according to regulations, and being cautious in his driving according to traffic rules, and a man jumped in front of him suddenly while being close such that the usual braking in such a place could not stop the car, and his jump was not foreseeable beforehand by a cautious and careful driver, and the car hit him despite the driver doing what was required of braking and the like.
Source pages p. 483

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi legal ruling on the liability of a driver for harm caused by his vehicle. It establishes the general principle (aṣl) that the driver is liable because the car is a pure tool (āla maḥḍa) in his hand, so direct causation (mubāshara) is attributed to him. The author then distinguishes between a transgressor (mutaʿaddī) and one who is not transgressing. If the driver violates traffic rules, he is clearly liable. Even if he follows all rules, he may still be liable, but there are exceptions where liability is removed. The exceptions involve cases where an external intervening factor breaks the chain of direct causation. This ruling is based on the Hanafi legal maxim: 'al-mubāshir ḍāmin' (the one who directly causes harm is liable) and 'al-mutaʿaddī ḍāmin' (the transgressor is liable). The examples given are practical applications of these maxims.

Key Terms
مباشرة (mubāshara): Direct causation of harm without an intermediary; in this context, the driver's control of the vehicle is seen as direct.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability, specifically financial liability for harm caused.
متعدي (mutaʿaddī): One who transgresses; in this context, a driver who violates traffic rules.
مزاحم (muzāḥim): An interfering factor that breaks the chain of direct causation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in which scenario is the driver NOT liable?
A) The driver was speeding and hit a pedestrian.
B) The driver's car was pushed from behind at a traffic light and hit a person.
C) The driver was distracted by his phone and hit a cyclist.
D) The driver hit a pedestrian who was crossing at a marked crosswalk legally.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle of driver liability is based on direct causation; liability is removed only when an external factor makes attribution unreasonable.

Structures Built in Public Roads: Permissibility Conditioned on No Harm
Ruling on structures in public roads

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on constructing structures in public roads and apply the condition of absence of harm to determine permissibility.

Core Idea
A public road is a shared right; construction is allowed if harmless, but removal is mandated if harm occurs.

This lesson addresses a common urban issue: can a person build a latrine, waterspout, balcony, or shop that extends into a public road? The Hanafi school provides a clear principle: such constructions are permissible as long as they do not cause harm to passersby. However, if harm arises, the structure must be removed. The passage also explains the key terms and a nuance regarding who can demand removal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Four Structures
Begin by listing the four specific structures mentioned: latrine (kanīf), waterspout (mīzāb), projecting structure (jurş), and shop (dukkān). Note that the term 'jurş' has multiple definitions but all refer to projections into the road.
The exact meaning of 'jurş' is disputed but does not affect the ruling; any projection is treated similarly.
2
State the Conditional Permission
The matn says 'إن لم يضر بالناس' (if it does not harm people). This is the key condition: the permissibility (mubāḥ) of the structure depends on absence of harm. If harm occurs, the permission is lifted.
Harm includes physical obstruction, foul smells, dripping water, or any inconvenience to the public.
3
Understand the Right to Demolish
The phrase 'ولكل نقضه' (everyone has the right to demolish it) applies when the structure causes harm. However, this right is limited: the person seeking demolition must not have a similar structure themselves; otherwise, they are considered vexatious (muta'annit).
This prevents hypocritical objections and upholds fairness.
4
Analyze the Vexatious Objection Exception
From the commentary of Isma'il al-Saffar: if the objector also has a similar harmful structure, his objection is not entertained because he should first remove his own harm. This is based on the principle of 'remove harm from yourself first'.
This exception ensures the rule is applied equitably.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يحدث في الطريق من أحدث في طريق العامة كنيفاً أو ميزاباً أو جُرْصاً أو دُكَّاناً وَسِعَه ذلك إن لم يضر بالناس، ولكل نقضه (باب ما يحدث في الطريق) [V] (مَن أحدث في طريق العامة كنيفاً أو ميزاباً أو جُرْصُنَا) أو دُكاناً وَسِعَه ذلك ، إن لم يضر بالناس)، الكنيف المستراح والميزاب : مجرى الماء، والحرصن : البرج، وقيل: مجرى ماءٍ يركب في الحائط ، وعن البزدوي ه: جذع يخرج من الحائط ليبني عليه، ولكل نقضه ( ) : أي في صورة لم يضر، فالحاصلُ أَنَّهُ إِن أَضرَّ [١] قوله : باب ما يحدث في الطريق ؛ لَمَّا فرغ من بيان أحكام القتل مباشرة، شرع في بيان أحكامه تسبّباً، وقدّم الأوّل لكونه أصلاً ؛ لأنه قتل بلا واسطة، ولكونه أكثر وقوعاً ، فكان أمس حاجة إلى معرفة أحكامه. [٢] قوله : جُرصنا ؛ - بضم الجيم، وسكون الراء ، وضم الصاد المهملة – ، هو ليس بعربي أصلي، فقد اختلف فيه، فقيل : البرج ، وقيل : مجرى ماء يركب في الحائط، وعن الإمام البَزْدَوِيّ: جذعٌ يخرجُهُ الإنسانُ من الحائط ليبنى عليه. كذا في «المغرب»). وقيل : هو الممرّ على العلو، وهو مثل الرف، وقيل : هو الخشبة الموضوعة على جداري السطحين، ليتمكن من المرور ، وقيل : هو الذي يعمل قدام الطاقة لتوضع عليه كيزان ونحوها. كذا في رد المحتار». [٣] قوله : ولكلّ نقضه ؛ قال إسماعيل الصفار (۳) له : إنما ينتقض بخصومته إذا لم يكن له مثل ذلك، فإن كان له مثله لا يلتفت إلى خصومته ؛ لأنه لو أراد به إزالة الضرر عن الناس بدأ بنفسه، وحيث لم يزل ما في قدرته عُلِمَ أَنه مُتَعَنّت كذا في «التبيين».
Chapter on What is Caused in the Road. Whoever causes [constructs] in a public road a latrine, waterspout, projecting structure, or shop — that becomes spacious for him if it does not harm people. And everyone [has the right] to demolish it. (Chapter on What is Caused in the Road) [V] (Whoever causes in a public road a latrine, waterspout, projecting structure, or shop — that becomes spacious for him if it does not harm people). The latrine is the toilet; the waterspout is the water channel; the projecting structure (jurş) is a tower, and it is said: a water channel mounted in the wall, and from al-Bazdawi: a beam projecting from the wall to build upon. And everyone [has the right] to demolish it: that is, in a case where it does not harm [but still can be demolished? The commentary clarifies the condition. The sum: that if it harms... (1) His statement: Chapter on What is Caused in the Road: When he finished explaining the rulings of direct killing, he began explaining its rulings due to causation, and he presented the first because it is fundamental, being killing without intermediary, and because it occurs more frequently, so it was more needed to know its rulings. (2) His statement: 'jurş' — with damm on jīm, sukūn on rā', damm on the important şād — it is not originally Arabic; there is disagreement about it. It is said: a tower; and it is said: a water channel mounted in the wall; and from Imam al-Bazdawi: a beam that a person extends from the wall to build upon. Thus in al-Mughrib. And it is said: the passage at the top, like a shelf; and it is said: the plank placed on the two walls of the two roofs to allow passage; and it is said: what is made in front of a window to place pitchers and the like. Thus in Radd al-Muhtar. (3) His statement: 'and everyone [has the right] to demolish it' — Isma'il al-Saffar said: It is only demolished through his litigation if he does not have something similar; for if he has something similar, his litigation is not considered, because if he wanted to remove harm from people, he would begin with himself; and since he did not remove what is within his power, it is known that he is vexatious. Thus in al-Tabyin.
Source pages p. 512

Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of a new chapter (باب) on structures built in public roads. The author transitions from direct killing (mubasharah) to killing by cause (tasabbub), because structures can cause harm indirectly. The matn states that if someone constructs a latrine (كنيف), waterspout (ميزاب), projecting structure (جرص), or shop (دكان) in a public road, it is permissible provided it does not harm the public. The key condition is عدم الضرر (no harm). The commentary then explains the meaning of these terms, noting that 'جرص' is a non-Arabic word with multiple interpretations: a tower, a water channel in the wall, a beam projecting from the wall for building, a high passage, a plank between two walls, or a shelf in front of a window. The phrase 'ولكل نقضه' means that if the structure causes harm, any affected individual has the right to have it demolished. However, the commentary from Isma'il al-Saffar clarifies that if the person who wants to demolish it also has a similar structure, his objection is not entertained because he should remove his own harm first; otherwise he is considered vexatious (متعنت). The ruling is based on the principle of removing harm (ضرر) from the public road.

Key Terms
كنيف (kanīf): A latrine or toilet built in or affixed to a public road.
ميزاب (mīzāb): A waterspout or rainwater channel that projects from a wall into the public road.
جرص (jurş): A projecting structure like a balcony, beam, or shelf built on a public road; the exact meaning is disputed among scholars; it could be a tower, water channel, or beam for construction.
دكان (dukkān): A shop or bench built extending into the public road.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a shop built in a public road that does not harm passersby?
A) It is forbidden and must be demolished.
B) It is permissible as long as it does not cause harm.
C) It is permissible only if the builder owns the land.
D) It is permissible but only for the shop itself, not other structures.

Key Takeaway
Construction in a public road is permissible only on condition that it does not cause any harm to the public; if harm arises, the structure must be removed, and only those free of similar structures may demand removal.

Rulings on Creating Structures in Public Roads and Liability for Harm
Commentary on permissibility and liability for road structures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on building structures in public roads, apply the condition of no harm, and distinguish when liability for death falls on the 'āqilah versus the builder.

Core Idea
A public road is common property: building is permissible but revocable, and liability for harm depends on causation and authorization.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah explains the legal status of structures built in public roads. While it is permissible to build if no harm is caused, the road remains a common right, so any individual may demolish such structures. The commentary then addresses liability when such structures cause death or property damage, introducing important distinctions between direct and indirect causation, and the role of the imam's permission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The passage states: 'Whoever creates in a public road a latrine, water spout, projection, or shop, that is permissible for him if it does not harm people.' The act is building a structure abutting the public way.
The structures listed are typical examples; the rule applies to any permanent fixture.
2
Understand the consequence of permissibility
Even when permissible, 'everyone has the right to demolish it' because the road is common property (ḥaqq mushtarak). This means the builder does not acquire a vested right.
Ismā'īl al-Ṣaffār adds that a litigant must not himself have a similar structure; otherwise his complaint is obstinate.
3
Apply the condition of no harm
If the structure harms people, it is not permissible. If it does not harm, it is permissible but still subject to demolition. Harm is determined by actual inconvenience or danger.
Harm here includes obstruction or risk to passersby.
4
Distinguish private roads
In a private road owned by a group, no alteration is allowed without unanimous permission of all partners, even if harmless.
Private roads are treated as joint ownership, not public trust.
5
Analyze liability for harm
If the structure falls and kills a person, the 'āqilah (agnatic group) pays diyah. If it kills an animal, the builder pays from his own wealth. Liability is waived if the imam authorized the construction. Additionally, if a person dies in a well from hunger or distress (not the fall), Abu Hanifah holds no liability.
This last point is an intra-Hanafi dispute with the Sahibayn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب ما يحدث في الطريق
مَن أحدث في طريق العامة كنيفاً أو ميزاباً أو جُرْصاً أو دُكَّاناً وَسِعَه ذلك إن لم يضر بالناس، ولكل نقضه
قوله : باب ما يحدث في الطريق ؛ لَمَّا فرغ من بيان أحكام القتل مباشرة، شرع في بيان أحكامه تسبّباً، وقدّم الأوّل لكونه أصلاً ؛ لأنه قتل بلا واسطة، ولكنه أكثر وقوعاً ، فكان أمس حاجة إلى معرفة أحكامه.
قوله : جُرصنا ؛ - بضم الجيم، وسكون الراء ، وضم الصاد المهملة – ، هو ليس بعربي أصلي، فقد اختلف فيه، فقيل : البرج ، وقيل : مجرى ماء يركب في الحائط، وعن الإمام البَزْدَوِيّ: جذعٌ يخرجُهُ الإنسانُ من الحائط ليبنى عليه. كذا في «المغرب». وقيل : هو الممرّ على العلو، وهو مثل الرف، وقيل : هو الخشبة الموضوعة على جداري السطحين، ليتمكن من المرور ، وقيل : هو الذي يعمل قدام الطاقة لتوضع عليه كيزان ونحوها. كذا في رد المحتار.
قوله : ولكلّ نقضه ؛ قال إسماعيل الصفار له : إنما ينتقض بخصومته إذا لم يكن له مثل ذلك، فإن كان له مثله لا يلتفت إلى خصومته ؛ لأنه لو أراد به إزالة الضرر عن الناس بدأ بنفسه، وحيث لم يزل ما في قدرته عُلِمَ أَنه مُتَعَنّت كذا في «التبيين».
كتاب الديات / أنواعها وأحكامها
وفي غير نافذ لا يسعه بلا إذنِ الشركاء، وإن لم يضر.
بالناس لا يجوز له أن يفعل ، وإن لم يُضِرَّ بهم يجوز، لكن مع ذلك يكون لكل واحد نقضه ؛ لأنَّه تصرُّف في الحقِّ المشترك ، فلكل نقضه كما في الملكِ المشترك مع أنه لم يضر ، وفي غير نافذ لا يسعه بلا إذنِ الشركاء، وإن لم يضر. وضَمِنَ عاقلتُهُ دَيَّةَ مَن مات بسقوطها، كما لو وضع حجراً، أو حفر بئراً في الطريق، فتلف به نفس، فإن تَلِفَ به بهيمةٌ ضَمِنَ هو إن لم يأذن به الإمام، فإن الضمانَ في جميع ما ذُكِرَ بإحداث شيءٍ في طريق العامة إنما يكون إذا لم يأذن به الإمام، فإن أذن أو مات واقع في بئر طريق جوعاً ، أو غماً فلا، هذا عند
قوله : وفي غير نافذ.... الخ ؛ أي من أحدث شيئاً من هذه المذكورات في طريق طائفة مخصوصة لا يجوز له ذلك ؛ لأنها مملوكة لأهلها فلا يجوز التصرف فيها إلا باذن الكل.
قوله : وضمن عاقلته دِيَـة مـن مـات.... الخ ؛ أي إذا أحدث شيئاً من هذه المذكورات فسقط على إنسان فهلك فضمنَ الدِّية على العاقلة ؛ لأنه مسبب لتلفه، متعدّ بشغله هواء الطريق، وهذا من أسباب الضمان وهو الأصل في هذا الباب. كذا في «الهداية».
قوله : ضمن هو ؛ أي الواضع أو الحافر ؛ لأنه متعد، فيضمن، يتولّد من تعديه، غير أن العاقلة تتحمّل النفس دون المال، فكان ضمان البهيمة في ماله ، وإلقاء التراب واتحاد الطين في الطريق بمنزلة إلقاء الحجر والخشبة لما ذكرنا من أنه متعد فيه. كذا في «الهداية».
قوله : فإن أذن...الخ ؛ أي فإن أذن الإمام فلا يضمن ؛ لأنه غير متعد، حيث فعل ما فعل بأمر من له الولاية في حقوق العامة.
قوله : فلا ؛ أي فلا يضمن ، قال في «الهداية» : لا ضمان على الحافر عند أبي حنيفة ؛ لأنه مات لمعنى في نفسه والضمان إنّما يجب إذا مات من الوقوع.
Chapter on what is created in the road. Whoever creates in a public road a latrine, water spout, projection, or shop, that is permissible for him if it does not harm people, and everyone has the right to demolish it. His saying: Chapter on what is created in the road: when he finished explaining the rulings of direct killing, he began explaining its rulings by causation, and he presented the first because it is the original, since it is killing without intermediary, and it is more frequent, so it was more in need of knowing its rulings. His saying: 'jurṣun' – with dammah on jīm, sukūn on rā', and dammah on ṣād – it is not originally Arabic, and there is disagreement about it: it is said to be a tower, and it is said to be a water channel mounted on the wall, and from Imam al-Bazdawī: a beam that a person extends from the wall to build upon. Thus in 'al-Mughrib'. And it is said to be a passage on high, like a shelf, and it is said to be the wood placed on the two walls of two roofs to enable passage, and it is said to be what is made in front of a window to place water jugs and the like. Thus in 'Radd al-Muhtār'. His saying: 'and everyone has the right to demolish it'; Ismā'īl al-Ṣaffār said: it is only demolished by his litigation if he does not have the like of it, but if he has its like, his litigation is not considered; because if he intended to remove harm from people, he would start with himself, and since he did not remove what was in his power, it is known that he is obstinate. Thus in 'al-Tabyīn'. Book of Diyāt / Its Types and Rulings. And in a non-passage (private road), it is not permissible for him without the permission of the partners, even if it does not harm. By the people, it is not permissible for him to do it, and if it does not harm them it is permissible, but nevertheless everyone has the right to demolish it; because it is a disposition in common right, so everyone has the right to demolish it as in common property even though it does not harm. And in a non-passage, it is not permissible for him without the permission of the partners, even if it does not harm. And his 'āqilah (agnatic group) is liable for the diyah (blood money) of one who dies from its falling, as if he placed a stone or dug a well in the road and a person perished from it; and if an animal is destroyed by it, he is liable himself, if the imam did not permit. Indeed, liability in all that is mentioned regarding creating something in a public road only occurs if the imam did not permit; if he permitted, or if one died in a well of the road from hunger or distress, then no liability. This is according to. His saying: 'and in a non-passage... etc.' i.e., whoever creates any of these mentioned things in a private road of a specific group, it is not permissible for him; because it is owned by its people, so it is not permissible to dispose of it except with the permission of all. His saying: 'and his 'āqilah is liable for the diyah of one who dies... etc.' i.e., if he creates any of these mentioned things and it falls on a person and he dies, then the diyah is imposed on the 'āqilah; because he is a cause of his destruction, having transgressed by occupying the airspace of the road, and this is among the causes of liability and it is the principle in this chapter. Thus in 'al-Hidāyah'. His saying: 'he is liable' i.e., the placer or digger; because he is a transgressor, so he is liable for what arises from his transgression, except that the 'āqilah bears the [liability for] life not property, so liability for the animal is on his own wealth; and throwing dirt and mixing mud in the road is like throwing a stone and wood due to what we mentioned that he is a transgressor in it. Thus in 'al-Hidāyah'. His saying: 'if [the imam] permitted... etc.' i.e., if the imam permitted, he is not liable; because he is not a transgressor, since he did what he did by order of the one with authority over public rights. His saying: 'so no [liability]' i.e., no liability. He said in 'al-Hidāyah': there is no liability on the digger according to Abū Ḥanīfah; because he died due to an internal cause in himself, and liability is only wajib if he dies from the fall.
Source pages p. 512

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of al-Wiqāyah, expanding the rulings of creating structures in public and private roads, along with liability for resulting harm. The commentary begins by explaining the placement of this chapter after the chapter on direct killing, noting that this deals with causation (tasabbub) rather than direct action. The term 'jurṣun' is defined in multiple ways, reflecting non-Arabic origin; the preferred Hanafi view is a beam protruding from a wall for construction. The main ruling: it is permissible to build a latrine, water spout, projection, or shop abutting a public road provided it does not harm passersby. However, because the public road is common property (ḥaqq mushtarak), every individual has the right to demolish it without the builder's consent, even if it causes no harm. Ismā'īl al-Ṣaffār adds that a litigant can only demand demolition if he himself does not have a similar structure; if he does, his complaint is disregarded as obstinacy. For private roads owned by a group (ṭā'ifa makhṣūṣa), no alteration is allowed without unanimous permission, even if harmless. Liability (ḍamān) arises if the structure causes a person's death by falling: the 'āqilah (agnatic heirs) pay the diyah because the builder is a muta'addī (transgressor) for occupying the road's airspace. If the structure kills an animal, the builder pays from his own wealth. Crucially, liability is conditional on the absence of the imam's permission; if the imam authorized the construction, there is no liability because the builder is not a transgressor. Additionally, if a person dies in a well from hunger or distress (not from the fall itself), Abū Ḥanīfah holds no liability because death was due to an internal cause, not the direct fall. This is an intra-Hanafi point: the Sahibayn differ. The commentary emphasizes that throwing dirt or mud in the road is analogous to placing a stone or wood in terms of liability.

Key Terms
كنيف (kunayf): A latrine or toilet structure built in the road.
ميزاب (mīzāb): A water spout or channel to drain rainwater from a roof into the road.
جُرْص (jurṣ): According to al-Bazdawī, a beam protruding from a wall to build upon; also said to be a tower or water channel.
دكان (dukkān): A shop or bench built in the road.
طريق العامة (ṭarīq al-'āmmah): A public road or thoroughfare shared by all people.
عاقلة ('āqilah): The agnatic group (male relatives on father's side) responsible for paying diyah in cases of accidental killing.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability or financial responsibility for harm caused, including diyah for death and compensation for property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school as explained in this passage, what is the ruling if someone builds a shop in a public road that causes no harm?
A) It is forbidden and must be removed immediately.
B) It is permissible, but any individual has the right to demolish it.
C) It is permissible and no one can object if it causes no harm.
D) It is only permissible with the permission of the judge.

Key Takeaway
Building in a public road is permissible only if harmless, but the structure is never permanent because the road is common property; liability for harm depends on direct causation and authorization.

Authorized Persons for Demolishing a Leaning Wall and Liability
Definitional ruling listing authorized persons for demolition and liability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state who is authorized to demolish a leaning wall threatening a public road and identify the liability rules for each authorized person.

Core Idea
Only those with legal authority over a wall may demolish it; liability follows the person's status.

In this lesson, we examine a key rule from Hanafi fiqh regarding dangerous structures. When a wall leans over a public road, not everyone has the right to demolish it. The text specifies certain individuals—owners, guardians, and slaves with limited capacity—and outlines the liability if the wall falls after a request to demolish. Understanding these categories is essential for applying property and tort law correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is demolishing a leaning wall that endangers a public road. The ruling is mubah (permissible) for authorized persons.
2
List the authorized persons
From the passage: any Muslim or dhimmi owner, mortgagor, father of a minor, executor, mukatib, and trading slave.
The list is exhaustive for this context.
3
Explain the basis of authority for each
Ownership or guardianship gives authority. Mortgagor retains ownership; father/executor act as guardians; mukatib and trading slave have limited ownership.
Mortgagee is explicitly excluded.
4
Determine liability if damage occurs
If after request the wall is not demolished and causes harm, liability varies: for father/executor, on orphan's estate; for mukatib, lesser of his value or blood-money; for trading slave, on master's 'aqlah.
Details from marginal notes are important.
5
Note the procedural point about witnesses
No witnesses are required for the request; mere request suffices. Al-Hidayah's mention of witnesses is not necessary.
This avoids proof problems upon denial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
افصل في الحائط المائل. رب حائط مال إلى طريق العامة، وطلب نقضه مسلم أو ذمي ممن يملك نقضه كالراهن يفك رهنه، وأب الطفل، والوصي، والمكاتب، والعبد التاجر. [١] قوله : وطلب نقضه الخ؛ في الاكتفاء على الطلب إشارة إلى عدم الاحتياج إلى الإشهاد، وما فعله صاحب «الهداية» من ذكر الإشهاد بعد الطلب فلا يتمكن بإثباته عند الإنكار، كما سيصرح بها الشارح. [٢] قوله : فإنه يملك؛ لا المرتهن؛ أي الراهن قادر على الهدم، بأن ينفك العين، وأعادها إلى يده فيهدم. [٣] قوله : وأب الطفل والوصي... الخ؛ أي كأب الطفل والوصي فإنهما يقدران على نقض حائط اليتيم بالولاية، لكن الضمان في مال اليتيم؛ لأن فعلهما كفعله، فالتقدم إليهما كالتقدم إليه، وهذا أولى من قول «الهداية»: والى أب اليتيم؛ لأنه لا يتيتم مع وجود الأب. كذا قال الجلبي. [٤] قوله : والمكاتب؛ أي وكالمكاتب، فإنه ممن يملك نقض حائطه الماثل، فلو لم ينقض بعد الطلب منه فسقط فأتلف إنساناً، فعليه الأقل من قيمته، ومن دية المقتول، وكذا العبد التاجر، فإنّه أيضاً ممن يملك نقض حائطه المائلة، فإن من أتلف إنسانا وعليه دين أو لا، فعلى عاقلة مولاه دِيَةُ الإنسان، وإن أتلف متاعاً ففي عتق العبد، هذا خلاصة ما في «العناية». ففي «المنح» في صورة المكاتب، وهذا لو أتلف حال بقاء الكتابة، فلو بعد عتقه فعلى عاقلة المولى، ولو بعد العجز لا يجب شيء على أحد، ويهدر الدم؛ لعدم قدرة المكاتب، وعدم الإشهاد على الولي، وفي «البرجندي» عن قاضي خان: إن أشهد على الولي صح الإشهاد أيضاً.
Section on the leaning wall. Any wall that leans towards a public road, and a Muslim or dhimmi who has the right to demolish it requests its demolition, such as a mortgagor to redeem his pledge, the father of a minor, the executor, the mukatib, and the trading slave. [1] His saying: 'and requests its demolition' etc.; in being content with the request there is an indication that there is no need for witnesses. What the author of al-Hidayah did by mentioning witnesses after the request cannot be proven upon denial, as the commentator will clarify. [2] His saying: 'for he has the right' — not the mortgagee; meaning the mortgagor is capable of demolition by redeeming the property and returning it to his hand, then demolishes it. [3] His saying: 'and the father of the minor and the executor' etc.; meaning like the father of a minor and the executor, they are able to demolish the orphan's wall by guardianship, but the liability is on the orphan's wealth because their act is like his act, so addressing them is like addressing him. This is better than the statement of al-Hidayah: 'and to the father of an orphan', because one is not an orphan while the father exists. So said al-Jalabi. [4] His saying: 'and the mukatib'; meaning also the mukatib, for he is among those who have the right to demolish his leaning wall. If he does not demolish after being requested, and it falls and kills a person, then he is liable for the lesser of his value or the blood-money of the killed. Likewise the trading slave, for he also has the right to demolish his leaning wall. If he kills a person, whether he has debt or not, the blood-money of the person is upon the 'aqlah (agnatic group) of his master; and if he destroys goods, then (it is) in the slave's freedom. This is the summary of what is in al-'Inayah. In al-Minh: regarding the case of the mukatib, if he causes damage while the kitabah (contract of manumission) remains, then after his freedom it is upon the master's 'aqlah; and if after inability (to pay), nothing is owed by anyone, and the blood is wasted, due to the mukatib's incapacity and the absence of witnesses against the guardian. And in al-Birjandi from Qadi Khan: if he witnesses against the guardian, the witness is also valid.
Source pages p. 517

Commentary
This passage is a definitional ruling from a commentary on al-Hidayah, specifying who has the right to demolish a leaning wall that endangers a public road. The main text lists: any Muslim or dhimmi owner, a mortgagor (to redeem his pledge), the father of a minor, the executor (wasi), a mukatib (slave under contract of manumission), and a trading slave (al-'abd al-tajir). The marginal notes expand on each. Note [1] clarifies that mere request is sufficient; witnesses are not required. Note [2] specifies that the mortgagor, not the mortgagee, has the right because the mortgagor retains ownership and can redeem. Note [3] explains that the father and executor act as guardians for the minor; their action is like the minor's own, so liability for damage falls on the minor's estate. The author prefers this over al-Hidayah's wording 'father of an orphan' because a child with a living father is not technically an orphan. Note [4] discusses the mukatib and trading slave: if they fail to demolish after request and the wall kills someone, the mukatib is liable for the lesser of his own value or the victim's blood-money. For the trading slave, the blood-money is on his master's 'aqlah (clan), and if he destroys goods, it affects his freedom. Further details from al-Minh and al-Birjandi are given about timing and witnessing.

Key Terms
الحائط المائل (al-ḥā’iṭ al-mā’il): A wall that has tilted, posing a risk of falling onto a public thoroughfare.
نقض (naqḍ): Demolition or dismantling of the wall to prevent harm.
المكاتب (al-mukātib): A slave who has entered a contract of manumission (kitabah) and is working to earn freedom.
العبد التاجر (al-‘abd al-tājir): A slave who is permitted by his master to engage in trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) His master
C) The executor
D) Any Muslim

Key Takeaway
Only persons with legal authority over a wall may demolish it; liability for damages follows the authority's status.

Liability for a Leaning Wall: Istihsan over Qiyas
Legal ruling with proof and madhhab dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability when a leaning wall falls after a request, and explain the istihsan reasoning that overrides qiyas.

Core Idea
A formal request makes the owner a transgressor; liability follows even without direct causation.

This lesson examines a classic Hanafi case from property law: a wall leaning towards a public road. The ruler must determine liability when it falls. The Hanafi school, using istihsan, holds the owner liable after a proper request, while Shafi'i follows qiyas and absolves him. Understanding this dispute reveals core Hanafi methodology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and parties
The wall owner (any eligible person like pledgor, guardian, mukatab, trading slave) must be asked to demolish the leaning wall. The request is sufficient; witnessing is not a condition.
Note that the request must be made by someone with legal standing.
2
Analyze the condition for liability
Liability arises only if the owner refuses or delays demolition after a proper request and within a reasonable period. If he demolishes in time, no liability.
The period must be sufficient for him to act.
3
Understand the istihsan justification
Istihsan overrides qiyas because the owner's inaction after request makes him a transgressor. The wall occupies public airspace, so he is obliged to clear it. Analogized to one who holds another's garment and refuses to return it.
Istihsan is based on narrations from Shuraih and al-Nakha'i and on the principle of preventing public harm.
4
Compare with the Shafi'i qiyas
Shafi'i holds no liability because no direct causation. Hanafi responds that refusal to act after request is itself a cause of harm, and liability is needed to protect the public.
Intra-Hanafi difference: Imam Muhammad requires witnesses to three specific matters for the 'aqila to be liable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
افصل في الحائط المائل
ورب حائط مال إلى طريق العامة، وطلب نقضه مسلم أو ذمي ممن يملك نقضه كالراهن يفك رهنه، وأب الطفل، والوصي، والمكاتب، والعبد التاجر
[١] قوله: وطلب نقضه الخ؛ في الاكتفاء على الطلب إشارة إلى عدم الاحتياج إلى الإشهاد، وما فعله صاحب «الهداية» من ذكر الإشهاد بعد الطلب فلا يتمكن بإثباته عند الإنكار، كما سيصرح بها الشارح.
[٢] قوله: فإنه يملك؛ لا المرتهن؛ أي الراهن قادر على الهدم، بأن ينفك العين، وأعادها إلى يده فيهدم.
[٣] قوله: وأب الطفل والوصي... الخ؛ أي كأب الطفل والوصي فإنهما يقدران على نقض حائط اليتيم بالولاية، لكن الضمان في مال اليتيم؛ لأن فعلهما كفعله، فالتقدم إليهما كالتقدم إليه، وهذا أولى من قول «الهداية»: وإلى أب اليتيم؛ لأنه لا يتيتم مع وجود الأب. كذا قال الجلبي.
[٤] قوله: والمكاتب؛ أي وكالمكاتب، فإنه ممن يملك نقض حائطه المائل، فلو لم ينقض بعد الطلب منه فسقط فأتلف إنساناً، فعليه الأقل من قيمته، ومن دية المقتول، وكذا العبد التاجر، فإنه أيضاً ممن يملك نقض حائطه المائل، فإن من أتلف إنساناً وعليه دين أو لا، فعلى عاقلة مولاه دية الإنسان، وإن أتلف متاعاً ففي عتق العبد، هذا خلاصة ما في «العناية». ففي «المنح» في صورة المكاتب، وهذا لو أتلف حال بقاء الكتابة، فلو بعد عتقه فعلى عاقلة المولى، ولو بعد العجز لا يجب شيء على أحد، ويهدر الدم؛ لعدم قدرة المكاتب، وعدم الإشهاد على الولي، وفي «البرجندي» عن قاضي خان: إن أشهد على الولي صح الإشهاد أيضاً. (في «ذخيرة العقبى» ص٦١١)
فلم ينقض في مدة يمكن نقضه ضمن مالاً تلف به، وعاقلته النفس (١٢). وصورة الطلب أن يقول: إن حائطك هذا مائل فاهدمه، وصورة الإشهاد أن يقول: اشهدوا أني تقدمت إلى هذا الرجل لهدم حائطه، واعلم أنه ذكر في الكتب الطلب والإشهاد، لكن الإشهاد ليس بشرط، وإنما ذكر ليتمكن من إثباته عند الإنكار، فكان من باب الاحتياط.
[١] قوله: في مدة يمكن نقضه؛ أي في مدة يقدر على نقضه فيها، فلو ذهب بعد سقط الحائط لم يضمن؛ لأن مدة التمكن حتى الطلب لطلب من يهدمه، وكان في ذلك من إحضار الإجراء مستثنى في الشرع. كذا في «رد المحتار»(٣).
[٢] قوله: ضمن مالاً تلف به وعاقلته النفس؛ وهذا استحسان، والقياس أن لا يضمن، وهو قول الشافعي؛ لأنه لم يوجد منه صنع هو فعل، ولا مباشرة علة، ولا مباشرة شرط أو سبب، والضمان باعتبار ذلك، فصار كما إذا لم يشهد عليه، وبطل نقضه منه. ووجه الاستحسان ما روي عن شريح والنخعي وغيرهم من أئمة التابعين ما قلناه؛ ولأن الحائط لما مال فقد أشغل هواء الطريق بملكه، ورفعه في قدرته، فإذا طولب برفعه لزمه ذلك، فإذا امتنع مع التمكن منه صار متعدياً، فيلزمه موجبه؛ ولأن الضرر الخاص يجب تحمله لدفع الضرر العام كالكفار إذا تترسوا بالمسلمين، ثم ما تلف به من النفوس تحمله العاقلة؛ لئلا يؤدي إلى الإجحاف.
قال محمد: لا تتحمل العاقلة حتى يشهد الشهود على ثلاثة أشياء: (١) لأن امتناعه من تفريع الطريق المشتغل هواؤه بملكه مع تمكنه من التفريغ بعد طلبه، كمن وقع في يده ثوب إنسان فإنه لا يكون متعدياً في الإمساك، ولكن لو طولب بالرد فلم يرد صار متعدياً، فكذا هنا بخلاف ما قبل الإشهاد؛ لأنه بمنزلة هلاك الثوب قبل الطلب؛ ولأن الضمان لو لم يجب عليه لامتنع عن التفريغ فينقطع المارة خوفا على أنفسهم فيتضررون. (انظر: «فتح باب العناية» ٣: ٣٧١) (٢) كالهداية (٤: ١٩٥). (٣) رد المحتار (٦: ٦٠٠).
Chapter on the leaning wall. Every owner of a wall that leans towards a public road, and (the owner) requests its demolition—whether Muslim or dhimmi, from among those who own its demolition, like the pledgor redeeming his pledge, the father of a minor, the executor, the mukatab, and the trading slave. [1] His statement: 'and requests its demolition etc.': in being content with the request, there is an indication that there is no need for witnesses. What the author of Al-Hidayah did by mentioning witnesses after the request, (the claimant) cannot prove it when denied, as the commentator will clarify. [2] His statement: 'for he owns (the demolition)': not the mortgagee; i.e., the pledgor is able to demolish, by the property being released and returned to his hand, then he demolishes. [3] His statement: 'the father of the minor and the executor etc.': i.e., like the father of the minor and the executor, for they are able to demolish the orphan's wall by guardianship, but the liability is on the orphan's wealth because their action is like his action, so approaching them is like approaching him. This is preferable to the statement of Al-Hidayah: 'and to the father of the orphan' because he is not an orphan in the presence of the father. Thus said al-Jalabi. [4] His statement: 'and the mukatab': i.e., and like the mukatab, for he is among those who own the demolition of his leaning wall. So if he does not demolish after the request from him, and it falls and kills a person, then (the mukatab) is liable for the lesser of his value and the blood money of the slain. Likewise the trading slave, for he also is among those who own the demolition of his leaning wall. So if he kills a person—whether he has a debt or not—then the blood money of the person is upon the 'aqila of his master, and if he destroys goods, then (it is) in the emancipation of the slave. This is the summary of what is in Al-'Inayah. In Al-Minh, regarding the case of the mukatab: this is if he kills while kitabah remains; so if after his emancipation, then upon the 'aqila of the master; and if after incapability, nothing is incumbent on anyone, and the blood is wasted, due to the mukatab's lack of ability and the lack of witnesses against the guardian. And in Al-Birjandi from Qadi Khan: if he has witnesses against the guardian, the witnessing is also valid. (In Dhakhrat al-'Uqba, p. 611). So if he does not demolish within a period in which demolition is possible, he is liable for property lost by it, and his 'aqila (is liable) for the life (1). The form of the request is that he says: 'This wall of yours is leaning, so demolish it.' The form of witnessing is that he says: 'Bear witness that I have approached this man to demolish his wall.' And know that the request and witnessing are mentioned in the books, but witnessing is not a condition; rather it is mentioned so that he can prove it when denied, so it is a precaution. [1] His statement: 'within a period in which demolition is possible': i.e., a period in which he is able to demolish it. So if he leaves and the wall falls, he is not liable because the period of ability... until the request to hire someone to demolish it, and that is exempted in the law. Thus in Radd al-Muhtar (3). [2] His statement: 'he is liable for property lost by it and his 'aqila for the life': this is istihsan, and by qiyas he would not be liable, and it is the saying of al-Shafi'i, because there is no action from him that is a deed, nor direct causation, nor direct condition or cause, and liability is based on that. So it becomes as if he did not have witnesses against him, and his request for demolition is void. The justification for istihsan is what is narrated from Shuraih and al-Nakha'i and other Imams of the Tabi'in—what we have said; and because when the wall leaned, it occupied the airspace of the road with his property, and its removal was within his ability. So when he is requested to remove it, it becomes obligatory upon him. When he refrains despite ability, he becomes a transgressor, so the consequence of transgression becomes incumbent. And because private harm must be borne to repel public harm, like when unbelievers shield themselves with Muslims. Then, what is lost of lives is borne by the 'aqila to prevent injustice. Muhammad said: The 'aqila does not undertake (liability) until the witnesses testify to three things: (1) because his refusal to clear the road whose airspace is occupied by his property despite his ability to clear after the request, is like one in whose hand falls the garment of another: he is not a transgressor in holding it, but if he is requested to return it and does not, he becomes a transgressor. So here too, unlike before witnessing, because it is like the destruction of the garment before the request. And because if liability were not imposed on him, he would refrain from clearing, so passersby would stop for fear of themselves and be harmed. (See: Fath Bab al-'Inayah 3:371) (2) Al-Hidayah (4:195). (3) Radd al-Muhtar (6:600).
Source pages p. 517

Commentary
This passage is a detailed legal ruling on the liability of a property owner whose wall leans towards a public road. The author, commenting on Al-Hidayah, explains that the owner (whether Muslim or dhimmi) is obligated to demolish the wall upon request by anyone who has authority to request, such as the pledgor (for his own property), the father or guardian of a minor orphan, the executor, the mukatab (slave under contract of manumission), and the trading slave. The key point is that the request is sufficient; formal witnessing (ishhad) is not a condition for liability but is recommended for proof in court. If after a proper request the owner fails to demolish within a reasonable time, and the wall falls causing damage to property or death, the owner is liable: for property damage he pays compensation, and for death his 'aqila (paternal relatives) pay the blood money. This ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference), which overrides the qiyas (analogy) that would absolve him because he did not directly cause the harm. The istihsan is supported by precedents from Shuraih and al-Nakha'i, and by the principle that private harm must be borne to prevent public harm, similar to the case of unbelievers using Muslims as shields. The text also includes an intra-Hanafi difference: Imam Muhammad requires that witnesses testify to three specific matters for the 'aqila to be liable, implying a stricter condition. The Shafi'i view (which follows qiyas) is that there is no liability at all since no direct action occurred.

Key Terms
حائط مائل (ha'it ma'il): A wall that leans or inclines towards a public road, posing a danger to passersby.
طلب (talab): A formal request to the owner to demolish the leaning wall.
إشهاد (ishhad): The act of having witnesses present at the time of the request to prove it if needed.
عاقلة ('aqila): The paternal relatives of the liable person who collectively pay blood money (diyah) for unintentional killing.
استحسان (istihsan): A juristic preference overriding qiyas in favor of a more appropriate ruling based on broader legal objectives or precedent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence if the owner of a leaning wall refuses to demolish it after a proper request, and the wall later falls and kills a person?
A) The owner is not liable because he did not directly cause the death.
B) The owner must pay blood money from his own wealth.
C) The owner's 'aqila (paternal relatives) are liable for the blood money.
D) The owner is liable only if witnesses were present at the request.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a formal request turns inaction into transgression, making the owner liable even without direct causation, based on the principle that private harm must be borne to prevent public harm.

Liability for Animal Excretion on Public Roads
Ruling on liability for animal excretion on the road

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for damage caused by a riding animal's excretion on a public road and identify the condition that triggers liability.

Core Idea
A rider is not liable for his animal's excretion on the road unless he stopped the animal for an unrelated reason.

This lesson examines a specific scenario from the Hanafi law of torts: an animal urinating or defecating on a public road and causing harm. The passage distinguishes between when the animal is moving or stopped for excretion (no liability) and when it is stopped for another purpose (liability). We will explore the rationale and the underlying condition of safety.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the harm
Determine what the animal did (excretion) and what harm it caused (person injured).
The text says 'عطب إنسان' meaning a person is harmed by the dung or urine.
2
Examine the two scenarios: moving vs. stopped for excretion
The text states no liability if the animal is moving or if it is stopped specifically to excrete. Understand why: animals naturally excrete, and such acts are unavoidable.
Stopping for this purpose is not considered transgression.
3
Isolate the scenario that incurs liability: stopped for another purpose
If the rider stops the animal for some other reason (e.g., to talk, to rest) and it then excretes, the rider is liable because his stopping was unnecessary and created an avoidable risk.
The text explicitly says 'ضمن' (liable) in this case.
4
Connect to the principle of shart al-salaamah
The general principle: using public roads is permissible only on condition of safety. This condition applies to avoidable harms. Excretion while moving or stopped naturally is unavoidable, so no liability. Stopping unnecessarily makes the harm avoidable, thus liability.
This principle is explained in the commentary and applies to all public road cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أو عطب إنسان بما راثت أو بالت في الطريق سائرة، أو أوقفها لذلك، فإن أوقفها لغيره ضمن ، فإن أصابت بيدها ، أو رجلها حصاةً أو نواة، أو أثارت غباراً أو جراً صغيراً، فَفَقأ عيناً أو أفسد ثوباً لا يضمن، وضمن بالكبير
(أو عطب إنسان بما راثت أو بالت في الطريق سائرة، أو أوقفها لذلك، فإن أوقفها لغيره ضمن)، فإنَّها إن راثت أو بالت في الطريق حالةَ السَّيرِ لا يضمن ، ، أَمَّا إذا أوقفها لتروث أو تبول لا يضمن أيضاً ؛ لأنَّ بعضَ الدَّوابِّ لَا يفعل ذلك إلا بعد الوقوف، وإن أوقفها لغير ذلك يضمن لأنه متعد بالإيقاف. (فإن أصابت بيدها ، أو رجلها حصاةً أو نواة ، أو أثارت غباراً أو حجراً صغيراً ، فَفَقأ عيناً أو أفسد ثوباً لا يضمن، وضمن بالكبير) ؛ لأن الاحتراز عن الأول متعدّرٌ بخلاف الثاني.
لأن في الوجه الأوّل صاحب البداية مباشر للإتلاف؛ لأنّ ثقله وثقل الدابـة تـصـلُ بالمتلف، فكأنهما وطئاه جميعاً ؛ ولهذا يجب على الراكب الكفارة إذا وطأت الدابة برجلها أو بيدها، ويحرم عن الميراث، والمباشر ضامن سواء كان متعدّياً أو لا. كذا في «الكفاية»(۱). والأصل فيه أن المرور في طريق المسلمين مباح مقيد بشرط السلامة لا يتصرف في حقه من وجه، وفي حق غيره من وجه ؛ لكونه مشتركاً بين كل الناس، فقلنا : بالإباحة مقيداً بشرط السلامة ؛ ليعتدل النظر من الجانبين، ثم إنّما يتقيد بشرط السلامة فيما يمكن التحرز عنه، ولا يتقيدها فيما لا يمكن لما فيه من المنع عن التصرف وسدّ بابه وهو مفتوح. والاحتراز عن الوطء وما يضاهيه ممكن ، فإنّه ليس من ضرورات التسيير، فقيدناه بشرط السلامة عنه، والنفحة بالرجل والذنب ليس يمكنه الاحتراز مع السير على الدابة فلم يتقيّد به كذا في «الهداية)) (٢)
[۱]قوله : أو نواة... الخ؛ النواة : حب التمر، بالفارسية: وانه خر ما. [٢]قوله : حجراً صغيراً ؛ يعني الذي يقبلُ أن يرمى به ، فهو أكبر من الحصاة وأصغر من الحجر الكبير الذي لا يحمل ولا يرمى به إلا بالمشقة. كذا في الجلبي يا
Or if a person is injured by what it (the animal) produced as dung or urine on the road while moving, or if he stopped it for that purpose; if he stopped it for something else, he is liable. If it strikes with its hand or foot a pebble or a date pit, or raises dust or a small stone, and puts out an eye or spoils a garment, he is not liable; but he is liable for a large stone. The commentary explains: If it produces dung or urine on the road while moving, he is not liable. But if he stops it to produce dung or urine, he is also not liable because some animals only do that after stopping. If he stops it for something else, he is liable because he is transgressing by stopping. For the second part: If it strikes with its hand or foot a pebble or a date pit, or raises dust or a small stone, and puts out an eye or spoils a garment, he is not liable; he is liable for a large stone because avoidance of the former is impossible unlike the latter. Because in the first case, the rider is a direct agent of destruction because his weight and the weight of the animal reach the victim, as if both trampled him. Therefore, the rider must pay kaffarah if the animal tramples with its hand or foot, and he is deprived of inheritance; a direct agent is liable whether or not he is transgressing. Thus in al-Kifayah. The principle is that passing in the road of Muslims is permissible, conditioned by safety; it does not act solely in his right nor solely in others' right because it is shared among all people. So we say: permissibility conditioned by safety to balance both sides. Then it is conditioned by safety regarding what can be avoided, and not conditioned regarding what cannot be avoided because that would prevent action and close an open door. Avoidance of trampling and similar is possible because it is not a necessity of moving, so we conditioned permissibility with safety from it. But the kick (nakhdah) by the leg or tail cannot be avoided while riding, so it is not conditioned. Thus in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 523

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding the matn of al-Wiqayah on liability for injuries caused by a riding animal on public roads. The ruling distinguishes between avoidable and unavoidable harms. For excretion (urine/feces): no liability whether the animal is moving or stopped specifically for that purpose, because it is unavoidable. But if the rider stops the animal for some other reason and it then excretes, he is liable because stopping unnecessarily created an avoidable risk. For objects kicked up: no liability for small objects (pebbles, date pits, dust, small stones) because these are impossible to avoid while the animal moves. But there is liability for a large stone (that can be thrown or moved with difficulty) because the rider should have avoided it. The commentary then explains the underlying principle: the public road is shared, and its use is permitted on condition of safety. This condition applies only to avoidable harms; if removing the condition would prevent all use of the road, it is not imposed. The rider is a direct agent (mubaashir) for trampling because his weight combines with the animal's; hence he must pay kaffarah and is deprived of inheritance if he kills someone by trampling, even if not negligent. This follows the Hanafi position that the direct agent is liable regardless of intent.

Key Terms
مباشر (mubaashir): A direct agent who causes harm directly, such as the rider whose weight tramples the victim.
سبب (sabab): A cause that indirectly leads to harm, such as driving or leading the animal; not liable for kaffarah or deprivation of inheritance.
شرط السلامة (shart al-salaamah): The condition of safety that restricts the permissibility of using public roads; applies only to avoidable harms.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is a rider liable for damage caused by his animal's excretion on a public road?
A) When the animal is moving.
B) When the animal is stopped for excretion.
C) When the animal is stopped for an unrelated purpose.
D) Always, because the rider is responsible for the animal.

Key Takeaway
The rider is liable for harm from animal excretion only when he stops the animal unnecessarily, making the harm avoidable.

Liability for Objects Kicked by a Riding Animal
Ruling on liability for objects kicked by an animal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between liability for small vs large objects kicked by an animal and explain the Hanafi principle of avoidability.

Core Idea
A rider is not liable for small objects kicked up by his animal but is liable for large objects, because only the latter can be avoided.

This lesson deals with harm caused by objects that an animal kicks with its feet or tail while moving on a public road. The Hanafi school distinguishes based on the size of the object: small objects (pebbles, date pits, dust, small stones) are unavoidable, so no liability; large stones are avoidable, so liability exists. The underlying reasoning is the condition of safety on public roads.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objects and the harm
List the small objects (حصاة, نواة, غبار, حجر صغير) and the large object (حجر كبير). The harm is damage to an eye or garment.
Small objects are those typically found on roads and cannot be easily avoided.
2
Note the ruling for small objects: no liability
The text clearly states 'لا يضمن' for small stones, pits, dust. Understand that this is because the rider cannot prevent the animal from kicking such tiny objects while moving.
The commentary says 'الاحتراز عن الأول متعذر'.
3
Contrast with large objects: liability
For a large stone (كبير), the rider is liable because he should have steered the animal away or stopped to remove it.
Large stone is defined as one that can be thrown or moved with difficulty.
4
Trace the principle: condition of safety applies to avoidable harms
The general principle from the end of the passage: permissibility of using roads is conditioned on safety, and this condition only restricts avoidable actions. Kicking small objects is unavoidable, so no condition; large stones are avoidable, so liability.
This principle also applies to excretion lesson.

Liability in Animal-Related Accidents: Direct Agent vs. Cause and the Collision Dispute
Comparison of liability between rider, driver, and leader; and ruling on two riders colliding

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi ruling for rider (direct agent) vs. driver/leader (cause) regarding financial liability, kaffarah, and inheritance deprivation, and explain the Hanafi position in the dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i over diyah in a fatal collision.

Core Idea
The rider bears kaffarah and inheritance deprivation as a direct agent; the driver/leader only financial liability as a cause.

This passage explores the Hanafi classification of liability in animal-related accidents. It distinguishes between the rider, who is a direct agent (mubaashir), and the driver/leader, who are causes (sabab). It then presents a famous dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i over the diyah when two riders collide and both die, illustrating how the mubaashir/sabab distinction shapes the outcome.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Roles
Recognize the three actors: the rider (raakib), the driver (saa'iq), and the leader (qaa'id). Each has a different relationship to the animal's movement.
2
Understand Liability for Each
The rider is liable for financial compensation, kaffarah, and inheritance deprivation. The driver and leader are financially liable but not for kaffarah or inheritance deprivation.
This difference stems from the mubaashir/sabab distinction.
3
Grasp the Mubaashir/Sabab Distinction
The rider is a direct agent (mubaashir) because the trampling is caused by his weight. The driver/leader are causes (sabab) because they only bring the animal to the place without direct contact.
Kaffarah and inheritance deprivation are exclusive to direct agency.
4
Analyze the Collision Dispute
When two riders collide and both die, Imam al-Shafi'i says each Aqila pays half diyah because each death results from two actions. The Hanafi school says each Aqila pays full diyah because each rider's action is permissible (mubah) in relation to himself and thus not attributed to his own death, but attributed to the other's death.
The key rationale: 'Al-mubah fi haqq nafsihi la yudaaf ilayhi al-halaak.'
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
Under Hanafi law, two full diyah payments are made (each Aqila pays full diyah of the other). Under Shafi'i law, each Aqila pays half diyah, resulting in two half payments.
The practical consequence is a doubling of liability in the Hanafi view.

وضمن السائق والقائد ما ضمنَهُ الرَّاكب، وعليه الكفارة لا عليهما، وضمن عاقلة كل فارس دية الآخر إن إصطدما ومانا
وضمن السائق والقائد ما ضمنَهُ الرَّاكب، وعليه الكفارة لا عليهما) : أي إن كان مكان الراكب سائق أو قائد يضمن كل منهما ما ضمنَهُ الرَّاكب، ويجب على الراكب الكفارة، لا على السائق والقائد والراكب يحرم عن الميراث ، لا القائد والسائق.
وضمن عاقلة كل فارس دية الآخر إن اصطدما وماتا) : هذا عندنا، وعند الشافعي : يضمن كل نصف دية الآخر ؛ لأنَّ هلاكَهُ بفعلَيْن، فعل نفسه، وفعل صاحبه، فيُهْدَر نصفه ويعتبر نصف صاحبه، قلنا : فعل كل منهما مباح ، والمباح في حق نفسه لا يضاف إليه الهلاك، وفي حق غيره يضاف.
(1) وأصغر من الحجر الكبير الذي لا يحمل ولا يرمى به إلا بالمشقة. كذا في الجلبي يا )
اقوله : يضمن كل منهما ... الخ ؛ لأنهما سببان ؛ لمباشرتهما شرط التلف، وهو تقريب الدابة إلى مكان الجناية، فيتقيد بشرط السلامة فيما يمكن الاحتراز عنه كالراكب إلا أنّ على الراكب الكفارة فيما أوطأته الدابة بيدها أو برجلها ؛ ولا كفارة عليهما، ولا الراكب فيما وراء الإيطاء ؛ لأنّ الراكب مباشر فيه ؛ لأنّ التلف بثقله، وثقل الدابة تبع له ؛ لأنّ سير الدابة مضاف إليه، وهي آلة له، وهما سببان ؛ لأنه لا يتصلُ منهما إلى المحل شيء. به در وكذا الراكب في غير الإيطاء، والكفارة حكم المباشرة لا حكم التسبب. وكذا يتعلّق بالإيطاء في حق الراكب حرمان الميراث والوصية دون السائق القائد ؛
لأنه يختص بالمباشرة. كذا في «الهداية»(٢). قاله (۲) قوله : كل فارس ؛ وكذا الماشيين، لكن لما كان موت المصطدمين غالباً في الفارسين خصهما بالذكر، كما ذكر في «الأكملية»، كذا في الجلبي (۳). [٣]قوله : لا يضاف إليه الهلاك ؛ فلا يصلح مستندا للإضافة في حق الضمان :
And the driver and the leader are liable for what the rider is liable for, and kaffarah is required from the rider, not from them. And the Aqila of each rider pays the diyah of the other if they collide and die. [Commentary:] 'And the driver and leader are liable for what the rider is liable for, and kaffarah is required from the rider, not from them': i.e., if in place of a rider there is a driver or leader, each is liable for what the rider is liable for, but kaffarah is obligatory on the rider, not on the driver or leader; and the rider is deprived of inheritance, not the driver or leader. 'And the Aqila of each rider pays the diyah of the other if they collide and die': this is our (Hanafi) position. According to al-Shafi'i, each pays half the diyah of the other because his death is due to two actions: his own action and his companion's action; half is dropped and half is compensated. We say: the action of each is permissible, and what is permissible in his own right is not attributed to causing his death, but in the right of the other it is attributed. [Footnote:] (1) [A note on stone size.] He says: each of them is liable because they are causes; they directly bring about the condition of destruction, i.e., bringing the animal to the place of injury. They are conditioned by safety regarding what can be avoided like the rider, except that kaffarah is on the rider when the animal tramples with its hand or foot; there is no kaffarah on them, nor on the rider for other than trampling. Because the rider is a direct agent in trampling since the destruction is by his weight, and the animal's weight is subordinate to his; the animal's movement is attributed to him and is his tool. They (driver/leader) are causes because nothing from them directly reaches the place. Similarly, the rider for other than trampling; and kaffarah is a ruling of direct agency, not causation. And the rider's deprivation of inheritance and wasiyyah is attached to trampling, not to the driver or leader because it is specific to direct agency. Thus in al-Hidayah. He said: (2) 'Each rider' – and similarly pedestrians, but because death from collision is more common among riders, they are specifically mentioned. Thus in al-Akmaliyya, as in al-Jalabi. [3] He said: 'It is not attributed to him' – so it is not valid as a basis for attribution in the context of liability.
Source pages p. 524

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding the matn on liability for injuries caused by animal and riders. It compares the liability of a rider (direct agent) with a driver or leader (causes). The ruling: driver and leader have the same financial liability as a rider for damages caused by the animal, but they do not have to pay kaffarah (expiation) and are not deprived of inheritance. This is because the rider is a direct agent (mubaashir) – his weight and the animal's weight together cause the trampling – while the driver/leader are merely causes (sabab). The Hanafi position is that kaffarah and deprivation of inheritance attach only to direct agency, not to causation. The passage then addresses a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i regarding two riders who collide and both die. According to the Shafi'i school, each rider's heirs pay half the diyah of the other because each death results from two actions: the victim's own action and the other's action. The Hanafi position is that each rider's Aqila pays the full diyah of the other because the action of each is permissible (mubah) in relation to himself – so his own action is not attributed to his death – but is attributed to the other's death. Therefore, each is fully liable for the other's diyah. The commentary also clarifies that this ruling applies equally to pedestrians, but riders are specifically mentioned because collisions are more common among them.

Key Terms
مباشر (mubaashir): Direct agent: the rider whose weight directly causes trampling; liable for kaffarah and inheritance deprivation.
سبب (sabab): Cause: driver or leader who indirectly leads to injury by moving the animal; financially liable but no kaffarah or inheritance deprivation.
عاقلة (Aqila): The group of male agnatic relatives who pay diyah on behalf of a killer for accidental death.
دية (diyah): Blood money: financial compensation paid to the heirs of a victim of wrongful death or injury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, if two riders collide and both die, how much diyah does each rider's Aqila pay?
A) Half the diyah of the other rider.
B) Full diyah of the other rider.
C) No diyah, because each rider caused his own death.
D) Full diyah for both riders combined.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the distinction between direct agent and cause crucially determines whether kaffarah and inheritance deprivation apply, and in collisions, each rider's Aqila pays full diyah.
